The Red Page
ANZAC.
High-hearted from the seas they came; the
morn
A'as grey with clinging mists that in a veil
Stern fastnesses concealed. Through flay-
ing hail
Of focman fire, in bleeding ranks and torn
They gripped the alien shore, and there was
homt.
A warrior race, to falter not nor fail
In long, dread days; self-pledged to never
quail
Before the direful task, the hope forlorn.
For us the lonely hours, the darkened heart.
In hush of purple nights the sore regret
For silent feet and voices stilled; for them
The hero's sleep to crown the splendid part.
And their bright fame imperishably set —
•Chief jewel in a nation's diadem.
M. 1..
LILLA GORMHUILLF. McKAY.
The Theatre
Ihere is a popular superstition held b
Australasians that we possess theatres —i
fact, it is an article of belief that we hav
quite a lot of theatres. A mere glance a
The Theatre of To-day, by Hiram Iv. Moder
well (Lane, 7s. (id.), however, absolutel
disposes of this childish delusion. We hav
a number of large, draughty, uncomfortable
garishly-decorated barns in which plays ar
presented, and some of these are of quit
recent date, but we haven’t a single moderr
theatre in the Commonwealth. Moderwell’
instructive book is a description and explana
tion of the new forces which have enterec
theatrical production in the last 10 years
for which, by the way, we have chiefly t
thank Russia and Germany. They do
lot of things well in Germany, in addition
to making war, and though their dramatist
have not contributed much to the develop
ment of the drama, in the presentation anc
housing of their plays they stood, and, for al
we know, may stand, supreme. The thorough
ness of Moderwell’s treatment may b
gauged by his chapter-headings. He deal
with the improvements in stage-equipment
the stage-setting, the artistic forces —the us
of color and lighting, stylisation. and modern
scene designing, the intellectual forces behinc
the drama, the literary forces of the variou
nations, the imaginative dramatists, the
,social forces—modern theatre organisatioi
and economics. And the book is fully illus
trated with reproductions of scene designs
and scenic effects, plans of theatres and theii
accessories.
Ten years ago, he says, this book wouh
have been written entirely about dramatic
literature. “At that time we thought of tin
institution of the theatre as being a collec
tion of printed plays together with a few
necessary buildings to present them in” —
conception of the theatre that still subsists
in the minds of our Australian theatrical
managers.
A drama is far more than characters speak
ing words and imitating, or attempting to
imitate, literal facts. It is a series of arehi
tectural designs: why not make use of the
fine art of the architect? It is a series o;
pictures: why not give them the beauty
which painters have, after centuries of study
given their canvases? It is often rhythmic
spectacle: why not apply to it the fine art of
the dance? It is a kaleidoscope of color:
why not make use of the art of color as the
painters have mastered it? Further, the
theatre, which is essentially a performance
for the crowd, is the most democratic of the
arts; why not organise it so that it shall
be the property and the servant of all men,
rather than the “cinematograph of the idle
rich” or the pink tea of the literary select ?
O*
The last 10 years have brought to the
stages of Germany and Russia (and in a
smaller measure to those of America) highly-
trained mechanicians and long-needed
mechanical inventions. These include the
revolving stage, permitting five or more
complete sets to be built at once
and changed in the space of a few
seconds; the sliding stage, permitting 6ne
scene to be built on one-half of the stage
while the other half is being used for the
play ; the rolling stage, by which any num-
ber of scenes can be set up at leisure and
quietly rolled before the proscenium when
their turn comes ; the Fortuny lighting sys-
tem. by which our present fiat and unreal
lighting is replaced with soft, reflected glows
of real beauty; the new types of theatre
architecture.
Moderwell’s picture of the hopelessly-unreal
“stage-picture” of 10 years ago Would afford
the Australian reader no sense of contrast, as
it would merely reproduce the crude effects
still produced on our stage; but his picture
of theatre architecture 10 years ago will be
appreciated in this land that is a decade
behind the world.
Most theatres of the old style are built in
utter contempt of the man with a small
income. One feels that the architect thought
he w r as doing us a favor to let us in at all.
Many seats in the ordinary “horseshoe”
theatre make the stage partly or wholly
invisible. Very frequently the back of the
balconies is so ill-ventilated that the evening
is torture. The acoustics of such caves are
often wretched.
In the theatres designed by Littmann. of
Munich, he says: “There shall be no bad
seats; there shall not even be any w'orse
seats.” His playhouse has all its rows prac-
ticaily parallel with the proscenium and its
floor rising at such an angle that every spec-
tator can look clean over the head of his
neighbor in front. The revolving stage has
not reached Australia. It is merely a circu-
lar portion of the whole stage, some 40 feet
in diameter, showing about a quarter of its
circumference before the proscenium. It
practically does away with the interval, to
which the Australian audience, ignorant of
stage-design over-seas, has grown hardened,
and even has learnt to like. But it is only
certain theatres that require such an elabo-
rate stage. The interval can be just as easily
cut out by other, and cheaper, means,
notably by the German waggon-stage, appar-
ently adopted in the recent Sydney produc-
tion of “On Trial,” where almost
instantaneous changes of scene are
necessary—-though, with characteristic cas-
ualness. the management still retain
the unnecessary intervals between the
arbitrarily-divided acts. The waggon-stage
consists of a number of platforms,
fitted with noiseless rubber wheels, on which
the various scenes are set up before the per-
formance, and in turn rolled into place. The
sliding stage avoids the necessity of placing
properties by hand, and is invaluable where
elaborate sets are required, since the whole
scene can be set ready to be slid into place;
but it requires more space than is usually at
the disposal of our type of theatre. Of
course, all these methods relieve the stage-
hand of much of his work. With a sunken
stage one theatre in Germany was able to
dispense permanently with one-third of the
stage-hands.
J*
Another important invention, lacking, of
course, in Australian theatres, is the dojne
of solid plaster built over the stage which
does afvay with the “sky-drops” or strips of
white canvas which so pitifully pretend to
represent the sky. Upon this white dome
the lights are played, giving a striking illu-
sion of depth and luminous atmosphere.
This neutral background permits of every
subtle effect of lighting and color being pro-
duced —even moving clouds—and simplifies
stage-setting, since there is no longer any
need for masking the wings and top with
canvas in order to cut off the scene from the
stage-wings beyond. But its chief merit is
that it solves the problem of lighting.
Ordinary sunlight is not a direct light, but
an infinitely reflected light. The mellow-
ness and internal luminosity of ordinary sun-
light is wholly due to this infinite reflection.
It is the lack of this that makes the lighting
of the Australian stage, with its blazing
direct artificial glare, so unreal. The For-
tuny system of lighting is the chief techni-
cal revolution in the contemporary theatre.
Instead of lighting the stage with incan-
descent bulbs of crude color, the Fortuny
method throws its brilliant illuminations
(from arc lights) away from the stage
against bands of colored silk which reflect
the light in any color or tint desired, either
on the whole stage or upon any desired
part. Sometimes the light is thrown mainly
against the dome, which reflects it a second
time. The whole apparatus is under the
control of one man, who, like most of the
mechanics of the modern art theatre, must
himself be an artist. . . .The whole system is
in reality economical, since one man can
operate one lamp, and a few lamps will
accomplish what formerly required hundreds
of bulbs and a complex system of wiring.
And what effects can be got by this system,
with an artist directing it, can only be re-
motely guessed from the vivid descriptions
of actual lighting schemes given us by the
author. Australian theatre-goers can have
no conception of the wonders of the new
realms into which the modern methods of
lighting would lead us. And the marvels of
color, stage color, in the hands of a
Leon Bakst, the Russian scene and costume
designer, are equally beyond our conception.
“Stage color now has two factors—two
dimensions, almost —pigment and light.”
Working on the principle that on the stage
a color painted on canvas will not become
visible until a light is thrown upon it which
contains at least some element of that color,
Josef Urban has developed a complex sys-
tem of coloring which he calls “pointillage.”
He spots or “points” his canvas with all
the colors he intends to bring out on it in
the course of the scene. At a distance the
spaces between the points are not visible as
such, so when he throws a red light on the
scene all the red spots jump out of the
canvas and blend together to make a red
surface. Similarly if he now throws a green
light, the red spots retire into darkness, and
the green takes possession of the whole sur-
face. The result to the observer is nothing
short of magical If the light is a mix-
ture of green and red then both the green
and red spots become visible, according to
the proportions of green and red in the
light. . . .A color once drawn out by a simi-
larly colored light is more brilliant than
under white.
The producer of a decade ago had but one
principle: Let there be light. If he needed
a second principle it was this: In case of
emotion, let there be green light. “One
easily recalls the stage table which shone
brighter on its Under side than on top. On
the old stage no one ever had a shadow. Or
if there was a shadow, it was east, life
size, on the distant landscape.” The tre-
mendous discovery of the modern producer
was that light must come from somewhere,
and. therefore, it must cast shadows. Foot-
lights, kept low. still have their uses, but
not to cast shadows.
J*
The “modern” theatres that dicorate our
main thoroughfares are anachronisms. They
are mere remnants of the theatre of royalty
and society. The old theatres were built to
exhibit the audience, not to exhibit the
play, to enable society to view itself or par-
ade itself. It is curious to notice how it
needed a new art —the cinematographic art
—before theatres were built with the deli-
berate intention of giving every patron a
clear view of the screen. The newer Sydney
movie theatres actually attain that aim:
the other theatres, with their boxes —pro-
viding the worst possible view of the stage,
obstinately perpetuate a bad tradition. The
conventional decoration of our theatres be-
longs to the same unhappy period: they
glare with the lavish gilt of the eighteenth
century. A theatre should be a place where
one goes to see a play in comfort, without
being hit by glaring colors. Moderwell pro-
vides plans and elevations of the modern
theatre that would prove illuminating to
our Australian managers.
The theatre as a social organism is a sub-
ject that makes to the Australian a special
appeal. Our theatre is not in the least
a social organism: it does not give to its
audience the commodity it desires, and it
is not owned by its audience. The idea
that an audience should own its theatre
may seem absurd to Australians; but there
is—or was, before the war —in existence in
Berlin a completely successful theatre of
this kind. The New Free Folk Stage is
owned by its audience of over 50,000. It
gives nightly and matinee performances to
its members, and tickets for each perform 3 ®
ance cost one shilling each. In addition,
the theatre has made arrangements with 12
of the best theatres in Berlin for special
evening or Sunday-afternoon performances,
at which it buys out the whole or part of
the house, and supplies the seats to its mem-
bers at the usual rate, or near it. This is
not charity—the commercial theatres are
only too glad to accept the offered income.
The theatre itself is not a charity. It is a
business organisation, and it pays its way.
The reason is that it supplies its market in-
stead of forcing it, answers its demand in-
stead of trying to create it. The middle-
man, organising commercial entertainment,
constantly endeavoring to raise the price,
low er the cost and pocket the difference,
has been eliminated. The audience owns
the theatre; and by means of its elected
officers it demands the plays it wants; but
the director is necessarily an autocrat in
his artistic activities. And it exercises a
real and steadily growing influence over the
choice of plays in the commercial theatres.
Moderwell has written a text-book from
which our theatrical managers might con-
ceivably derive some profit—if they had time
to spare from their paramount public
activities. '
Germany in Defeat?
j Gfiarles de Souza wrote Germany in
Defeat to prove that Germany, having
tailed in her strategic plan by being thrown
back from the Marne, was therefore
strategically in the position of a defeated
.Power. Unfortunately for us and for the
author, Germany has subsequently refused
to recognise that she is defeated; hence the
author now has to explain. iD Germany in
Defeat Second Phase (Kegan Paul, per
Dymocks Library), that she has been again
finally defeated at the first battle of Ypres.
llis book was apparently published before
the big battle of Verdun; and that event
will- certainly need a third volume from
Charles de Souza to explain that, once
again, Germany is done for. Perhaps by
then the author will have got tired of telling
Germany that she is down and out when the
liun is too busy smiting his opponents to
hear the referee’s count.
His fiist volume, however, was a valuable
contribution to the strategical history of the
war, since the author was the first to stress
the paramount importance of Joffre’s
strategy in retreating to the Marne, refusing
to be beguiled into Belgium, and correctly
gauging the Hun’s intention to break
through in force at Verdun. And there it
was that Joffre smote the Hun his first
death-blow. This volume shows how Joffre
got in his second death-blow—but the Hun
seems to take some time in dying. The
facts seem .to be that Joffre undoubtedly
smashed the Hun’s plans at the Marne; but
that individual thereupon evolved a new
scheme almost as good—which new scheme
the author shows in the second volume was
finally settled at Ypres.
The author disposes of the belief that
Germany was able to transfer armv corps
from one frontier to the other. Germany
had not even contemplated any such con-
tingency as a war being waged with equal
forces on two frontiers at once. In con-
tradiction to the assertion that the positions
on the Aisne at which the Huns stopped
had been prepared by them in the event of
a retreat, the author points out that in the
hurry of their retreat they unwittingly
abandoned several important positions which
afterwards they did their utmost to recap-
ture, such as the towns of Soissons and
Reims.
“The race to the sea,” the phrase that
picturesquely expressed the movements sub-
sequent to the battle of the Aisne, had, the
author assorts, no such
meant to rescue the Belgkn Joff *-e

be end of the great German offensive in
the Western theatre of war. L ate r it t
rue, at various periods in the siege’opera-
tions which ensued, the Germans made other
and vigorous efforts against the Allied line
efforts doomed to fail. But the titanic en-

the chain woven by the defenders around
the blustering aggressors. From that
moment the invaders were held tight as in
a vise. They could neither retrekt nor
advance, and, necessarily, they must suc-
cumb on the spot.... The eventual fall or
dissolution of the Central Empires became
as certain, as inevitable, as the mathemati-
cal descent of the solar disc over the horizon
at sunset. Such w-as the portentous mean-
ing of the battle of Ypres—a battle that
was not decisive in itself.”

A Journey from Gallipoli to Good Health.
CLEMENTS TONIC CURED.
(Returned Soldiers’ Series, So. t.)
CORPORAL E. J. ROBSOX, D.C.M., No. 1088. D. Company, 4th Battalion,
who comes from Tamworth, says:
CLEMENTS TONIC, LTD.,
“Since taking your Clements Tonic I have found considerable
improvement in my health generally, hut more especially to my nerves,
which were quite undermined in consequence of the strenuous times in
the trenches, and being subsequently wounded. I was seven weeks in
the trenches before I was disabled, but I did not feel the strain until
getting away from the din and roar of the firing line. Then I collapsed,
and my recovery has been very slow until I got on to Clements Tonic.
A Second Military Letter to
Clements Tonic.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Idyll.
If things remained an idyll!
An idyll is a weaving delicate
Of honey-horn and tendril, and the blue,
The sempiternal blue of summer skies.
If there are grey grubs curled inside the
leaf
And the moist cubes of caterpillar shrouds
And wliite-ants working in the dark, behind
The filigree of biscnit-colored bark,
They show not in the idyll. Gentle winds
Dance some green-skirted tango. in the trees,
And twigs together do a gaby glide,
And mouths kiss light moth-kisses. Easy
gifts!
As cool and soft as down on thistle-tops.
There is no red wound on the passing sun,
No storm cloud pennon blots the lilac hill;
The buttercups turn tenderly to sleep,
The river’s tinkle is a vesper bell.
If things remained an idyll!
Breaks through the bark the blind, instinct-
ive mite,
Crash to the earth falls the deluding bough.
Smears o’er the leaf’s rayed veining, horrible,
Obscene and slow, the progress of the grub,
And crystal-hard grow those dear hazel eyes
That were so fond and mystical with
dreams;
The leaves are staggering in a peevish wind
Angry and hot, and thirsty with the dust,
And dry and sated are the-lips that clung.
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
The Scarf.
Yellow gold is the color note
For ivory chin and ivory throat!
’Tis a golden scarf for a golden girl!
Oh, every heart shrines a girl of gold !
And I twine it under a dusky curl
And cover a kiss with its silken fold.
Oh, yellow gold may be lost or won,
But the girl of gold is the girl to win ;
And a golden scarf brings a gleam of sun
To the curve o’ the throat an’ the point o’
the chin!
The color note is the yellow of gold
For an ivory throat which mirth doth hold!
There is more than sound in your lilting
laugh,
O throat which thrills to my finger tips !
And I twine my heart in the golden scarf
With a smile that is more than a twist o’
the lips.
Oh the hue of a laugh is a golden hue;
An you’ve taken my kiss you must give a
kiss
For the bright little scarf I have brought
for you,
An’ I’ll tuck it close under your chin —•
like this!
Oh the hiss of lips and love's own laugh
Make fair exchange for a silken scarf!
MAORILAN D.
BOYCE BOWDEN.
The Telephone.
Out of the still, expectant dark
The bell rings
Shrill and insistent;
With hopes caught up, and heart afire
I answer it.
Yes, I hear. The cold room catches fire,
And all the living current of my voice
Speeds on the vibrant wire.
So far it goes, and yet not far ;
Just to him,
The giver of warmth.
I have forgotten all those lonely moments
When I waited —
The sick doubts of all those chilly moments,
Now that the eager calling bell has loosed
Its message in the dark.
TASMANIA.
NANCY WEIR.
The Poet’s Tragedy.
A poet with a razorable chin
Came to a lady (who was. very sweet)
And said: “Your eyes agleam are deep as
sin ;
My life of dream lies anguished at your
feet!”
To whom the dame: “Strange that love’s
flame burns purest
In persons who disdain the manicurist.
It leaves you, sure, this ardent, ceaseless
raving.
No time for sin,, since you’ve no time for
shaving.”
The poet crooned: “Your bosom’s chaste
cool snow
Sqft woos, as doves coo soft against the
moon.
Your slender, exquisite feet that come and go
Are pearls ablossom in your envious shoon !”
To whom quoth she: “Queer that in linen
shady
The poet evermore must seek his lady.
And yet I'd go with you in this glad
weather....
We’d risk it, would your shoes but hold
together.”
The poet cried : “With loyal heart and time
I bring my joyous Muse to praise you, dear.
The stars are gold, and all the world is you ;
The mountains deeplier breathe to feel you
near !”
“True,” she agreed, “you glorify the passion
Divinely, when you hymn it in such fashion.
Love calls, but, oh! his world a world of
strife is^
And, ah, the little patient dear your wife is !”
His cup that brimmed thus muddied to the
dregs,
He scurried home , his tail between his legs.
N.S.W.
JOSEPHUS Q. HARRIS.

Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright. by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable ), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.

THURSDAY. JUXE 1. 1916.
THE BLISTER OX THE AUSTRA-
LIAN’S HEEL.
The bill is piling up all the time, and in the
House of Reps, the other day Treasurer
Higgs remarked that one of the items in
the document by June next year would be
a little matter of £109,000,000. He was sure
of that much. About various other millions
for pay and army maintenance he was a
hit doubtful —as to the exact total,
at any rate. But as someone would have
to hand out £5.550.000 for interest and sink-
ing fund on what he was certain of. Higgs
reckoned that the debt generally is worth
a little consideration. It certainly is
Before the House of Reps, shut down and
bolted into seclusion last week it gave sanc-
tion to the borrowing of £75.000.000. Add
this to the prior Commonwealth debt, and
ornament the total with the plaster that
the States have put on Australia, and it
becomes clear that at the end of the financial
year the mortgages held by Cohen over this
bright young nation will amount to the dis-
reputable sum of at least £530,000.000 —
possibly £55u.000.000, because there are
various chickens cruising round which have
not yet arrived on the roost. Cohen has
been lately collecting nearly fl3.otXi.ooo a
year for the accommodation. Xext year he
will be holding out his hand for well over
£20.000.000. And he will have to be paid.
The job of doing this may. in the main,
according to well-established practice, be
fobbed off upon the Australian kid. But
not wholly. The business of finding these
additional millions has in great part to be
faced now. The politicians, aware of this,
are trusting to Inek and muttering "God
help ns!" am mg themselves. And the average
fool-citizen, with a mouthful of loose talk
about heaps of money somewhere in the
country, reckons that the bill can be paid
with hot air as usual and that everything is
all right.
* * * *
There are two ways, and only two. by
whieh the tremendously increased charges
can legitimately be met. One is by_ cutting
down public expenditure, thus setting free
part of the present revenue to meet interest
and sinking fund. The other is by so greatly
extending the production of exportable com-
modities and of goods now imported that the
credit margin will be sufficientlv great to
permit of an additional seven millions being
annually raised without inflicting hardship
on the general body of the people. A pos
sible third way is to let tliinss slide, and
have a shot at scraping up the seven mil-
lions from existing sources of income and
such others as may be devised. By one of
these methods or a combination of the first
two Australia has to get the money. The
only alternative is to borrow it. thus keeping
up the disgraceful old drunk. It might be
possible to do this, but it doesn’t look like
it. It would mean bankruptcy, anyhow.
♦ * * *
In any case, whether the multitudinous
Treasurers and Parliaments of Australia
get sane under the pressure of necessity or
grow still more mad. it is as certain as the
sunset that unless production is greatly in-
creased in Australia there must be a revo-
lution in public and private life. The pour-
ing in of the products of other countries
represents a leakage of financial strength
irreconcilable with sanity. We are five
million people who have piled up a debt of
over five hundred million pounds, with
more in sight: and for us, thus placed, to
be importing foreign goods in excess of our
exports, while cheerfully pushing up by
millions the ordinarv cost of government, is
simple madness. And the tragedy of it is
that every Australian Parliament knows it
to be so, yet goes blindly on. plunging deeper
and deeper into debt, levying larger and
larger toll upon the earnings of the people,
and discounting by these exactions the
power of the community to meet the in-
creasing burdens placed upon the public
shoulders. The demoralisation of most of
these Parliaments is absolute. They have
become mere mendicants, whose existence is
a peril to the nation ; they are afraid to do
anything but borrow, boom and spend. The
Federal Parliament, hearing even the
Treasurer express alarm at the development
of the importing disease, and knowing that
the interest bill is leaping un to a figure
that would have looked incredible a year
or two ago, did —what? Xothing. except
pass loans and indulge in crude vote-catch-
ing debates on a couple of jerry-built bills.
And from the State Premiers picnicking at
Adelaide there is nothing to be heard except
meaningless words and agitated whispers
mostly about how they can get hold of more
millions to drug the constituencies with.
For a long time the nolitieian with the loud
mouth deluded the mob that his borrowing
was in some occult way strengthening the
nation. Well, how about it now. in the light
of the degrading confession that, with a
quarter of a million Australian men under
arms, the rest will be without a job unless
they borrow some more? Judging by the
cowardice of Parliaments and the banal talk
that is going on about employment and the
repatriation of our soldiers, it looks as if
there are ideas abroad that war may be more
desirable than peace!
♦ * * *
Australia’s borrowings in the past have
been a social disgrace—the negation of
every national ideal. They have been per-
petrated. however, and it is no use weeping
aDout them. But the result of them has to be
grappled with, and it is no use pretending
that the mountain of debt is something that
will evaporate under the influence of talk.
It is real and very solid; and it can be made
less fearsome only by getting to work, and by
enterprise and toil and a wiser distribution
of the burden of taxation. Borrowing for
war purposes has been unavoidable in the
circumstances. But while this war-borrow-
ing is in progress continued borrowing by
the States is criminal and any waste of
cash anywhere a monstrous wrong. Yet
State borrowing still goes on. The Federal
Parliament is a party to it. too. through a
dishonest. Fiji-uncle arrangement f«>r trans-
ferring money raised in London for "war”
purposes. And the Federal Parliament, too.
has sanctioned the expenditure of nearly
three millions more during the current year
than in the preceding 12 months —this not
for war but for ordinary departmental
purposes. The demented Seven Parliament
system is still in full blast; bloated civil
services are in existence in every State.
For any sign of economy in administration
or prudence in spending the wretched tax-
payer, harassed by the cost of living and
the distant yells of Hughes, looks as vainly
as the Kaiser for Uncle Sam's final Xote.
The frequent assurance that our debt is "of
small consequence and can be easily pro-
vided for” is a lie. It is of tremendous
consequence, and if the obligation to meet
it with constructive legislation, with strict
economy in expenditure and determination
to ent away the idle wheels cumbering the
machinery of government, is evaded much
longer, the consequences are going to be
swift and disastrous.
SIX O’CLOCK AND LIQUOR REFORM.
The useless wrangle over the Liquor Refer-
endum in X. S. Wales, which on alleged
patriotic grounds is diverting attention from
decent patriotic endeavor, will find it hard
to make excuses for itself before those who
in the cold future count up the cost of onr
war hysteria. Xo referendum at all was
needed. There is not the smallest reason
to suppose that liquor has in any
way retarded recruiting: and if it
could be shown that it had retarded
training, the fault must be traced to
the military authorities, who refused to
have a wet canteen which would have placed
the direct control of Billjim's beer in
their own hands. Deliberately, and with
full knowledge of the position, theso authori-
ties decided that the men in training must
go beyond the camp to look for the refresh-
ment they had been accustomed to. and
when trouble arose they placed the blame
on the hotel bars instead of shouldering it
themselves. Getting the hint from some
lofty military persons who had side-stepped
the real issue, the no-liqnor enthusiasts of
the Ma State gathered all their umbrellas
and rushed to battle, declaring that the
salvation of the army and the Empire de-
pended upon the hour at which the beer-
taps were turned off. After some delay
and a good deal of backing and filling, the
Holman Government agreed to take a
referendum on the question, although
everybody knew that the military authori-
ties had the power to close the hotels at
any hour they pleased. So the attention of
the State has been once more concentrated
on the noise and fury of a meaningless
battle, a lot of money is being wasted, and
there is not even a suspicion that any solid
good can come out of it.
* * * *
The proposal to close hotel bars at six
o’clock is not only likely to be futile as a
reform, but it is illogical and absurd. The
argument that the business of providing
refreshment and. in the old phrase, "enter-
tainment” for the public should be limited
to the hours when the public is not free to
be refreshed and entertained is of the kind
that might find a place in comic opera, but
can hardly be taken as a grave and serious
contention. Why not compel all other
forms of entertainment to keep within busi-
ness hours? Why should milk-shake and
ice-cream and vaudeville and Shakspearian
tragedy and picture-show and tea-meeting
not come under the same rule? They are
all similar in their nature and intent —all
intended to tickle palate, eye or ear. If
liquor is to be used at all—and the people
of the State have again and again sanc-
tioned its use —it must be clear to any sane
person that after business and working
hours is the time when its flow is most in
order. Xo-liquor zealots have utterly failed
to show that an ordinary consumption of
good liquor under decent conditions is in-
jurious to the community as a whole. They
have not even succeeded in proving that an
extraordinary consumption of middling
liquor under those inferior conditions to
which they have been almost criminally in-
different did much in the past to lower
national standards. But there is evidence,
and plenty of it. that undue restrictions in
the matter of liquor sales, when unwisely
pressed, have resulted in evils far greater
than those which the persons applying the
restrictions set out to remedy.
* * * *
But the claim of those who clamor for
six o’clock is that they are looking after the
interests of the soldier; and in this con-
nection they are more wildly incoherent
than usual. Their argument appears to be
that because a man makes the fine sacrifice
of enlisting for the service of his country
and is prepared to face toil and death to
keep up Australia’s repute in the eyes of
the nations, he should at once be treated
as a convict and deprived of those ordinary
rights which he enjoyed when he wasn't
worrying himself about anything except get-
ting the best out of the job of living. The
insanity goes further than this, and would
deny the ordinary comforts to which they
were accustomed in time of peace to those
returned soldiers whom in one breath they
acclaim as strong heroes, and in the next,
by inference, describe as lawless ruffians or
poor weaklings who must be kept in moral
fetters. This picture of a pair of infuri-
ated coat-tails whirling to the tune of six
o’clock and attempting to prescribe his
habits for the big, strong, clear-eyed Aus-
tralian is one to rouse the laughter of a
continent.
* * * *
Tet Australia has a liquor problem : and
it is for this very reason that The Bulle-
tin is sorry to see the wasted effort and
the "reform" which is no reform at all. The
real problem has no special reference to our
military arrangements, save insofar as
the establishment of a wet canteen would be
a benefit both to Private Billjim and the
people in charge of his training. The one
visible remedy for the great real evil lies
in the public ownership of the whole trade
in alcohol. Let ns take the matter in our
own hands, cut out our bad liquor, our
frowsy drinking dens, and onr bad drinking
conditions, let in light and air on the entire
business, turn the profits into the public
purse and get rid of all private interest in
the manufacture and sale of beer, spirits
and wine. Then we shall have eliminated
all those grosser evils which are touched
neither by six o’clock closing nor by Local
Option.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Habit of Downing Tools.
There are times when the Law is more of
a rogue than an ass. Last week it penal-
ised a Melbourne baker who gave his em-
ployer ten minutes' notice of his intention
to go on strike and by hurriedly dropping
the dough caused the boss some small
monetary loss. The employee had some
grievances about wages and his employer's
conduct in the bakehouse; and. in any case,
having knocked off, he had the grace not to
insist on being paid for the time he was
going to be idle. Tet on the very day the
Law rubbed it into this person 111 other
employees ceased work in Melbourne and
left their employers in the lurch, without
the semblance of an excuse, bnt solelv lie-
cause they were too tired to toil. Not only
so. but they impudently demanded and will
accept their full working salaries. That
these salaries are considered adequate is
proved by the fact that they fixed them
themselves: and they not only "signed on"
for that remuneration, bnt effusively pro-
mised. when engaged, to do certain specified
work immediately—notably, to build a
Tariff Act and an Initiative and Referen-
dum Act. That is nearly two years ago.
and two years is a long "immediately." If
the baker's case is a precedent every and
any employer of these ill employees should
have a good claim for damages on account
of loss occasioned through their refusal to do
their work, more especially through their
knocking off just when there is a loud call
for the job to be finished in a hurry. That
the Law would probably disallow the claim
is luck for the 111; if it were granted they
would certainly be ruined, for the loss caused
to their employers by their traitorous neglect
is simply incalculable.
The Great Danger of Xot Being Mad.
Thebe are two recognised methods of treat-
ing appendicitis. One is to dig it out aud
trust that the tools were clean. The other
is to leave it alone and trust the under-
taker to do the rest decently. According t
the available evidence it looks as if a | v
cates of the latter system were chosen as
delegates to the recent Chambers of Com
meree Conference at Brisbane. Anyhow"
when someone proposed a resolution to sot
at the importing disease by digging some of
it up, the Conference jumped on him with
great emphasis. The motion was:
That the Conference, having in view the import
ance and necessity for the developing and fosterir, ■
of industries, and providing employ inert f or work J*
is of opinion that the Commonwealth and StatP
Governments should not import for their respectiy
uses anvthing that ear. and ia being manufactured
in the Commonwealth. lureo
It was a mild proposition, Heaven knows
expressive of nothing more than a desire to
sec the fair thing done; yet it was thrown
heavily out —because it was “too drastic”'
These people probably imagine they are
gf>od Australian patriots. They certainly
think they are capable of expressing reason-
ably sane ideas on national finance
They passed at this very meeting several
nebulous resolutions about fostering new
industries and encouraging production and
all the rest of it. But this motion, which
was patriotic and practical and aimed at
the destruction of a lunatic system of finance,
was found objectionable. Because it was
really “drastic”? No! Simply because it
was definite. There can be no possible
defence of an Australian Government
taking work away from Australian work-
men and giving the cash to foreigners. Such
conduct is merely a bad form of maladmin-
istration. Xot only does it directly rob a
particular Australian industry but it pre-
judicially affects every other industry by
lowering the common power of purchase.
Further, if Governments systematically buy
lceally-made goods the general body of the
population is liable to contract the same
habit —partly because the bigger the out-
put the cheaper the supply in most cases—
and this cuts the importer’s income off at
the meter. Doubtless that is the drastic
proceeding the Conference was thinking
about. But the fact that an indent agent
may miss 21 per cent, on a big deal in
machinery is no reason why Australia
shonld drop 071 per cent, into an outsider's
bank account instead of keeping the whole
100 per cent, on the premises. Apparently
the people who were a little time ago say-
ine that "The time is not ripe” are now
going to start on “Don't be too drastic.”
4.
The Results of Earnest Bishing.
After wrestling with the spirit, the confer-
ence of Australian Anglican Archbishops
and bishops the other day issued a series
of notes from which it transpired that gait-
erdom had decided —
(1) To postpone an all-round “call” to the com-
munity to arise and awake and repent and amend its
life.
(2) To remind the clergy that they are exempt
from military service and ought to stay at home,
unless they go away as chaplains.
If the reasons given for these decisions
satisfy the righteous they will also make
the unholy scoff. It turns out. for instance,
that as “the labor and self-sacrifice of those
who stay at home is as essential to victory
as the courage and endurance of the soldiers
on the field" —a fine, large slab of assertion,
which is only true in a limited sense —
the real patriotic job before the rector and
curate is "to labor with untiring zeal to
foster such spirit as will support and up-
hold those who are at the Front.” Boiled
down to simple terms the meaning of this
is that the essential requirement of the
moment is a series of "Buck up!” sermons
adaressed to non-combatants. The Bishop
of Cheltenham, who guards his flock some-
where in England, remarked the other day
that, after prolonged reflection, he was com-
pelled "to say with all reverence that God
is sitting on the fence in this struggle."
This paper side-steps the theological issue
involved, but suspects that when Australian
gaiterdom was framing these resolutions it
reckoned the top rail of the slip panels was
the best place, too. Meanwhile, pending
the regeneration of the elect, which is also
affirmed to be necessary. The Bulletin’
takes off its hat to the memory of such men
as Hutton Sams, of Bananaland. and Pe
la Touche, of Sydney. They didn't sit on
any fences or uphold Billjim at the Front
by prayerfully supporting someone at the
rear. They died like men beside men. with
bayonets in their hands and a love for
human liberty in their souls.
A Relic of Barbarism.
The unfortunate Y.D. subject has a hard
row to hoe on active service. His misfor-
tune is the result of a passion which is as
insistent as any; yet. while the detected
drunk (say) is let down lightly, the V.D. is
treated very like -a criminal. He is sent
to an establishment which is half-?aol
half-hospital, or he is dealt with behind a
barb-wire enclosure, which is guarded by
soldiers with fixed bayonets. He gets no
pay so long as he is ill. Any separation
allowance which he may have made con-
tinues to be paid: but the money is deducted
from the pay accruing to him when he re-
joins his unit, as an outcome of which n
draws nothing, or next to nothing, for
after his discharge from hospital—a
hardship in a hishly-paid army such as tna
of Australia. Rut the cruellest and
needless stab of all is the regulation enjoin-
ing that his name and shame shall be rea
out in General Orders before a full P®
of his regiment. The effect is practoca .
to make him a pariah. So seriously do jun -
regard this matter that it is a crimm:
offence to impute certain forms of venere n
disease to a citiien; and there seems
Yalid reason why a soldier should lack
same protection. One reform and one on .
has been instituted in connection with .
cal eases since the beginning of the war. lo .
are no longer returned to the Commonwea _
Instead, they are sent back to the trenc


as soon as they have been patched up suffi-
ciently to be of use there. This, of course,
is no concession to the wretched V.D. It
arose from the fact that the States objected
to a stream of tainted humanity being
directed at their already polluted cities,
while the military authorities realised
that a convalescent V.D. can hold a rifle as
straight, for a time at least, as any other
man. The basic mistake about our attitude
towards this class of sufferer is the spirit
of revenge which inspires it. Little or no
effort is made to check the evil; the object
which is advanced on with misguided
enthusiasm and infernal ingenuity is the
punishment of the invalid. The British
navy—or, at any rate, a portion of it—de-
vised a scheme many years ago which was
put into operation in some of the more
notoriously-diseased ports—notably Sydney
and Auckland (M.L.) —and which had the
effect of reducing venereal troubles amongst
the sailors by from 40 to 50 per cent. There
is no need to specify the device ; it is enough
to say that it is one which can be employed
efficaciously on any body of men subject to
discipline. Where, as in the case of our
Expeditionaries, prevention of the affliction
is comparatively easy, given an earnest and
watchful medical officer, it is surely an
insane arrangement to go on trying to eradi-
cate original sin according to a recipe of the
Dark Ages. The commonest justification
of the appalling treatment meted out to the
V.D. subject, and one which is put forward
with particular frequency and smugness by
civilians, is that “he deserves it all and
more.” This, coming from members of a
community which is afraid of compulsory
notification of venereal disease, for the excel-
lent reason that close on half the total popu-
lation are suffering or have suffered from a
“secret complaint,” is a unique and likewise
very loathsome form of Pharisaism.
The Y.M.C.A. in the War.
A striking feature of the war, so far as
Australian troops are concerned, is the grow-
ing importance of the part which the
Y.M.C.A. plays in it. Two years ago the
average Billjim thought of the Y.M.C.A.
as a genteel anaemic blood relation of the
red-corpuscled Salv’army, and he had little
or no fellow feeling for it. To-day he thinks
of it as a practical friend in need, and a
real good sort. It started, apparently, by
providine refreshment and library tents in
the various camps for the convenience firstly
of the many members who had enlisted.
The young Christians in khaki helped to
popularise the Y.M.C.A. kiosks, and so the
organisation went on. It followed the
forces to Egypt, and proved in a
little while to be a most valuable restraining
influence on a large majority of the boys.
From the outset the aim of the organisation
has been to keep the reasonable Billjim out
of serious mischief; and medical men back
from the Front testify to the magnificent
work done after the first disastrous experi-
ences of the greenhorns in Cairo. The lied
Cross had a terrible number of syphilis cases
to deal with, but there is universal testi-
mony that the tally would have, been greater
had there been no Y.M.C.A. influences on
the side of health. As the war goes on, trie
Australian fighting forces are scattered over
a wider field, and the Association is looking
for the means to befriend them and give
them healthy recreation wherever they are.
The present need, according to the hard-
headed business men who manage the con-
cern, is for 150 new ‘‘huts,” a necessary
equipment of secretaries and all the
stationery and sundries which count as the
comforts of home for soldiers on active ser-
vice. The national secretary, «T. H. Lang.
is a very live and able man of business, and
what he and all the secs, in all the States
are looking for is the good big cheque from
the good big capitalist and the smaller
cheque from the smaller capitalist. On the
evidence of dozens of old Bulletin men at
the Front this paper has no hesitation in
saying that it believes very excellent use
will be made of every pound.
Democracy—or What?
Dear Bulletin, —It is all very well for
* Boomerang’’ ( B. 25/5/’l6) to say that the
Initiative and Referendum are more “live”
as issues than ever they were, but where
is the evidence of this? What he probably
means is that among many earnest demo-
crats there is something more than a sus-
picion that we have all our lives been work-
ing with a blunt tool—that Parliamentary
government under a wide franchise is not
materially different from the animal sus-
tained by the other sort of franchise. It
lost some of its bad habits with the change
in diet, but it contracted new ones, and it is
just a matter for speculation whether the
fresh hag of tricks is any less bad than the
old one. Anyhow, I am not ashamed to
confess that I have not made up my mind
whether an animal which bit me is any
more desirable when it abandons that prac-
tice in order to kick me. Of course what
“Boomerang” wants is to tack the Initia-
tive and Recall on to.the brute as a sort of
combined hobble and muzzle ; but is that
going to prevent the brumby rolling on us
or enticing us on to its back merely to buck
us off into the hospital for repairs? Per-
sonally I don’t know, but I have my sus-
picions. Also I have heard of the cry “Give
us Barabbas,” and of the Second Empire,
and have a deep distrust of the results that
may follow attempts to secure democratic
government with an instrument of which
handle and blade were fashioned for the pro-
tection of an oligarchy. Incidentally you
may have observed that Fat is contemplat-
ing the referendum with much indulgence.
Of course we have not got democratic gov-
ernment. But we have Party Government;
and if “Boomerang” can show that the
Initiative and Recall will not result in in-
tensifying Party Government he ought to
tackle the job. Everyone has heard a lot
about what the I. and R. will do—or what
various persons suppose it will do. Now,
is it not a historical fact that popular specu-
lation on what was going to be the result
of this, that and the other political device
has invariably been wrong? Personally I
can recollect talking with great assurance
about what was going to be the result of
the Labor platform. Perhaps “Boomer-
ang” is in the same position. Was he
right? I wasn’t, anyhow, and I don’t know
anyone who was. The same with one-man-
one-vote. The vital point about the I. and
It. is not what w r e think it will do, but
what we have reason to believe it will fail
to do. If it will not protect the nation from
the effects of brain storm, from the gusts of
lunacy which periodically sweep over
society, then it is no good. I don’t think
it will. I am sure it will not with Party
Government in full blast. An issue is not
“live” merely because some of us say it is.
If it was, the questions of electoral reform,
taxation reform and land ownership would
be agitating every citizen in the land, and
the Federal Parliament would have got
busy on the tariff long ago. The question
of how to win the war might reasonably
be suspected of being a live one. But is it?
—Yours, Tracker.
*
The Dean of Sydney in appealing for six
o’clock closing the other day gave as remark-
able an exhibition of logic as has been heard
for some minutes. In the trenches at Anzac
the Dean demanded and obtained rum; but.
on patriotic grounds, he opposed liquor being
available all day in N. S. Wales while it
could be obtained only every second day at
the Front. On the same line of reasoning
he ought to set his face against hot baths,
daily newspapers and church services, be-
cause all these were scarcer at Anzac than
they are in Sydney. The chaplain at Gal-
lipoli was in very little more danger than
thousands of the workers at their daily toil;
and lie was paid much better for any risks
he took than most of those toilers can ever
hope to be. In ordinary decency, then, the
fact that he wanted rum at Anzac ought to
convince him that others who carry on the
rough trade war at his own door
may want a stimulant also. Had the
Dean tested rum at the Front and
found it of no use, he might reasonably
enough have been expected to denounce the
stuff and all its relatives when he came
back to Australia; but seeing that he found
it good and insisted on having it, his atti-
tude is about as absurd as that of a pawn-
broker denouncing interest. If patriotism
required rum in the trenches, patriotism may
equally require rum or beer to help it
through its hard physical tasks at home.
Whether it is required in either case may be
open to doubt; but to admit its value in one
case is to assert its value in the other. Why
doesn’t the Dean say honestly that he is join-
ing in a meaningless parrot cry because it
seems up to a churchman to do it?
Justice Higgins’s recent outburst of indig-
nation because somebody had talked of
workmen getting what they could “enforce”
was very magnificent; but surely his Honor
had his tongue in his cheek all the time. If he
hadn’t he takes himself and the Arbitration
Courts much too seriously. The Naval Dock
clerks supply a recent illustration. These
men had been promised certain conditions
months ago; but there was continued delay
in giving effect to the promise. Why?
Without a doubt simply because clerks are
supposed to be poor worms who won’t turn.
It is impossible to conceive of a militant
union being treated that way. It ought to
be unprofitable for men to strike. Unfor-
tunately, it isn’t; and so long as employers
delay justice to men who don’t look as though
there is a kick in them, so long will there
be the incentive to skirmish round and find
a kick.
Every day the man who hasn’t gone to the
war because the papers are so confident
about the foolish mistakes of the enemy is
further cheered by the news that General
Von Slush has been appointed Minister for
Rations, or that sausage riots have broken
out in Berlin, or that some rich frau of
Dresden is being hissed 14 hours a day be-
cause she asked for a milk card for her lap
dog. “Ah,” says the delighted reader, “the
British blockade is beginning to tell! Noic
they know what a war of attrition is!”
Then he goes off confidently to his peaceful
job and remains cheerful until he gets the
bloodstained evening sheet and reads that
the Hun chemists have discovered how to
make a high explosive out of violets and
are extracting ham and eggs from the air.
That depresses him for the evening. But he
braces up for his next day’s shirk when he
is told very gravely that, owing to the
shortage of macaroni, the noblest Germans
are now lacing up their boots with rushes
brought from the Masurian Lakes. After
17 months of this sort of thing and “volun-
tary” recruiting, a heavyweight English
publicist has seen fit to warn his fellow-
countrymen that this isn't exclusively a
war of attrition, or boot-laces, or lap-dog’s
milk. “It is,” he gravely concludes,, “a war
of ivar.” It isn’t what you could call a
wildly original remark; but there is so
much mental fog about that it needed
stating.

“Centurion’s” yarn from the desert: —
The Sermssi, who have been giving us a little
recreation recently on the western border of Egypt,
are the champion wowsers of the world. They are
a religious sect and not a tribe, as most people sup-
pose, and their adherents are scattered all over the
Mohammedan world, though the main body of the
cult is located in oases in the great desert
between Egypt and Tripoli. They have their rivals
in the Wadani, who are equally convinced that
theirs is the only satisfactory brand of religion.
These two sects hate each other heartily for the
love of Allah, but have joined together in the pre-
sent trouble because they hate the Christian more.
The peculiar feature of the Senussi community
is the provision of Zawais, or sacred towns, in the
different oases. These are the headquarters
of each community and contain a sort of
university for students. As far as is known, no
white man has ever entered one of these Zawais.
The sect was founded about the beginning of the
nineteenth century by a Mohammedan John
Wesley, who came to the conclusion that too much
dog was decorating the old ritual. He stood pat
on the Koran with no trimmings. His son made
the venture a success. El Senussi, as this wowser
Pope is called, is now a big temporal as well as
spiritual personage. His method of taking, up a
collection is the envy of his Christian brethren, or
would be if they knew anything about it. When
the crops, etc., are in, the Senussi adherent takes
enough to satisfy his needs, and El Senussi takes
the rest. Rich absentee adherents are assessed and
have to “kick in” promptly.
Contrary to popular opinion, the Senussi has a
fairly well drilled and armed regular force, aug-
mented by ragtag and bobtail irregulars. Many
Turkish and a few German officers were with them
recently. The infantry are great marchers, and last
January, when the country was very boggy, they
actually outflanked an Australian Light Horse regi-
ment, though the latter did their best to outpace
them.
The severe defeat recently inflicted on them will
probably end their half-hearted rising. What will
happen to the wowser Pope the Christians’ Lord
only knows. Snowy Baker might import him into
Australia and match him with Archdeacon Snuffle.

Since it was decided to grant rejected vol-
unteers something that they might flash in
the face of suspicion there has been a curi-
ous feature about the recruiting figures.
The standard has been modified in many
ways, yet the proportion of rejects has in-
creased enormously, sometimes reaching
half of the total volunteers. One day lately
7(5 out of 156 men, who attended at Vic-
toria Barracks, Sydney, were turned down.
It is quite certain that many of these men
are bluffers, who, while wanting to skite
about their willingness to fight, are con-
fident that some physical incapacity will
save them. Amongst the material offering
was a man with one leg inches shorter
than the other and several heroes who
refused to strip for examination. But they
demanded the hard-luck certificates. Now,
what’s the matter with finding jobs for
them as clerks or rouseabouts or sanitary
experts? There are a lot of good soldiers
being wasted in those departments.
This ought to be the limit as a fact or a
dangerous misstatement. It came from R.
McCallum in Maoriland’s House of Reps
a few days ago. After referring to the bar-
riers which kept Billjim out of first-class
hotels in Egypt, McCallum said
Our men in Egypt were not allowed to sit in first-
class railway carriages, even if they paid their fares.
If it is impossible to give an official denial
to this, then Pearce may at least be ex-
pected to ascertain whether the same prac-
tice is still in force. If it is he should
be required to say what measures he has
taken to plainly intimate that Australia
simply won’t put up with it.

The prophecies of a renewed Victorian epi-
demic of meningitis, repeated at intervals
by qualified prophets ever since last year’s
outbreak, are being duly fulfilled. The whole
business has been shamefully mishandled.
The Commonwealth authorities, according
to quarantine-director Cumpston, can do
nothing whatever unless the disease comes
from outside Australia. On the other hand.
Dr. Noel Bull, who runs the meningitis
ward at the Melbourne Hospital, recom-
mends the isolation of all military camps
for seven days, since “last year a history
of contact with soldiers was obtained in
practically every case” —a suggestion which
throws scorn at the plaintive refusal of the
Commonwealth to accept any responsibility.
The Argus deprecates undue alarm—“seri-
ous as the outbreak is. there is no occasion
for a scare” —while Dr. Robertson, chair-
man of the Board of Health, declares that
“everyone should assume that he harbors
germs of meningitis and take steps to dis-
infect his nose and throat.” He also thinks
it may be necessary to close picture-theatres
and similar places. Aikman, M.L.C., de-
clares that “the Minister for Public Health
is wrangling in a most undignified manner
with municipal councils as to whether they
or the Minister should undertake to fight
the scourge”; and Donald McLeod, the
Minister, replies that he isn’t wrangling at
all. The position, which seems to be that
everbody in any sort of authority is wait-
ing for somebody else to do something, is
disgraceful. Incidentally it is a lamenta >le
thing for Liberalism that, in the last resort,
the responsibility for the administration o
the Health Act should fall upon a casual,
undistinguished old fellow who, having pui-
sued office for many years, doesn't know
what to do now that he has got it.
“Carburetter” : The time is ripening for a
nice family disaster on a motor-boat. The
X. S. Wales Marine authorities have grap-
pled with the problem to the best of their
limited ability, but accidents on these craft
are increasing. Within a few weeks this
list has been posted:—
1. An oil launch destroyed by fire on Port Hun-
ter. The crew scrambled ashore in what they
stood in. Launch destroyed.
2. An oil launch caught Are off Wollongong. Crew
got ashore in boat. Launch disappeared.
3. Launch caught fire in Newcastle. Occupants
swam ashore. Launch ruined.
4. Naval launch at Cockatoo blew up when cox-
swain struck a match.
5. Launch at Lane Cove, Port Jackson, damaged
by fire. Sunk.
Fires on motor-cars are bad enough, but fires
on launches are the devil, owing to the appal-
ling speed of the flame-bursf. According to
a recent Gazette the Marine Department
has licensed scores of motor-boats, carrying
up to 100 passengers, all over N. S. Wales’s
navigable waters. At Easter these boats,
crowded with men, women and children, ran
long distances to all parts of the Hawkes-
bury Inlet, from Brooklyn to Gosford, from
Broken Bay to Wiseman’s Ferry. And of
supervision I saw none. One boat with at
least 80 people on board (including me) ran
a 20-mile trip. The driver and others
smoked en route, and if assistance had been
urgently needed there was nobody in the
vicinity but Davy Jones. In the regula-
tions I can’t see any sort of provision as to
oil supply. No standard is fixed ; even a
flashing point isn’t named, nor is there any
stipulation as to testing the oil. Elaborate
care is taken in prescribing chemical fire
extinguishers and sand and scoops, but recent
accidents show that there is never any time
to put the fire out. More care should be
taken to prevent its outbreak. Providence
is giving those responsible a decent sporting
chance; on the launches recently destroyed
there weren’t any passengers. But the
luck can’t last and in some remote water-
way, probably, Ma will have her first well-
patronised crematorium. The Bulletin
(11/5/’l6) said that the British Board of
Trade won’t stand passenger seagoing petrol
launches. I find this is correct, and. further,
the B. of T. insists on paraffin on till motor-
launches. Why shouldn’t N. S. Wales follow
the lead ?

IN the seventeenth century, when despotic
kings were being toned down into limited
monarchs by the simple process of chasing
them with spears and occasionally removing
their heads, one of the burning questions
of the hour was whether the king was his
own first subject or whether he was above
his own laws. Something of the same sort
has been worrying the North Sydney Coun-
cil, where a Government building is to be
erected in defiance of the regulations. The
Council condemned the plans because cer-
tain party walls were not continued through
the roof. The Commissioners of the Gov-
ernment Savings Bank, who were concerned
in the erection of the premises, replied that
the plans were all right, and that as the
Government wasn’t bound by the Local
Government Act they were going to do as
they darned well pleased. Naturally the
North Sydney Council is indignant and
wants to raise Cain about the matter.
Whatever the merits of the particular case
may be, it seems pretty bad that local
endeavors for the beautification or safety
of a town should be liable to be thwarted
by any Government department which likes
to build a glorified dog-kennel in the main
street. Judged by its efforts in and about
Sydney, the State .Government doesn’t run
much to taste; and practically any local
body could give it points. Before the
Greater Sydney scheme drops in, it would
be well therefore to have this point settled.
If a party of real reform got to work on
the congested city and tried to wash its
face and straighten its deformities, it would
be bad business to have an unsympathetic
Government with power enough to blunder
in, pile up fresh dirt and make new crooked-
ness. The position seems too clear to re-
quire argument. What is wrong when
done by the individual cannot be any less
wrong when done by the mob.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
From scrub, and plain, and river bend
They’ve torn the best away :
The sound of wind and clean of limb
Must pay the toll to-day,
But exemptidn for the piebald, the skew-
bald, and the grey.
No one has asked for their consent,
Or cheered the chosen ones,
But far behind the trenches, where
The line of battle runs,
They’re hauling in the transport teams and
dragging up the guns.
Their hoofs have bruised the desert dust,
Where Egypt’s heat-waves dance,
Their backs have known the harness gall
On shot-torn roads in France;
No pomp and pride of war for them, or
trappings of romance.
And do they dream of sheltered ways,
Where once they wandered free,
From Gippsland’s timbered hills to where
The Flinders finds the sea,
The scrubs that line the Warrego, the flats
that hold the Bree?
And do they hear the crack of whips
Above the screaming shell?
(The colts come racing to the yard,
Clear chimes the cattle-bell)
And, dreaming there of home and peace,
awake to war and hell?
The Flemish plains will find a grave
For hearts the Barwon bred,
The Aisne will spill its overflow
Beside the Cooper’s dead.
Horse and man, it matters not, when all
the world is red.
From ridge and creek and cattle-camp
They’ve trucked them all away,
The best alone are good enough,
And so the buyers say,
But exemption for the piebald, the skew-
bald, and the grey.

Got this down before you go further
that of 143 Cars —new Cars—exhi-
bited or sold at the Sydney R.A.S.
1916 Show 82 were equipped with
Goodyear Tyres, of which a majority
were the popular “all weather tread.”
Makers of Motor-cars are jealous of
the tyre performance of their cars. If
tyres do not last—perhaps the car is
blamed. They are constantly on the
alert to determine which tyre meets
with the widest popular approval.
They have almost 200 different makes
of Tyres to choose from, and out of
this list Goodyear is given overwhelm-
ing preference. These motor-car manu-
facturers could “?a ve ” money by
selecting one of many other tyres.
Hut their deliberate and combined judg-
ment is that they will do better by
themselves and for their public, and for
the reputation of their car by paying
a little more money and fitting onlv
Goodyears.
They translate that conviction into
action by the marked preference which
they invariably give to Goodvear
Tyres.
Do you not begin to see that this con-
stitutes the strongest kind of a moral
and practical endorsement for Good-
year Tyres?
Does the fact that Goodyear Tyres are
preferred by the Motor-car manufac-
turers above all other brands really
mean anything to you as an individual
tyre buyer?
Goodyear Tyres and Tubes are now obtainable throughout Australia and N.Z.

A Further Postponement of Applause.
All the talk about strategy and tactics and
munitions and industrial advantages, and all
the weary harangues about national psy-
chology and the “will to conquer,” boil down
to this—-that the other fellow’ is again on the
offensive, and going strong. He is pounding
away at Verdun, pushing the Italian back-
wards, marching into Greece, keeping the
Russian busy around Riga, and violently
scrapping at the old address in Flanders.
There may be something in all this which
discloses weakness on the part of the enemy
—an indication that he is nearing the end
of his tether or something—-just as the in-
sistence of the German on losing men along
the Meuse was reported by various “experts”
two or three months ago to be proof that
he was running short of reserves. Also there
may not. Taking a broad view of things,
this paper at any rate can see no indication
that Lewis Habcourt, a member of the
British Government, was talking wildly the
other day when he remarked that “the Allied
armies will have to endure much before the
time comes for talking of peace.” This is
the inescapable conclusion from the meagre
supply of facts available. The position
has been critical all along, and every month
makes it more critical —for the enemy and
for the. Allies. The war has not yet
been won nor has it been lost, but
between winning and resisting defeat there
is a very wide difference; and the thing cuts
both ways. Nevertheless we have this to
the good; only through something in the
nature of an earthquake could the German
at this stage achieve what he failed to
accomplish when the ball was at his feet.
But a Germany on the defensive w’ould
more than likely be as stubborn a proposi-
tion as the Allies have so far found the
Turk. The main point at the moment is
that the enemy has not begun to be on the
defensive. All of w’hich means that the
time is not ripe anywhere for bursting into
cheers, but that, so far as the opponents of
Kultur are concerned, the moment continues
to lie opportune for getting to the top of
their form. For when the enemy does be-
gin to move astern it will do no harm if
the punch he gets keeps him going that
way. Australia’s own are concealed some-
where in the middle of the unpleasantness
and their doings from day to day are
wrapped in impenetrable gloom except when,
by the indulgence of some brilliant genius,
news is flashed through that Billjim was
a competitor in a bush-whacking competi-
tion or had listened to a speech by someone.

In spite of its “Nonconformist conscience,”
South Aus. had too much sense to treat its
one big statesman, the late C. C. Kingston,
as the British public treated Dilke ; and
the City of Adelaide does honor to itself
as well as to him in erecting a statue of
the great democrat eight years after his
death. Two men could hardly be more un-
like than he and Chief Justice Way; yet
the old Judge took a leading part in arrang-
ing the details of the statue. But he did
not live to see it erected, and the final
details had to be settled by Bonython,
whose Advertiser was a sound supporter
of Kingston in his prime. The statue,
unveiled by the G.-G. last week, is of bronze,
the work of Drury, R.A. It stands on the
west side of Victoria Square, looking to-
wards Kingston’s faithful district of West
Adelaide. The pedestal, of S. Australian
marble, calls him simply “patriot and states-
man.” At the sides of it are bronze
reliefs showing him presiding at the
Federal Convention and with his col-
leagues in the Commonwealth’s first
Cabinet; and there is a striking
panel of his father, George Strickland
Kingston, a pioneer of S. Aus., Speaker,
and President of the Leg. Council. King-
ston’s widow, a pathetic figure, laid a
wreath of violets at the foot of the statue
when it was unveiled. There is only one
feature about the work which the democrat
himself might not have cared for. It is in
Court dress, which may be due either to
Way’s influence or to the sculptor’s desire
to avoid modelling modern trousers when-
ever they can be avoided. “A born leader of
men,” was the Governor-General’s epitaph,
and Barton added this: “He was generous,
sympathetic, and loyal to a degree. He
never deserted a friend—although some
deserted him.”
The attempt to bring Pemberton Billing
into ridicule for his attack on the Govern-
ment in the matter of its aerial fleet is quite
characteristic of the laissez-faire party in
the House of Commons. Billing, the new
member who had no party political influence
to back his candidature, is a man who does
things. Starting as a trooper in the Natal
Mounted Police, he got himself into des-
patches during the Boer War. He founded
the first aerodrome in England seven years
ago, and is the inventor and constructor of
aeroplanes as well as the designer of an
ingenious anti-aircraft gun. No wonder the
average M.P. disparages him.

An advance report from the secretary of
the N.S.W. Film Censor Board: —
The Board, or as many of its members as could
fit into the Criterion Theatre (Sydney), sat in judg-
ment upon “Madame X” on Saturday evening, as a
sort of dress rehearsal for the more exacting duties
to be presently done in connection with the flickers.
The Inspector-General of Police turned down his
thumb when he heard the evidence of Fontaine, of
the Bordeaux Police, during the trial of Madame X.
If such a play were allowed, he said, the effect
upon young policemen would be dreadful: they
would be lured into giving their evidence in a pain-
fully unofficial manner, and might even be tempted
to arouse laughter in the court. Such misbehavior
would be subversive of all police discipline.
The representative of the B.M.A. gave his vote
emphatically against the licensing of the play. He
pointed out that Arthur Greenaway’s name is not
upon the B.M.A.’s books; and the B.M.A. felt that,
in the interests of the public, an unauthorised actor
should not be allowed to practise even on the
stage. He might be tempted to go and cure a
school-child.
The representative of the Arts Society voted
“No” on account of the furniture in the room of
the Bordeaux pub. The exhibition of such articles
would have a bad influence upon the minds of the
public.
The representative of the Labor Leagues thought,
that the play might pass as an allegory of the
way the worker is sweated by the capitalists, though
before it received his permission the author must
put in a reference to the necessity of abolishing the
Upper House.
The representative of the Bench pointed out vari-
ous technical errors in the argument and the plead-
ings and in the murder trial. Although these
might with fairness be put down to the fact that
the trial was held in France, still they might con-
fuse young counsel when they appeared before him
—and that would hinder the course of justice. Also,
he noted with disapproval that the President of
the Court did not make a single epigram. This would
convey to the public the impression that the Bench
did not make epigrams, which might serve to bring
the Bench into disrepute.
The Council for Civic and Moral Advancement,
through its representatives, said that its condemna-
tion of “Madame X” was unequivocal. Unanimously
its delegates had resolved that they could not
countenance the public performance of a play which
showed the heroine drinking absinthe and ether
and liking both, leaving her husband for a lover,
and finally sinking to the lowest depths that a
woman is capable of—a woman, too, who murdered
her lover and was acquitted against all the evi-
dence. The spectacle of this degraded wretch was
not one that could be publicly shown to the civic-
ally and morally unadvanced, especially the uncivic
and immoral youth of this city.
The representative of the Catholic Federation
objected to the play on the ground that no priest
was present at the death of the heroine.
In their report to the Chief Secretary conveying
their emphatic and unanimous refusal to license
such a shocking and uncivic spectacle, the members
of the Board admit that Madge Fabian robs the
ruin of the heroine of its utter degradation, though
she thereby spoils the moral, and that this actress
is surprisingly effective in the earlier scenes, dis-
playing an unsuspected reserve of dramatic power.
But they regret that in the final court-scene she
misses that thrilling revelation of a mother’s feel-
ing shown by Muriel Starr in what remains that
actress’s finest piece of work on the Australian
stage. They also note that in the first act Sydney
Stirling is at times emotional without being stac-
cato ; that Gerald Kay Souper is a rather stodgy
husband ; that Harold Moran, though excellent, fails
to achieve the subtle effects of his predecessor in
the part; that Charles Lawrence and Arthur Cor-
nell clown their comedy parts with the exuberance
evidently necessary as relief in a melogloom; that
Leonard Stephens’s speech to the jury is almost as
good as it is long; that Frank Harvey, as Laroque,
is delightfully insouciant, stressing rather the
comedy element than the dramatic; that his
trousers are a dream; and that Gwen Burroughs
makes a charming chambermaid and Alma Rock
Phillips a quite innocuous ingenue. The Censor
Board finally insists that such an immoral drama
should not be allowed to contaminate the Criterion
Theatre for longer than to-night.
It is understood that the Firm will there-
fore withdraw the play, and on Saturday
something harmlessly soapy, entitled “It
Pays to Advertise,” will be substituted,
with Myrtle Tannehill and Hale Hamilton
in the leading parts.
War is a nice, clean outdoor business if
it’s what it’s pictured by the French Gov-
ernment's Official Films, now showing at
Sydney Town Hall. The newspaper readei
will find them as mild as watered milk aftei
his usual morning orgy of Verdun —“slopes
covered with German dead,” “ravines
choked with storming parties lashed by
whips of fire,” “trenches converted into
smoking rubbish heaps,” and so on. If tin
French movie-operators had any such
material available, they either didn’t film it
or else the French authorities didn’t want
to stress that side of war. What is shown
might have been made very comfortably 20
miles behind the lines; wherefore the pic-
tures have about the same blood interest
as the photograph of the suburban street
where the murder occurred with the actual
house marked with an X. A public that has
been banqueting on horrors for nearly tw’o
years is not going to be satisfied with sw-ept
and mopped battlefields. Still the films have
an interest of their own in that they show
a good deal of the working of the elaborate
war machinery. The wonderful simplicity
of the French .75 —an artilleryman is set-
ting the fuses with a sort of beex--engine, and
the long, lean gun is swallowing and spitting
shells as fast as they can be fed to it—
is a joy in itself. So is the box-kite photo-
graphic section putting its war toys to-
gether and sending an electrically-operated
camera aloft to map the enemy’s trenches.
Altogether it is an unsensational but educa-
tional show; and no Australian who sees it
can doubt the need of long and elaborate
preparation for the highly specialised busi-
ness of war.
“Mother Goose” and the feathered gold-
mine called Priscilla are still strengthening
the gold reserve at Her Majesty’s,
Sydney. What a beautiful show Premier

Looking at “Peg o’ My Heart” these even-
ings one remembers poor old Dublin and
wonders whether Peg’s father, whose patri-
otic speeches she quotes so prettily, would
have been operated on by a firing party. If
the audience allows its attention to wander
in that way, the Sydney Palace applause
begins to sound like the rattle of musketry.

formation threatens to be interesting, par-
ticularly the migration of Frediswyde
Hunter-Watts from the wailing Lady Anne
to the coquettish Rosalind.

Queen Vera Pearce, of the Sydney Tivoli
Follies, attained her 21st theatrical birth-
day last week-end ; but the heavens neglected
to fall and no tidal waves were reported
along the coast. The only signs given to
the outside world were (1) some official
agitation visible in the Tivoli advts., (2) a
frequency of bouquets, and (3) a few new
artists in the first part. Jimmy Fletcher,
a new talking contortionist from the U.S.,
strikes Woodrow Wilson attitudes, and Mary
Dorr, a bright girl from the same vaudeville
address, works off some singing impersona-
tions. Norton and Earle, in songs and
sketches with dance trimmings, also win
respectful mention. The front car of the
bill now’ compares better with the rear one,
which contains the Follies.
