Selinsky, violinist, made his first appear-
ance in Melbofirrie on Saturday night at
the Auditorium. Melba had made boom-
raising remarks in praise of the young man.
but was unable to be present to "give him a
first clap. The house was thick as bees —
and ail muffled up in furs or its warmest
clothes, for a fog drifted in from out-of-
doors and wrapped everything in a romantic
haze. The same fog got into Selinsky’s pre-
cious violin and worried the young man.
Still he made his music sing and sway
deliciously. And he gave a final little en-
core waltz or dance-of-sorts that sent the
crowd swinging home at a Pied Piper pace.
Frank St. Leger accompanied. He is the
thin youth whom Melba took round the
U.$.A. with her, and then entertained at
Coombe Cottage. He was once an infant
prodigy, but on every fresh appearance in
Australia he seems to add a foot or two
of length. Miss Elsie Treweek padded the
intervals with song.
“The Spoilers,” Rex Beach’s meaty novel
minced into a melodrama sausage, wgs
served at Melbourne Princess’s on Satur-
day in the gunpowdery atmosphere of the
original. Most of the play characters rely
strongly on their artillery in an argument,
three of them, Alec McNamara, Roy Glenis-
ter and Bill Dextry, being pre-eminent in
military readiness. The drama is a conflict
between McNamara, a claim-jumper, and
Glenister and Dextry, representing the
jumped, so the proceedings naturally bristle
with threatening weapons, voices being lost
in the din of high explosives. Mac., of
course, gets the worst of the deal, for, after
the smoke of the last battle has cleared
away, he is found in the custody of the
Alaskan law whose aid he had invoked to
down the mining men. There is the usual
girl in the play, and she is alleged to be a
radiant influence who turns the most law-
less ruffian into something like a curate.
Even the deadly McNamara, who has made
the damsel his unwilling associate, finds her
sufficiently taking to offer her marriage;
whilst his accomplice, the furtive Struve,
suffers paroxysms of emotion when she is
about. The young lady, Helen Chester, who
eventually weds Roy Glenister because lie

Melbourne Royal continues to relate by
biograpli how a Nation was Born with
Sambo blood in its veins. This lengthy
view of the Murkan Civil War is sanguin-
ary, riotous, and only occasionally impres-
sive, but it serves to reveal that when Abe
Lincoln was Boss the U. S. Government
never rushed to the dictionary in defence of
its principles. Lincoln is the greatest fig-
ure in “The Birth of a Nation,” and the
passages in which he occurs afford a wel-
come relief to the intelligent spectator
fatigued by the yards of massacre, hugger-
mugger and protracted nigger-hunting by
the Ku Klux Klan. The Ku Kluxers are
the limit in comic-opera frightfulness, and
when they gallop across the scenery in their
flapping nightgowns, the colored populace is
expected to shiver with dread. After to-
morrow (Friday) night “The Birth” shifts
over to the Auditorium to make room for
the first appearance on Saturday of Miss
Madge Fabian in “On Trial.”
Rose Berry, another songster and talker
from the U.S.A., was the only fresh face at
Melbourne Tivoli on Saturday; but Blanche
Colvin, a new songaloguist, and Jack Born-
hold, whose turn is described as an “amaz-
ing novelty,” were to happen on Monday
and cause further changes in the entertain-
ment. . Meantime Horace Goldin proceeds
with h,is exhibitions of trickery, mostly de-
voting his time to the mechanical illusions
wherein his own visible work is limited
to firing a pistol and looking blandly
surprised at the result. In other parts
of the programme Cardo and Noll
maintain their operatic intensity, while
Marie King-Scott continues her pretence of
rustic artlessness just to aggravate the
effect of her songs and jests.
“Something Doing” remains the busiest
half of the show at Melbourne Bijou. The
revue contains some interesting features,
not the least whereof is the Futurist Ballet
by Gick Watson and other flappers. In
Part I of the present bill the proceedings
are slow so long as the Littlejohns juggle
their glittering implements, but matters liven
up when the Paulastos tumble about and
Harold Parkes recites C. J. Dennis’s “Sing-
ing Soldiers.” Also Harold gets loud
applause by declaiming “No Sport,” which
discloses Den’s views of Hun methods of
warfare.

From an old Westralian campaigner:—
The Country Party got a severe bump at the
recent AVestralian Legislative Council elections.
Patrick, the sitting member, turned his Liberal coat
to run as a farmers’ candidate against a Laborite
for the Central Province, and was licked. For the
South-West Province (two vacancies) and the East
Province there were straight-out fights between Lib-
eral and Country party nominees. In each case the
hayseed was blown away, although Hamersley, the
old member for the East Province, only scraped in
with the help of the returning-officer after a recount
had knocked out his first-count majority of one
vote. For the remaining (North-East) Province
the Labor man beat an Independent for the seat
previously held by R. D. McKenzie, who is running
a motor-ambulance somewhere, in Joffre’s district.
Although the Hayseed organisation extracts a pledge
from its Parliamentary reps, to abstain from taking
office while in a minority in the House, it looks
as if they will combine with the Liberals to bring
about a dissolution when Parliament does reassemble
-—-some time in July. But unless a Redistribution
Bill can be forced through first, the solid Goldfields
block of electorates practically settles the chances
of the anti-Labor section securing a decent working-
majority. The wilted mining centres are a satire
on the one-vote-one-value theory, but they may pos-
sibly save the Scaddan Government from the kick-
out its awful administration has earned.
The notorious negro convict William
King, who gave Pentridge more uneasy
hours than any other man for 25 years past,
was recently discharged, conditionally on his
exiling himself to America. But Wilson’s
luck still holds; for the human wild-beast
died and was buried at sea.

“Stargazer”: I see there is some talk of
bringing American evangelist Billy Sunday
to Australia to awaken this democracy to
a sense of its unholy shortcomings. Sun-
day, with charming Yank candor, has
avowed that he will not “quit hosing these
guys with the scaldin’ waters of all holi-
ness,” till he has coopered one million solid
in dollars; and if he comes to Australia it
will be in strenuous pursuit of dollar No.
909,000. The suggestion is that he be
accompanied by “a well-chosen party of
American evangelists, of peculiar excellence
in their different specialities.” What is
wanted is an Evangelical Variety Company.

“Barmitzoah”: The Hebrew can still
paddle his own canoe as cleverly as did the
clipper ship Ark, Noah master, outward
bound to Ararat for orders. In witness
whereof I cite the experiences of two of the
Chosen on the s.s. Dongarra, whose crew,
in view of their rough passage, might well
have regarded the pair of seafarers as
blood-brothers to Jonah. Ten days out
from N’Yark one of the Dongarra’s officers
noticed a stranger on the fo’c’s’le. Brought
to bay, he unearthed a companion. The
strangers were an American and a Dutch
Jew, who, hoping to obtain engagements as
vaudeville artists which the States had
failed to yield them, had stowed away to
Maoriland. The skipper put them in cus-
tody, and soon afterwards the trouble be-
gan. The Dongarra made such heavy
weather of it that even the ship’s cat was
glad to reach Auckland. There the Hebrew
brethren were brought before the local
beaks, charged with having stowed away,
and a fare of £2O was claimed from each.
They proved that they had good chances of
supporting themselves as vaudeville per-
formers, so although convicted —not of being
vaudevillains, but of being stowaw r ays—they
were merely ordered to come up for sentence
when called upon, the question of the fare
being held over. With the keenness of their
race they lost no time in getting in on their
boom, and next night were appearing at an
Auckland theatre billed as “The Stowaways
from the Dongarra.”
“Up Stage” : The threatened movement to
form a United Arts Corps of actors and
artists for home defence has evidently been
dropped overboard. The circular that
reached our theatre stated: “It [the move-
ment] would do much to kill that peculiar
apathy which is part and parcel of our
make-up.” To date 35 native-born actors
have volunteered, and 26 are on active ser-
vice. Max Clifton (late Bilanderson’s),
Alf Scarlett (Bland Holt’s), and Wilfred
Hilary (Meynell and Gunn’s) hold com-
missions ; and with a few exceptions the
others are n.c.o.’s. This list doesn’t include
Lieuts. Dartnell, Y.C., and G. E. Kelly,
D. 5.0., both good Australians w r ho have
since taken the final call. Kelly had mana-
gerial aspirations around the Sydney
suburbs before he answered his cue at the
Gallipoli landing. Where is that “apathy”?
Sumner Locke, the bright Australian girl
who looks through pince-nez on the world
and finds it mostly a joke, has written a
dramatic version of her “Mum Dawson,
Boss,” stories, which is to be put ou shortly
at Melbourne King’s by the Bailey-Grant
Company—an Australian play by an Aus-
tralian company. Sumner reckons that she
will be the first Australian woman drama-
tist to be played professionally, and she is
probably right.
Theatrical history was made quietly ou
May 22 when the compact Howard Vernon
Co., with the veteran himself in the lead,
lit the first professional footlights that ever
glimmered at Canberra—the place where a
continent is pretending to make a capital
out of a rabbit warren.
“Kaniva”: The death of “Lou” Carina
deprives Melbourne of its premier drummer.
Heaps of people will remember his father,
Pietro Canna, who was regarded as the
world’s champion on the bladdery instru-
ment. In 1862 he was appearing in Mel-
bourne at the old Alhambra Concert Hall,
where he gave an exhibition with 25 of his
pets, from the small side-bomb to the larg-
est of kettles. The piece, which was very
descriptive, was entitled “The Siege of
Sebastopol,” and the way Canna skipped
round producing the sharp volleys from the
rifles, the dull booming of cannon and other
orchestral battle effects was terrific even
from an athletic point of view. Canna.
senior, played in orchestras for many years
in Melbourne, and was drowned off Burmah
in 1884 by the overturning of a surf-boat.
The new Majestic Theatre in Adelaide is
being rushed to be ready for its opening
next Saturday. It is on the site of the old
Tivoli, which was Hudson’s* Bijou before
that, and originally, as White’s Rooms, the
chief assembly-hall of infant Adelaide. The
new house is (of course) to be the last word
in comfort and effect; all new houses are. It
has two features claimed to be new to Aus-
tralia —a counter-weight curtain-raising
idea and no internal pillars. Sayers and
Lennon have been behind the scheme, Wil-
liams and Good being the architects; and
the Majestic Amusements Co. will run a
Brennan-Fuller variety show twice daily—
a new idea for Adelaide.
Clayton Hamilton, in N. Y. Bookman,
makes the painful computation that every
season he has to attend about 150 new pro-
ductions ; and only one play in every 23 is
worth some afterthought. Wonder if it
would cheer him up to come to Australia?

For the most cutting thing said of late
about the Holman Government its own
Attorney-General is responsible:—■
In my opinion there will be an overwhelming
majority in favor of the retention of the Upper
Chamber.
In other words, Davy Hall and his colleagues
have made such a horrible mess of things
that the people will not trust the House in
which the Government has a majority.
That good Australian and Protectionist,
Senator Stewart, made his usual kick in the
Senate last week against the tariff delay,
and received the usual answer that Hughes
being absent, the tariff must wait. To that
Stewart replied that the presence of Hughes
is not needful to the rising of the sun,
which is scarcely more vitally necessary to
Australian health. That was all he got,
however—the Government is so busy with
things alleged to be Imperial that things
that are Australian don’t matter.
State frights are not feeling very well in
Adelaide just at this moment. The other
week a Victorian youngster set out for
South Aus., burgling as he went. Crossing
the border at Wolseley he was easily
nabbed by the S. Aus. police as soon as he
had done his first “job”; whereupon he
spread out his trophies gathered from
Ararat, Horsham and other towns in his
grand tour. The S. Aus. police for a
moment thought they had been smart where
the Vies, had been merely dull; but pre
sently, when S. Aus, suggested that Vic.
should take back its young imp and Vic.
said that S. Aus., having arrested the imp
for a crime committed in that State, must
keep him there, the local Law began to won-
der whether there hadn’t been method in
Victoria’s dullness.
Alf Edden, who has sat for the district
now called Kahibah ever since the first
N.S.W. Parliamentary Labor party arrived
in 1891, has just been turned down by his
local P.L.L. There are not many of those
ninety-oners left in politics now. Only
McGowen and Cann have stuck to their
seats throughout. George Black has been
m and out, J. D. Fitzgerald is leading the
House which the 25-year-old party came in
to wipe out, Joe Cook and A. Gardiner are
in Federal politics, and Arthur Rae will be
there again after the next Senate election.
Alf Edden, though he battered a few of the
letters of the alphabet, was one of the
natural orators of the House, and few more
telling speeches have been heard there of
late years than those in which the old man
pleaded for better treatment for the coal-
miners. But he was no administrator, and
never ought to have been given the Mines
Department.

Darkened Her Grey Hair and
Promoted Its Growth with a
Simple Home-made Mixture.
Miss Blanche Rose, a well-known actress,
who darkened her grey hair with a simple
preparation which she mixed at home, in a
recent interview made the following state-
ment: “Any lady or gentleman can darken
ther grey hair and make it soft and glossy
with this simple recipe, which they can mix
at home. To a half-pint of water add loz.
of bay rum, a small box of Orlex Compound
and ioz. of glycerine. These ingredients can
be bought at any chemist’s at very little cost.
Apply to the hair twice a week until it be-
comes the required shade. This will make a
grey-haired person look 20 years younger. It
is also fine to promote the growth of hair,
relieves itching and scalp humors, and is
excellent for dandruff and falling hair.” [1

C Squadron of the a?th Light Horse
arrived to do patrolling duty at a spot where
the Libyan Desert meets the irrigated area.
There is a big population of Christian Copts
and Mussulman Arabs thereabout, and they
love each other about as well as Satan and
Father Peter do. The advent of Australian
soldiers pushed the local Copt potentate up
in importance, wherefore he was moved to
tears of joy and this address of welcome: —
To the Commander in-Chief of the British
Force of Behnasa.
Dear Sir, —With the deepest respect and admira-
tion, I beg to present you some nut. ment for the
victorious English Army our protector. The
materials present are
Number. Kind of Material.
1 .. A Calf.
2 .. Rams.
40 . . Okkas of Bread.
16 . . Okkas of Milk.
230 . . Eggs.
1 . . Pot of Butter.
At last I hope that you will kindly accept this
humble offer as a sign of true loyality, thankful
feeling and hearty love toward the British Crown.
It was the proudest moment in that squad-
ron commander’s pious life.

Solemn warning given by a Sydney
magistrate to two lads last week: —
“When you see a crowd of persons gathered on
Sunday night, keep away from them. Rest assured
they are up to something you should keep out of.”
Dear, dear —are even our archbishops now
as bad as that?
“E.L.C.W.”: The new frightfulness has
been giving a lively time to a party of Aus-
tralians in London. The Kaiser s confi-
dants may deny torpedoing the Tubantia
till they’re black in the face; but if they
didn’t torpedo it they made it a physical
impossibility for any boat to dodge death.
They’ve strewed the North Sea so thick
with mines that you can hardly help hitting
them. Agag isn’t in it with the ship’s
captains these days when making the Hook
of Holland offing. That was the experi-
ence of four Sydney men, B. R. Wise,
Jones, Pratten and Mason, who went over
to see how Holland runs its Red Cross.
They stood all the time of the crossing in
their lifebelts, and in dodging the high explo-
sives took two days to come back instead of
the usual ten hours.
* * * *
At Melbourne, which is less particular
about disease than Sydney, with its past
experiences of plague and smallpox, they
“board” most of the military invalids on
the hospital ship, and thereafter let them
go home without “reporting” at a hospital.
A Light Horse officer, with his left leg off
at the hip, faced the three young doctors
who constituted the Board in Port Phillip
a while ago. “Well,” said one of them,
cheerfully, as the repatriated ruin subsided
into a chair, “and what’s your trouble?”

By the Office Curate :
As a set-off to the proposed closing of Cathedral-
street and granting part of Phillip Park to provide
an ornamental approach to Sydney’s R.C. Cathedral,
Lord Mayor Meagher has suggested to the City
Council a favor which it might do to St. Peter’s
Anglican Church in Woolloomooloo. The church
at present is hidden from view by a row of shops
in William-street and a terrace of five houses in
Bourke-street, none of them with more than 40ft.
depth. 'The Council’s scheme for widening William-
street will mean that the shops in that street and
two of the Bourke-street houses must come down.
The Gaud Mayor’s proposal (made after a deputa-
tion from the church had whispered something in
his ear) is that the remaining three houses in the
Bourke-street terrace should also be resumed, and
their site handed over to the church, the latter to
pay off the cost of the resumption by annual in-
stalments. The narrow lane at the back of the
houses would be thrown in free, and the church
would thus have a frontage to both William and
Bourke streets. If the scheme goes through, it will
settle a long-standing grievance. When St. Peter’s
fine stone church was built, in the fifties of last
century, the site was given by old-time Sydney
merchant Burdekin, who had big blocks of freehold
thereabouts. A few years later he had a row with
the church authorities, and as the title to the land
had never been legally conveyed to St. Peter’s, he
built the aforesaid shops and houses so as to hide
HIS EXPERIENCE.
S HE: “But how do you know their affair is
not platonic?”
H E: “Well, Eve never met a platonic
widow."
church and school from view. Probably two-thirds
of the passengers along William-street don’t know
of the church’s existence, in spite of signboards at
corners of by-streets pointing “To St. Peter’s
Church”—one a few years ago was affixed to the
wall of a well-known Temple of Venus. On the
corner of William and Bourke streets Burdekin
planted a pub, called after him, which passed out
in the slaughter following a local-option vote.
Narrow lanes were made along two sides of the
church to give access to the back of the shops
and houses, leaving church and school on a sort of
island bounded by lanes and backyards.

The Melbourne Bumbles who asked Town
Clerk Ellery for a report on local municipal
matters after his 10 months’ experience of
them are sorry they spoke. Ellery had a
pocketful of blue metal, and aimed it with
enthusiasm at the trams, the dust, the smoke,
the untidy parks, the practice of driving pigs
to market at all hours, and the partly-
neglected Yarra. But his weightiest missile
was reserved for the city’s electric supply
scheme. About this he remarked that though
it was started in ’94, while Sydney’s was set
going 10 years later, the latter had more
than twice the annual revenue and now
makes a profit of £64,758 as against the
older scheme’s £36,960. It looks from all
this as if Ellery has the quaint idea that
Melbourne’s councillors are elected to serve
the public.

“Rule Nisi” : Re the suggestion that
Judge Docker deserves a rest, it is an
opportune moment to consider the N. S.
Wales District Court more comprehen-
sively. Last week the venerable Judge
Murray, presiding in a criminal court,
handed out seven years to a man who stole
£lO worth of chocolates. He sentenced to
penal servitude for life a husband convicted
of shooting with intent to maliciously
wound his wife. The man, under provoca-
tion, had fired at the woman, but missed.
Maybe the chocolate-stealer deserved seven
years; maybe the bad shot deserved life.
What I wish to remark is that if a man is
owed over £5OO he can’t sue for it before a
District Court Judge. That person is only
thought to know enough about law to decide
questions under £5OO. But he can award
half a lifetime to a person accused of steal-
ing a box of sweets. A District Court Judge
can’t decide a divorce case. He has not
power to separate man and wife for any
conjugal shortcoming. But he may sen-
tence to death or separate from his or her
life-partner for ever any man or woman
guilty of a serious offence against the per-
son. The whole business is so anomalous
that it screeches for overhaul.
A return shows that between March, 101"),
and February, 1916, there were 40,007
attendances of venereal patients at Prince
Alfred Hospital (Sydney), and there were
2105 new patients. If in a city of three-
quarters of a million there are 40,007 atten-
dances of venereal patients at one hospital
during one year, and if there are 2105 new
patients in that time, how many
patients were treated privately, and
how many weren’t treated at all,
during the same period? The answer
can't even be guessed at, but it’s safe
to say that the figures wouldn’t be nearly
so awful if the Law were to (1) compel doc-
tors and chemists to report venereal cases
and (2) inform every patient who came
within ear-range that he’d have to be a very
unobtrusive citizen until he was cured.

THE PERFECT MAR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When first this dreadful war began my young friend
Snitchford’s mar
Said “What about it, Herbert? I suppose you mean
to go?”
To which he had to answer “Yes,” though poor young
Snitch was far
From wishing to be shot or stabbed by any foreign
foe.
But my old mar says: “Algernon, let volunteering
be:
Remember what the Good Book says, ‘Them persons
also serves
What only stands and waits.’ Your safest game’s to
bide with me.”
An ideel mother, that, for one, like me, with chronic
nerves.
Bill Smith, although the owner of a wife and pleasant
home,
A fambly and a job worth fully sixty bob per week,
Chucks ev’rything, enlists, and hurries off across the
foam.
My mother says: “You go straight down to Gridyard,
Sons and Bleek
(Bill’s firm) ; take all your ref’rences, your hymns,
and in your fob
The watch they gave you at the Dodgecombe Sunday-
school last year—
Take all of ’em along, my son, and try and get his
job.”
Now can you wonder that I hold my mar so very
dear ?
I never got Bill’s job. I may remark the boss
announced
That young and healthy applicants might just as
well hop off;
lie also said some youngsters there would presently
be bounced
Unless they did “the proper thing.” I scoffed a
scornful scoff
And left, to find my own soft job, which I had held
for years,
Had that same day been promised to a cove of forty-
eight.
My mar says: “What’s the odds? Stay home till
something fresh appears.”
Ah! looked on as a mother she undoubtedly is great.
Through all the months that followed, when they
tried to make me join
By rallies, public meetings and brief personal
appeals,
By bitter sneers, white feathers and the cutting off
of coin
(All treatment which a Christian lad emphatically
feels),
My good old mar stood firm and said: “No, Algie,
don’t you stir.
What cause have you to plunge in bloodshed, dirt,
disease and strife?”
Ah J friends, they give V.C.’s to them as do no more
than her.
I reckon that that grand old soul has saved my
precious life.
N.S.W.
10-2-4.

Syrup of Figs—“Calitig”—will begin to do

saut-tasting, refreshing fruit laxative

“Up Stage” : I am an actor. Heaven help
mP ;— iV mere strolling player who moves
from place to place and sleeps in suburban
barns that are full of hash instead of sweet-
smelling hay, as in the olden times. Being
what I am, I suppose I shouldn’t have in-
dulged in the luxury of fatherhood ; but
this is a fine, new country, crying out for
population, the wife and I are fond of chil-
dren —and there you are! He’s a rosy,
good-tempered little fellow —the sort of
baby that decent, home-living people always
seem eager to play with. But each time
we encounter the usual landlady with the
cameo brooch we are made to feel that any
sort of stinking, flea-bitten hound would
be more welcome than our young Aus-
tralian. I may be unduly optimistic, but
I'm hoping that after this war has combed
out the world it will be made a criminal
offence for any pub or lodging-house to
refuse shelter to the indispensable infant.
If not I’m afraid I’ll some day strangle the
soured star boarder on whom the ’lady
always puts the blame.

“Deir” : Among the surprises of Egypt are
the incredibly ancient Christian monasteries
scattered about the
Western Desert, far
from the tourist’s track.
I recently ran into one
at Wadi Natrun, an oasis
on the edge of a vast
soda deposit which has
been worked from time
immemorial. It was, of
course, Coptic; but the
R.C. padre with our
regiment got in touch
with the hermits who in-
habit it and gathered
some details. It claims
to- be the oldest Chris-
tian monastery in the
world, and scornfully re-
pudiates the Greek show
at Mount Athos as being
a thing of yesterday.
Its records go back 1200
years. The inmates do
no proselytising; they
are just hermit men and
women —for there are
nuns, too —entirely - self-
contained within a stone
wall 50ft. high and 30
thick, outside which they never go.
“Dalriada”: Sometimes the Irish fairy-
story does come true, and the “crock iv
gould” sheds its Danae shower into the lap
of the deserving. In this case it has filled
a widow’s cruse after lying unclaimed for
vears, the beneficiary being Mrs. I*. O.
O’Brien, of Millbrook, in the Ballarat dis-
trict. Her late husband, Patrick O'Brien,
was, with his brother John (also “the
late”), a legatee under the will of a Queens-
land relative, Patrick O’Callaghan. The
property devised was a well-stocked station
in the northern State. It was bequeathed
to the O’Briens, and, in the event of then-
death to their widows, O Callaghan s
brothers in Ireland also being left a share.
Mrs. Patrick is believed to be the only
O’Brien left; and she is said to have been
offered £30,000 for her whack of it. The news
of the existence of the legacy only arrived
through a casual Queensland visitor to
Ballarat, who accidentally furnished links
of knowledge which joined those held by a
local man, and made a complete chain. Mrs
O’Brien’s solicitors, Pearson and Mann, did
the rest.
“W.C.M.”: The crack shot mentioned
recently by “Y.Z.,” who was rejected because
he stuttered, would have been useless in a
bayonet charge. My infantry text-book
says: “In a bayonet charge the soldier will
cheer.” Now we really couldn’t have a man
who ran forward yelling, "Hip-pip-pip-pip-
pip!” You can’t terrify a civilised enemy
by pretending to be a motor car.
The man in khaki has settled one of Aus-
tralia’s most vexed problems—“ What shall
we do with our girls?”

“Barber Joe”: Let the soldier look to
his hair. A pink, bald head glistening with
honest sweat and wanking in the sun over
the edge of a trench is like a full-page
advertisement to the watchful sniper who
knows his business. The mates of any bald
warrior should insist that he keep his hat
on, or stain his bare cranium a neutral
tint. A hairless patriot now in camp in
N. S. Wales was almost stung to mutiny
last w T eek by a command to march down
to the other end of the paddock and bare
his skull to the sun in order that his com-
pany officers could judge what sort of
sniper-bait he was. Head and hand-stain
will be the next field requirement of the
fighting man.

“J.F.H.” (London) : Kalgoorlie and
Bockhampton are 2000-odd miles apart on
the map, but they are next-door neighbors
in London. A few hours after the gold-
fields Church of England diocese had made
a pitiful appeal to English charity to save
it from financial collapse, the Bishop of
Stepney was presiding over some Londoners
brought together to help Rockhampton keep
its dusty old head above water. This con-
stant circulation of the hat in London in
aid of alleged poverty-stricken Australian
Anglican bishoprics is the worst possible
advertisement for the Commonwealth, and
it is time it was made a statutory offence.
The Rev. E. Iv. Streeton, late of St. Paul’s,
Rockhampton, remarked that people might
ask why the diocese of Rockhampton does
not support itself. It certainly is a reason-
able question. The R.C.’s, ' Presbyterians,
Baptists and Methodists all manage to do
it Why can’t the Anglicans? Streeton
says “the desire to be stlf-supporting is
very strong among the churchmen of Rock-
hampton, but it is not possible to realise
it in its entirety.” Why not? They can
if they will put their fingers as deeply in
their pockets as the people of other de-
nominations. The Bishop of Stepney, by
the way, advocated an exportation of super-
fluous London curates to Bananaland. He
thought they should be sent in gratitude
for the gallantry of the Australian soldiers
at Gallipoli and the “splendid speeches of
Mr. Hughes in London.” Heaven help
you! Heavily burdened with war and
other debt you ask for somebody to make
two loaves ,of bread grow wffiere none grew
before, and instead they propose to send
you somebody who will now proceed to take
lip the offertory.
In a little article on feather-boning and
the use of starch, a fashion-writer has
broken the news that “the day of the stif-
fened skirt is undoubtedly at hand, and a
cachet of the smartest style of skirt rs the
‘whisk’ which the wearer of full flounces
is able to give as she turns. Stiffened
skirts mean that starch will again be neces-
sary.” And they mean, too, that life is
presently going to be a much less simple
matter for Clarence, sitting in the dark-
ened front-parlor with his crackling, inac-
cessible Clara, qnd trying to be truly affec-
tionate in a decorous and noiseless manner.
The All British Society, of Perth —the
crowd which raved at Jack Scaddan be-
cause he told them that if they wished to
hound out of the public service all persons
of German descent they had better start
with King George—struck a snag the other
day. In their “All British Newspaper,”
under the heading “German School
Teachers,” they published a list of teachers
in' W.A. State schools wffiose names were
alleged to suggest a German .origin, and
called upon the Minister for Education to deal
with them. One teacher in the list, named
Shugg, fired in a writ for libel. At the
trial he proved that Shugg was an old
Cornish name, and that he hailed from the
western duchy. The All Britishers claimed
that if it wasn’t a German name, it ought
to be; but an unsympathetic jury said the
libel was worth £75 to Shugg and Heaven
only knows how much to Costs.
Melbourne’s “queen of the carnival” con-
flict goes on amid an alarming hubbub of
meowing and scratching, with hardly a purr
to be heard above the uproar. Even the
pleasant noises which arose when Sol
Green handed in a million votes or so in
the name of Queen Healy, of the Federated
Books, were drowned in a sudden storm of
squalls and hisses as the barrackers for the
rival “queens” expressed their variegated
opinions of the matter. Old friends of a
month’s standing are sending one another
piles of tickets for sale. The morning billy
and bacon are accompanied by sheaves of
letters imploring assistance. Ting !—the
telephone. “Mrs. Canberra-Smith speak-
ing. Oh, Mrs. Sockdolager, would you
make a pair with Mrs. Hogget Boggs to
sell tickets on the Block this afternoon for
the Queen of the Pastoralists —Mrs. Mad-
dox-Paddox, y’ know?” When the baker
calls with the daily loaf, he leaves a leaflet
with the bread upon the doormat; and the
effusive Duchess of the Private Bar has the
whole lot of “queens” up her sleeve, ready
to produce any one of them at a moment’s
notice. They call it charity.

“E.”: If you don’t want trouble, don’t
lake a chorus girl aeroplaning—you’re bound
to meet somebody who will tell the other
cat. A lieutenant of artillery was roosting
on the top of a French haystack “observ-
ing.” Another officer crawled up, and, with
a word of apology for the intrusion, began
“observing” for his lot. The pair presently
started yarning. First they discovered that
they both hailed from Maoriland ; then they
narrowed it down to Auckland ; finally they
discovered that their mothers’ backyards
adjoined. It’s no good—the old world’s too
small to conceal one live man in.
THE FERRIES SPEAK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There have been complaints of the noise made by
the ferry sirens when a transport is leaving Sydney.
From every point and jetty, from all the little bays,
We’ve borne them morn and evening across the Har-
bor ways-
The brawny fact’ry workers, the men who served in
shops,
The swanking members of a “push,” the Blockite's
seeming fops.
And now they’re at the railings, the transport lies
mid-stream ;
“Good luck to those in khaki,” we shout with all our
steara.
Why are our planks so flimsy? Were we of stronger
build
We might have braved the ocean, our decks with
soldiers filled —•
We who have borne them daily to find their work
and play
Might bear them out to exile, as resolute as they.
Bound for the Great Adventure, led by the war-
sword’s gleam,
Through misted eyes they watch us, and hear our
cheering steam.
0 ye that bore them daily, know ye their manhood’s
dream t
Let loose, let loose all bravely, your soul of prisoned
steam!
x.s.w.
J. E.
Time steals along, dear Rosalie,
No one his course can stay ;
Another varied year has fled,
Again is thy birthday.
Accept this humble gift of love,
On thine eighteenth birthday,
From one who loved thee when a bov,
And loves thee to this day.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
The Premiers’ Conference in Adelaide is
the first big thing of the kind held there
since the Federal Convention of 1897. It
has set people with memories—Fred Johns
of course at the head of them—reminiscing
about that great gathering. Elliott Lewis
(Tas.) and Peacock (Vic.) were the only
two officially delegated to both, but of the
S.A. team of 1897 Judge Gordon and J. H.
Howe were both present at last week’s
functions, and Barton (leader of the Con-
vention) and Isaacs looked in as High Court
Judges.
The proudest man in Melbourne these
Sundays is “Chummie” Fleming, the tame
anarchist, who discovered the Yarra Bank
some 30-odd years ago, in company with
about a dozen others, including the Rev.
“Polly” Hopkins. Anyone in search of
entertainment may now take L’is choice of
some 14 different shoivs, including the
1.W.W., Australasian Socialist Party (non-
political), Victorian Socialist Party (politi-
cal), Women’s Peace Army, Freedom
League, Non-Conscription Fellowship, Re-
cruiting Sergeants, two or three religious
cranks, a couple of evangelists, a chemist
who talks on health, and a peanut monarrh.
On a recent Sunday the Bank gave change
to 10.000 people, a dozen foot police and
six troopers. When “Chummie” surveys
this lot from his own particular soap-box,
the front of his back sticks out with the
pride of a pioneer showman. He says so
himself.
11. A. Nichols, member of the Tasmanian
House of Dodery, has the luck of Jonah’s
whale. Five times has he run a dead-heat
for some public position, and every time
he has lost the prize. He has had it both
ways, too. Four out of the five times the
result has been decided by drawing lots,
and every time he has pulled the wrong
straw. The other day he had a run for a
job 'on the Parliamentary Public Works
Committee. It was settled by the casting
vote of the President, and again the other
name of Nichols was Mud.
Dr. Norris, who preceded Dr. Robertson
as chairman of the Vic. Board of Health
and was after-
wards Common-
wealth Director of
Quarantine, left
Australia for Eng-
land in connection
with immigration
matters shortly
before the war be-
gan. After the
slaughter com-
menced Dr. Norris
helped to organise
the Harefield Park
Hospital; then his
special abilities
•were recognised by
the Rockefeller
Institute at New
York, with the re-
sult that he is
now on a scientific
mission to China
and the neighbor-
hood, at a salary
which makes his
late Health and
Quarantine job
look like a weevil.
BOYNTON,
Director of
Supplies and
Transport,
Federal Railways.
Corporal Fern, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), is now
an inmate of an English hospital, having
been invalided from the Western Front
almost as soon as he got there.
J. B. A. Reed, Supt. of Stores in the
N. S. Wales Public Works Dept., has been
dismissed as a result of the Petrol Commis-
sion. The Commissioner found that Reed
was not guilty of improper practices; but
he knew there was a bad smell and didn’t
report it. He goes out under clause 65 of
the Act, which places every public servant
absolutely in the power of his Minister with-
out appeal. Few civil servants are aware
of this volcano until it blows up under their
feet. Four eruptions have taken place
while the present Labor Government has
been in office—all in the Works Dept.
John Story Jamieson (The Bulletin’s
“Calico Jimmy” and a great rag-trader of
his day) died last week at 84. A man who
has known York-street (the Flindersdane of
Sydney) for 40 years, still chuckles over
the way he bustled into Jimmy’s office in
the misty long ago and confidently applied
for the job of second haberdasher to the firm
of Prince, Ogg and Co. “What salary d’ye
want?” growled the boss from the other
side of his desk. “Well, yours is a good
firm,” chirped the youngster—“l’d be willing
to take £175 to begin with.” “Are you get-
ing that 11010?“ breathed Jimmy. “Yes, sir!”
“Well.” snapped Calico James, “you take
my advice and stick to it —I don’t pay my
heads of departments that much!” Rag-trade
salaries have moved up since then; but so
has the price of steak.
Died the other day, at Ulverstone (Tas.),
Mrs. Crawford, one of the survivors of an
interesting experiment. She and hey' hus-
band, Colonel Crawford, were amongst the
first Anglo-Indians to settle on the
North-west Coast. They planned to work
up a big Anglo-Indian settlement at Castra,
so called because it was expected that the
settlers would be mostly from the Indian
Army camps. It never came to much, for
most of those who did emigrate got tired of
the scrub. But Mrs. Crawford stuck to it,
and was 93 when she died.
“Cocky” Bennett, said to be 120, and
probably Australia’s oldest inhabitant, died
the other day at the Woolpack Inn, Canter-
bury (N.S.W.). For many years he lived
wnth Mrs. Sarah Bennett, at Tom Ugly’s
Point, but w-ent over to that lady’s nephew
when she retired from business. “Cocky.”
who was a sulphur-crested Australian par-
rot, well known to tourists from all the
world, had for many years past owned not a
feather. Still he was merry and bright,
and to the end cheerfully swore that he’d
fly yet.
Toko Rua, the long-haired Maori “pro-
phet’s” son, was a fine example of the old
tamatane stock. At the arrest of his dad’s
crowd recently he was wounded twice
through the upper part of the body; then
a bullet almost took his arm off at the
elbow. He didn’t yell for the Red Cross,
but just dropped the rifle, took up the re-
volver and fired through the stockade fence
until he dropped from loss of blood.
William Pile, who died at Glenelg last
week, at 75, was S. Australia’s nearest
approach to the typical sporting English
squire. He was known to his intimates as
“The Baron.” The Pile brothers _ were
notable Darling squatters from 1850 till the
end-of-the-century droughts, but lived mostly
in Adelaide. William was a committeeman
of the S.A. Jockey Club from its foundation
almost to the end of his life, and had many
good stories of the old days of “good” living
and high play. He had many local suc-
cesses on the turf, and won an Australian
Cup with First Water. The Assyrian's
Melbourne Cup should also have been his,
but in a moment of characteristic impati-
ence he had sold the S.A. Derby winner be-
fore the big event came on.

A noted Maoiilander of a bygone age,
Kov. William Gittos, has passed on at 87.
lie was a Durham man, who reached Auck-
land in 1841, and was present at the battle
of Okaihau in 1845. Hater he joined the
Methodists and missioned the Maoris for 60
years. Most of his work was Kaipara
and in Waikato, where he did much to
modify the native disaffection.

nian in Britain

John Tebbutt:, Australia’s oldest astrono-
mer. began to be 83 last week. Born at
Windsor (N.S.W.) on May 25. 1834, he
has spent his whole life in the old Hawkes-
bury township. At 19 he began star-gazing
with a small marine glass, which he still
keeps on a pension. Nine years later he
bought his first refracting telescope, and
iii 1801 built a modest observatory to keep
it in. Two equatorials and a transit instru-
ment followed, and
are now housed in a
b r i c k observatory,
built in 1879, and
recognised as one of
tlio world’s important
stations. He was the
first to discover the
two biggest comets
which came along last
century—in 1861 and
1881 respectively—
and has detected
dozens of minor ones
which tried to slink
past the earth unseen.
In 1862, after he had
bagged his first big
comet, the Ma Stab 1
asked him to become
its Government Astro-
nomer, but he pre-
ferred to keep his in-
dependence as a pri-
vate worker. When
he pleases, he can
wear the Jackson
Gwildt medal of the
Royal Astronomical
Society of London,
which that body, of
which he has been a
Fellow since 1873,
presented to him in
1905 for services to
astronomy extending
even then over nearly
50 years. Comets and
planets keep late
hours, which don t
suit an octogenarian,
and Tebbutt has not
spent many nights at
his telescopes this
last year or two, but
he still personally at-
tends to the meteoro-
logical records which
he lias been keeping
for over half a cen-
tury, and- the other
dav he published a
further volume giving
the results' of _ his
observations of wind and weather at Vine
sor for the past 17 years.

HUM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
When he was in Gallipoli he sent his soldier ser-
vant to the quartermaster for his issue of rum, but
the answer was sent back that no provision had
been made for chaplains. He replied, “Well, I want
to be put on the same footing as soldiers whilst in
the trenches. The Turks make no distinction with
me, so why should 3'ou ?” After that his issue of
rum was sent along regularly. —Dean Talbot.
The warships lay in the sapphire bay,
Their big guns smoked and roared ;
And in that hell of shrieking shell
The cups of wrath were poured ;
Yet none might fear that foe anear —
For Death must surely come —
But one might dread he should be dead
Ere they had served his rum.
Tea for the parson,
Sugar for the queen,
Muffins for the curate —
Such have always been.
Beefsteak for the boxer,
For the horse a drench,
And rum for the soldier
A-fighting in the trench.
By sap and trench the acid stench
Of friend and foeman dead ;
And through the night the roaring light
Of shell that burst o’erhead ;
By day the flies that raw the eyes,
The nerves unsheathed and numb;
The mouth of dust that has one lust —
And that a tot of rum.
Water for the Baptist,
Tripe for aldermen,
Titles for the <LP.,
Lions for the den.
Rye for the Yankee,
AVhisky for the Scot;
But for Australian soldiers
The good rum in the tot.
The soldier who did not imbrue
In blood, but helped in prayer—-
Who turned the wheel, and burned the stick,
And taught the josses there —-
By diction free and rule of three
He proved to the canteen
That he who fights Old Nick o’ nights
Is soldier, though a dean.
Beer for the brewer,
And for the curate buns,
Bromide for the wowser,
And Epsom salts for Huns.
Callan Park for Tories,
Grey top-boots for a wench,
And rum for the soldier
A-fighting in the trench.
VICTORIA.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.

The £116,684 is made up this way:—
1914. 1915. Increase.
Formal reserves.. ..£61,565 £88,218 £26,653
Current risks reserve.. 25,858 28,466 2,608
£87,423 £116,684 £29,261
To start this office the Government provided
for the issue of £lOO,OOO worth of deben-
tures ; but it actually called for only £2OOO,
and that little lot it paid back years ago.
So, on a capital of £2OOO, which has been
returned to the proprietors, the latter have
in 10 years made a profit of, say, £88,218;
and in that reckoning the premiums put
aside to provide for claims under unexpired
policies are not regarded as profit.
The figures are quite attractive enough
to make you want to go out and start a fire
office. But perhaps you won’t be quite so
successful. This office had the business of
the Government behind it, to begin with;
and Maoriland, perhaps because it hasn’t
had any Labor Government to block them,
has an extraordinary number of very im-
portant Socialistic businesses. For instance,
it has a big Public Trust Office, possibly the
largest mortgagee in Maoriland; a Life
Insurance office; a State Coal department,
with numerous retail agencies; and a large
interest in the principal bank in the coun-
try, the chairman of directors being origin-
ally the State’s nominee. And all up and
down the land you will hear the puffiest old
sheep-raisers cursing Private Enterprise
and demanding that, for instance, the State
shall run boats between Wellington and
Lyttelton. So this office started under
exceptionally favorable auspices—-and all
the more favorable because the private cos.
were just then in bad odor.
But that wasn’t altogether the fault of
the cos. The fact is that for years what
is called the moral hazard was exceptionally
serious in Maoriland. In plain English,
Maoriland had a bad habit of having a fire
when things were dull. Things have been
the reverse of dull during the past few'
years, and especially during the past 12
months. It may or it may not be cause
and effect; anyhow, the loss experience of
this office has been unusually good during
these prosperous years, and most unusually
good during the last year of most unusual
prosperity. That it was a bogus prosperity
which will be followed by heavy taxation
and hax-d times isn’t reflected in the figures.
Of the year’s revenue of £76,299 the pre-
miums supplied £71,166; so the losses were
only 38.6 p.c. of the premiums and the
exes, were a remarkably moderate 27.5 p.c.
The reserves, w'hich are the total funds of
the office, are equal to 164 p.c. of the year’s
premiums; and that gives this office about
the same strength, on paper, as the average
of English cos., but much less than the best
Australian and Maoriland cos. The Act
contemplates the payment of bonuses to
policy-holders who haven’t burned up their
premiums; and there is some talk of soon
giving these bonuses a start. But it is
early days yet. Much better to wait till the
office has reserves equal to at least 200 p.c.
of the year’s premiums: even then it will
have less funds actually in hand, in propor-
tion to its risks, than most of its local
competitors. It is all the more important
to go cautiously because the past few years
will probably be found to be quite mislead-
ing. They represent the swing of the
pendulum towards champagne and chicken.
An insurance office, above all others, must
be prepared for the rebound to husks and
headache.

The Sun Newspaper, Ltd. (Sydney),
which bought the assets of the Australian
Newspaper Co. in March, 1910, for £28,000,
or £3132 less than they were then alleged to
lie w'orth

Don’t know' how much they wrote off this
year against depreciation. Previous year
£2OOO was taken out of revenue to provide
for wear and tear; and presuming that a
fair charge has been made each year, net
profits for the past four periods have been
as followslol3. £2293; 1914, £9570;
1915, £10,836; 1916, £12,760. If the
figures are like that after making a uniform
percentage charge on plant and machinery
to cover depreciation, the shareholders
wouldn’t be overdoing it if they asked
everybody concerned to come across the
road. What the investor neeus to poke his
inquisitive nose into is this matter of de-
preciation. Plant and machinery stand in
the books at £61,553. Some will last longer
than the rest; but it. is a safe thing to say
that in 20 years practically everything has.
to ; be made new. That means, besides_doing
odd repair jobs, putting by at least £50,000
of gross profit in the 20 years to replace the
old stuff—£2soo a year. The co. lias a
plant and machinery reserve, which it has
built partly out of share premiums and
partly out of profits (shown in the table
as written off). In the past year nothing
has been added to that reserve; but the
plant and machinery item has been reduced
by £2645, so it seems probable that depre-
ciation is being provided for. Still, as men-
tioned before, it is a thing the accounts
don’t tell a definite story about; and it is
a thing the investor must have a definite
story about before he can be dead sure
about the profits. The only other thing he
can do is to size up the directors and de-
cide whether he will swallow their bare
word. In this case he probably will.
But there is no shadow of doubt about
one thing: the progress made by the busi-
ness has been wonderfully good. The Her-
ald Co., which owns the Melbourne even-
ing paper, paid £198.000 for goodwill. That
meant a capital of £250,000. The Sun
hasn’t a penny of its capital represented by
goodwill. What the Herald Co. paid
£198,000 for the Sun Co. has got for no-
thing through the energy and skill of a few
people, backed, no doubt, by a bit of luck
in being born at the right time; and that
wasn’t all luck either. Its own capital is
£122,050, and it owes £27,490. On the
other side it has freeholds £62,870; stocks
(at cost, and no doubt worth much more,
as paper has been tearing along from £lO
to £4O a ton), £39,252; liquid assets,
£19,601. This table shows the movements of
these items: —

Under normal conditions and with reason-
able economy the Sun ought presently to
make a profit of £25,000 a year; and con-
sidering that the bulk of the share capital
is represented by solid, marketable assets,
this is a handsome prospect.

A paragraph printed some time ago ex-
plained what had happened: “The directors
relied for the correctness of the amounts
alleged to be owing by land buyers [from
the Haymarket Co.] on the amounts appear-
ing in the balance-sheets issued by the Hay-
market Co., and on the certificates of that
co.’s auditors that such balance-sheets were
true statements of the affairs and position
of the co.” Of course the auditors didn’t
certify anything of the kind. The wise
auditor never does. What Pratt and Pratt,
for years the auditors to this concern, did
certify was that, “as disclosed by the books,
the balance-sheet is a true statement of the
affairs and position of the co,” Which is
a different thing altogether.
But if the published accounts of the Hay-
market misled this co., its own accounts are
still less likely to be of serious value to its
shareholders. The assets are as follows :
150,000 shares in Arthur Rickard & Co. Ltd. £150,000
Balance P. & L. Account A. R. & Co., Ltd. 23,448
44,248 shares in Haymarket . . .. 84,964
A. R. & Co., Ltd., current account . . 35,8*24
Bank 9
Total .. £294,245
Now, obviously until you know the position
of Arthur Rickard and Co., Ltd., you know
nothing that is worth knowing about this
Extended Co.; and the accounts of A. R.
and Co., Ltd., are not given to the public.
As to the Haymarket, there are 44,903
shares, and this co. owns 44,248 of them.
Whether it has paid any dividends or not
this co. doesn’t say. The Haymarket Co.
itself, however, lately issued accounts, from
which it appears that, after writing off the
buyers’ balances which shouldn’t have
figured in the books at all, the old reserves
were all absorbed and the P. and L. balance
was £3584 on the wrong side. No dividend
was therefore paid. The whole business is
complicated and unsatisfactory. There
should be one co., not three; and then in-
vestors might possibly be able to get some
general ideas about the concern. For in-
stance, from the accounts of this A. R. and
Co Ex.. Ltd., you only know that 44.248
shares (out of 44,903) in the Haymarket
are valued at £84,964. You don’t know
that that Co.’s assets carry a debt of over
£115,000. In other words, the assets are
covered by a first mortgage to certain credi-
tors, to whom the eo. owes £115,000 ; what
A. R. and Co. Extended owns is merely a
sort of equity of redemption. What Arthur
Rickard and Co., Ltd., owes this paper has
no idea. And what the time-payment land
business in Sydney is going to feel like
three years hence this paper wouldn’t care
to guess. * * *

For six months after the war started it
looked as though Australian wool-growers
were in for a shocking time; and, as growers
who usually sold in Australia were sending
their clips to London to catch the market
whenever it might have a momentarj’ spurt,
it looked as though local brokers might do
still worse and just about starve. Then,
suddenly, the tide turned, and, as the Co-op.
directors say, “although faulty lines have
been in relatively poor demand, growers
have every reason to congratulate themselves
on the prices which have been realised, tak-
ing the clip as a whole.” But as against
that there was the drought and the shrunken
clip, so it isn’t likely that the co. would
have shown so good a profit if it hadn’t been
helped by the previous year’s caiTy over. The
co. had on hand on April 1, 1915, 14,720
bales, which was 12,500 bales more than
the previous year started with. So, as a
certain amount of money had been spent on
that 14,720 bales without return, the past
year’s profits were inflated at the expense
of those for the 12 months ended March,
1915.
The commission on wool, produce and
grain for the past year is, by the way, sub-
ject to the now-usual rebate of 30 p.c.; and
that reduces the actual selling charge in the
case of wool to 2 p.c. The existence of a
co. of this co-operative type, from which the
shareholders are content to draw a very
moderate dividend, returning the balance to
the co-operators, is doing a good deal to save
the trade from State competition. In
Queensland there has for some time past
been a demand for Government action, especi-
ally on behalf of the small grower. And
the State will probably do something, though
the chances are that the result would be far
more satisfactory if it encouraged co-opera-
tion. Of that there isn’t one-tenth part
enough in Australia, not only in marketing,
but in buying as well. We badly want the
Maorilanders over here to teach us some of
their lessons. Maybe it is because the other
brokers are beginning to see that the Co-op.
stands between the trade and State competi-
tion that there isn’t quite the same bitter
feeling towards this co. as there was a while
ago. In fact there is a tendency to care-
fully watch some of those in the selling
brokers’ ring rather than the Co.-op., which
is outside it.
This co. has a capital of £40,000, and its
accumulated profits amount now to £37,563;
and practically the whole of it has been put
into the stores at Pyrmont (£71,357) and
odds and ends of furniture and petty cash.
To finance the business —always some ad-
vances have to be made —the co. is indebted
to “sundry creditors” to the extent of
£96,309. What these sundry creditors are
this paper doesn’t know, and the accounts
would be all the better if they told. Just
now there seems to be special need to empha-
sise this: that the nature of a co.’s liabilities
has proved quite as important in times of
financial stress as the nature of its assets.
In the days of the bank smashes there
w quid n’t have been any smashes to speak of
if the cos. had borrowed wisely. They lent
foolishly, too; but it was the imnrudent
borrowing that was at the root of all the
rottenness.

It is a depressing document that the direc-
tors issue. All that they can comfort the
shareholders with is that the loss is less
than the previous year’s. And there is this
sting in the tail: “Meanwhile the directors
announce that it has been found necessary to
make a call of 10s. per share, payable in
four instalments, the first to be made on
July 1.” Probably the mortgage is the
trouble: the freehold (book value £92,500)
has a plaster of £52.000 on it. There looks
to be an ample margin there ; but these are
times when a lender is liable to want to
know what his security will produce. You
might have a herd of hippopotami worth
allegedly £100.000: bqt any sane money-
lender would consider (1) that the beasts
would have to be fed and (2) that they
probably wouldn’t earn tuppence a week.
The Grand is certainly not a very produetiv,.
beast. Though the average dividend for Hi!
past 10 years has been less than 21 n
the lay-by has been very small: ~ 2 ''"

The gross profit from the business last vear
was £7(51 : in the previous year there Va«
a loss of £220. That’s no good to a mort-
gagee. The whole position so far as these
first-class residential hotels are concerned
calls for review. Costs have raced ahead
during the last few years—both labor costs
and the food bill —and it is a very serious
question whether the “guests” have been
paying enough.

This is a nice little thing in the dental-
supply business; and apparently the mili-
tary idea that we may chew our way to
victory has been responsible for something
extra in trade during the past couple of
years. No doubt, also, the enhanced price
of dental goods has meant increased pro-
fits. The present capital is £29,431 and
£9756 of that was transferred from reserves
six years ago. The return is thus a bit
better than 10 p.c. to those who were then
shareholders at par; and there was enough
put by last year to pay another 6 p.c. on
the old capital. While the return is thus
good the finances cause no anxiety. Total
liabilities are under £2OOO, while assets
apart from stock amount to over £25,500,
including deposits £7876, severely written
down premises £4500, and book debts £8059.
For a trading concern the security for the
principal is thus quite exceptional.

Aladdin may have had a very wonderful lamp,
but we have our doubts. Anyhow if it was true,
then the lamp must have been a Mazda. There are
no doubts about a Mazda Lamp, and it is within
reach of all. Its light combines durability and
brilliancy, together with a caressing, soothing soft-
ness devoid of all glare, and clear as the light of
day.— Advt.
ALUMINOFERRIC is used by the Sydney Water
Board, New South Wales Public Water Supply Depart-
ment, and many country Water Boards and graziers
for precipitating impurities and clarifying water in
reservoirs, cattle dams, etc. It is simply thrown
into the reservoir in the form of a solid cake, or
dissolved and added as a liquid. In three hours’
time the most discolored water will be as clear as
crystal. One pound will clear over 2000 gallons of
water. It is exceedingly cheap and quite harmless.
Sole agents: William Adams & Company Limited,
Sydney, Melbourne, Brisbane and Perth.— Advt.

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yards
of road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yard, and the cost of the spalls per cub. yard aver-
aged 2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub.
yard, broken and spread. Their last contract under
the handbreaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per
cub. yard stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is. ;
total, 7s. 3d., as against 4s. 10d.- — a saving of 2s. sd.
per cub. yard. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stonecrusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers. — Advt. [2

Vislok is a single, self-contained lock nut, consist-
ing of two sections hydraulically forced together.
Vislok has a solid, continuous thread throughout, and
when locked gives a regular and positive hold on the
thread of the bolt. Vislok is so constructed that the
energy of the working load and vibratory force
exerted upon it tend to increase the locking action.
Vislok can be used as an ordinary nut, and is easily
applied in confined spaces. Vislok will not injure
the bolt-thread, and is quickly locked or unlocked
with an ordinary spanner. At the present time
Vislok is proving its usefulness on the Government
Railways and at the Cockatoo and the Naval Dock
Yards. Agents: B. J. Ball, Limited, Sydney and
Brisbane; Elder, Smith and Co., Melbourne and
Adelaide; Elder, Shenton and Co., Fremantle and
Perth; Colin J. Shields, Launceston.— Advt.

The other day four or five reputable Syd-
ney men were hit heavily by a Judge who
declared that, as directors, they had shame-
fully neglected their duties. It was about
time somebody was made to sit up. But if
that was a bad case, what do you think
of this extract from the directors’ report of
the Ilaymarket Permanent Land, Building
and Investment Co.? —
Your directors [they are new directors, of course]
regret to have to inform you that, some months
since, a special audit of the eo.’s books disclosed the
tact that the assets of the co. had been overstated
ii) the last published balance-sheet to the extent of
A 20,674. For a number of years past this co. has
been carrying on its books “secured buyers’ bal-
ances” in'the total above stated, which should long
since have been written off, as the sales in respect
of which the balances were alleged to have been
owing had lapsed, and the land resold to other pur-
chasers.
These were “secured buyers’ balances,”
according to the accounts of this co., sum-
maries of which were issued by the direc-
tors! Doesn’t it matter what sort of
figures or statements men put their names
to in N. S. Wales? Is there no guardian
of financial morality? Is there no body to
protect the investor?
* * * *
The old Lucknow mine, near Orange
(N.S.W.), is to be given another run, this
time by L. Edgley, storekeeper, of Bathurst.
T’he oid battery has been disintegrated and
distributed around the State —10 head to
Spfala, 10 head to Dad’s Millions, 10 head
to Breakfast Creek —-so Edgley proposes to
cart another 10 head from somewhere or
other and re-erect. It is a good illustration
of the remarkable manner in which one
crowd drags machinery away from one mine
to another, while another crowd drags
machinery from another mine to the first
mine and puts it up again. If the Lucknow
proves payable again it will do better than
the shows that hopefully installed the vari-
ous parts of its old battery. At any rate,
Edgley knows the game pretty well,
having owned shows at Swallow’s Nest,
Caloola Creek and elsewhere, and a private
proprietor with capital and experience and
grit may make a do of a proposition that
will beat a company.
* * * *
The idea of establishing copper smelting
and refining works at Townsville or Bowen
is up again for consideration. The Bowen
coalfield is an attraction, although it has
yet to be proved that coke suitable for smelt-
ing can be made there. Cheap coal means
cheap electricity, and that begins to be an
important matter. Queensland is so rich
in minerals that it certainly deserves better
smelting facilities somewhere.

- Mount Elliott owns a big interest in the
Dobbin group, Cloncurry (Q-). and is direct-
ing most of the prospecting and development.
At the 200 ft. level the Dobbin itself is show-
ing an ore body 10ft. wide, worth 10 p.c.
copper, which looks like improving as it is
driven on north. Beyond is the King VV il-
liam, which is owned by Mt. Elliott; and
further on is the Orphan, which was recently
acquired by Mt. Cuthbert on the recommen-
dation of manager Powell. The King Wil-
liam at 75ft. has 6ft. of 15 p.c. ore.

035 hours for only 6 tons 13cwt. of black
tin. The average value of the wash was
only 0.611 b. against 6|d. for costs. But
even if the tin had been produced for nothing
there wasn’t enough to pay the bill for inter-
est and prospecting (£1681). Recovering
11b. or so of black tin at a cost of 6d. is
simply hopeless, so the management intends
to confine operations to the “contact” area
which contains the best values. The latest
return is 30cwt. for a run of 119 hours,
which is more hopeful than six tons in six
months.
J-P-C. : U i» amimag how little use
Queensland has made ofits coal* That “big
seam , near Ma ckay,“ to which The Bulle-
tin lately referred, has been known for over
40 years. The first seam was discovered as
far back as 1873, in a gully near Bu Creek,
and several loads were taken away and tried,
with excellent results. Later Dr. Jack
reported: “A gratifying and promising
feature is the absence of any intrusive or
igneous rock in the district.” Government
analyses gave volatile hydrocarbon, 8.71;
fixed carbon, 84.74; ash, 3.56. Two or three
years ago a bore was put down a few miles
further up the creek, and at a depth of 26ft.
a seam of very clean semi-anthracite coal
was struck and proved 27ft. ' thick, with
another of Bft. below. This coal should be
not only excellent for coke-making, but
invaluable for navy use.
The mostly-Sydney people interested in
the Ida Bay (Tas.) cement proposition
recently offered the whole show to the Tas-
manian Government. The Ministry side-
stepped the question by referring it to Gov-
ernment Geologist Twelvetrees, apparently
to see if he will say the same now as he did
last year, when he pronounced it a good
thing. As he has been given a sort of rov-
ing commission to report on any other pos-
sible State cement shows, he has a chance
of putting in quite a lot of time on the job.
Meanwhile the State is going on buying a
good many thousands of tons of cement a
year and scratching round for imported
stuff to fill the bill.
Chairman T. Dunster and his colleague on
the Board, John Nicholson, of the still-water
flotation process, are scattering circulars
concerning the Tasman and Crown Lyell
(Tas.) like autumn leaves. Thinking he
had a soft snap in this show for his process,
Nicholson picked up 234,000 forfeited shares
out of 300,(XX) on the register for each.
When he got into the Board-room feathers
began to fly, and Dunster says he was given
10 minutes to quit. Dunster blared out
defiance, so Nicholson appears with a three-
page reply. Amongst the pleasantries is
one to the effect that Dunster received
10,000 shares at which, if unloaded
afterwards at 6d. to 9d., wasn’t bad business
for him. He adds in large type that he
hasn’t jobbed on the Stock Exchange, and
says he intends to have a free hand in the
Board-room, and that he won’t be hampered
by unsympathetic colleagues. The special
meeting to deal with Dunster promises to
be particularly lively, but of course the man
with the most votes will win. And that is
Nicholson.
After 35 years’ trudging through deep-
lead mines, E. Visick comes forward with a
scheme for tackling a couple of unworked
blocks of the Indigo and Chiltern Valley gut-
ters, He springs this surprise after discov-
ering that the miner can’t be kept out of
the Rutherglen Viticultural College property,
through which the lead curves. As a recom-
mendation for the float Visick points to
John Cock’s Chiltern Valley, the classy
neighbor to the south-east which has paid
£237,000 in divs., and to the pretentious
Great Southern Consols to the west. Visick
estimates 12,000 ft. of lead 500 ft. wide, gold
averaging 80s. per fathom, and profits about
265. per fathom. He has secured the old
Wallace Consols ground as the base of his
operations, so he has a shaft ready for opera-
tions as soon as the £6OOO he requires for
equipment and driving is forthcoming. Fred
Tricks has in hand the job of planting the
30.000 contribs. necessary to start. The
water squeezed into the show is 10,000 paids
of 20s. each, most of w-hich goes to vendor
Alex. Henderson; but Valley
also bags 2500 paids and £750 cash for hand-
ing over its block of 521 acres, in which is
the Wallace Consols shaft. The Rutherglen
district badly needed a mine to keep it alive,
so Treasurer Higgs did not hesitate to
bestow his non-committal blessing on the
modest capitalisation proposed.
The latest clean-up of Cock’s Pioneer.
Eldorado (Vic.), shows what a close thing
the co. is working. The last three runs
.have been as follows : —-
Run. Drift, cub. yds. Gold, oz. Tin, tons.
No. 5 .. 240,000 2732 38
No. 6 . . 100,000 1199 15J
No. 7 .. 167,000 1848 18
With gold at £4 and tin oxide at £lOO per
ton, results work out at about 13d. per cub.
yard. Two-thirds more ground was handled
than in the previous run, and on averages
the co. should have got 150 oz. more gold
and another seven tons of tin oxide. How-
ever, the yield ought to be payable at 13d.
per cub. yard if Moline s costs are at ..he old
level. But that depends on the distance he
is from the face, the percentage of worked-
out ground and whether the old miner left
him a wood-yard to shift in the ground
sluiced. Ahead he ought soon to be swing-
ing out of the range of the mined area.
* * * *
Edna May Consolidated’s figures to March
31 show that the lucky ones so far have been
the vendors who pouched the £ll,OOO tnai
came from the float. Since manager Lam-
bert donned khaki N. S. Stuckey oas been
overseeing things at the mine, with J. T.
Bremner as understudy. They are not lack-
ing in confidence, for they talk of the next
six months’ work establishing the mine on
a payable and permanent basis. The future
depends upon whether the make of stone in
No. 2 and No. 4 shafts extends to No. 5,
and on the possibility of some fresh forma-
tion coming in from under the cement in
the Golden Point claim. The reef now
showing has a measurement of about sft.,
with patches of lOdwt. stuff here and there.
Not much of that grade has been developed,
and at present even a strike at the main
shaft or at No. 5 wouldn’t do more than
help shares to go sky-rocketing.
* * * *
“Oof Bird’’: Some queer newspaper esti-
mates of the value and prospects of
Edna May Central have been dangled before
the public in the West. Thfe latest is that
if the 150 ft. proves as good as the 75ft. level
the mine will begin to rival the Edna May.
Even if the 150 ft. does turn out to be as good
as the 75ft. level the co. cannot hope to put
the tape round more than 35,000 tons, and
the mill average is not likely to exceed 50s.
Allowing £1 per ton for getting the gold,
less than £1 per share'is left. Of course
official assays show the value of the ore to
be much more than 505., but battery returns
will prove it otherwise.' Meanwhile if the
directors are anxious to get at the real value
of the mine and wish the public to know,
too, they should get a first-class engineer to
report on it. Are they game ?
* * * r% ‘ * *
Perth boomsters are cursing the Federal
Treasurer for his refusal to sanction certain
mining flotations. A number are mentioned
as “genuine things,” and Higgs’s veto, they
angrily declare, will have a serious effect
on the West’s gold industry. Well, there
is no reason to suppose that any of the
shows are bursting with gold; and what
metal there is won’t evaporate.
* * * *
Broncho Horseshoe (W.A.) is said to
have got the Federal Treasurer’s consent to
a reconstruction. The co. has hung on for
some time to a group of leases adjacent to
the Great Victoria on the South Yilgarn
field and reckons that with the expenditure
of a fair amount of capital it will become
possessed of a big money-making low-grade
mine. If, however, as a visiting expert
pointed out, only a small amount of capital
is raised, and the bulk of it is collared by
the promoters, nothing but failure can
result.
The Chamber of Mines (W.A.) is trying
hard to get the Kalgoorlie miners to sign a
new wages agreement, so as to obviate the
necessity of asking the Arbitration Court
to butt in. As spokesman for the local
mineowners, the Chamber offers to increase
the surface minimum from 10s. 9d. to
11s. 3d., and the underground from 11s. 2d.
to 11s. Sd. But the offer applies only to
married men or single men with proved
responsibilities, and' for this reason the
unions have turned it down.
* * * *
Associated Northern (W.A.) is busy
opening up the Gimlet at Ora Banda and
slowly working out the adjacent Victorious
leases. The Gimlet shaft is to be sunk
another 100 ft. to ascertain the value in
deeper ground of the two shoots worked at
285 ft. The western shoot assays up to £5
in the winze, or about double the average
of the mine. Everything depends on how
the latter shapes at the next level. The
Victorious has been persevered with down
to 700 ft., where prospects are disappointing.
Some good values were bored through below
this depth, but the shoot is so patchy and
unreliable that it is doubtful if any more
sinking will be donp. The co.’s old show
at Kalgoorlie is likely to snuff out at any
time, while the fate of its Mexican venture
is still unknown. Nobody connected with
the management has been able to get within
sight of it for some time.
* * * *
Sons of Gwalia has sent its first “part
wages and part interest” prospectors to Mt.
Luck, near Linden (W.A.), to look for a
new mine. Another party will probably
be sent later on to see what it can find in
the vicinity of Mt. Ida.
New Chaffers Ltd. (W.A.) has raised the
wind and acquired the Aucland mine, near
Leonora, for £lOOO cash and a few thousand
paid shares. In .the hands -of its late
owners the show lias furnished about 600
tons of £4 ore and it is said to be a mine of
possibilities.
* * * *
New Boddington (WIA.) has had its
name added to the Stock Exchange lists.
Sufficient gold was won during the option
to pay the vendors’ £6OOO. The co. reports
50dwt. ore at 73ft., and divs. are promised
at an early date. The show is one of the
rich specimen variety, ami everything fie*
pends on further development.
Mary Mac (W.A.) is adding to its treat-
ment plant, and hopes shortly to handle
2500 tons a month at a cost of 15s.
Meanwhile preparafions are being made to
open out and drive a level at 500 ft., to get
as many working faces ready as possible.
In the last 2-J years the co. has obtained,
principally over the plates, £26,127, but
shareholders do not appear to have had any
of it. Their turn is still to come.

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, May 29. 191 G.
My Dear Arini, —
Woman reflects the era in her clothes,
.lust now her millinery is built on biplane
lines. Silk bows that look like the wings
of an aeroplane, and hats shaped like the
body of a monoplane, show that the bird-
men's cages are the models of the moment.
Papua’s Governor Murray gets here in
June —a little later than usual —for his
yearly spell from the mosquitoes’ evensong.
Wife and daughter are waiting for him.
Rupert Carrington, junior, who stands
next to the title which Uncle Lincolnshire
will leave behind, has met his best girl at the
parson’s. News of his engagement to the
good-looking Sybil, a daughter of Lord Col-
ville, came this way some time ago. In
doing his bit in France the youngster was so
mishandled that he is likely to carry the
marks to his grave. Father was the elderly
beau with the sweeping moustache —of course
you remember him —who used to adorn the
Block years ago.
Saw it stated somewhere that Auntie
Sam (she’s a mere step-auntie nowadays)
has just discovered the choko. That’s
nothing ! Our G.-G. found it weeks ago. At
a battalion-fund garden party here, a Gen-
tleman Help, in silk hat, spats, etc., was
sent to buy and carry home a small con-
signment of the little vegetable. Whether
he was waylaid and robbed before he reached
the vice-regal chaise I don’t know. If he
dodged the lady brigands the G.-G. had
choko for dinner that night. It’s a delicate
morsel when it’s properly cooked. And
with plenty of ginger it will make jam. In
its life it is tucked away under a glossy
thatch of spreading vine.
The National Council emitted a little mild
thunder under the vice-regal roof the other
afternoon. The Strickland lady sat on the
little dais in the ballroom —the place where
once the Elect in stars and garters used to
watch the Gilded Youth pursue the Butter-
fly in the mazy dance. After Miss Ruby
Board had alluded to the bleak effort to
induce women to tell other women their ages
and other painful fictions in the Council’s
• “War Census” papers, the boyish-looking
Meredith Atkinson gave the company its
meat. A clever address urged economy that
would contribute to military efficiency,
instead of the more miserly quality of thrift
which only helped the individual. Then
came the tug of war. Ruth Beale opened it
with an admirable curtain lecture on the
support of Australian industries. The
remarks had - only one fault —there were
too many of them for a meeting so
thick with embryo lady orators. Mrs.
Hugh Dixson, having cleared her throat,
got into action after this star turn,
and regretted that she had once bought a
German piano. Gentle Rose Scott ambled
peacefully in home paddocks and talked, too,
of employment for our own people. The
tragedy of a lady who had to go out scrub-
bing floors in her nightie with an old wrap
thrown over it, as instanced at this crisis,
was too much for Aide Firth, who fled to
the baronial hall to look for a chaperon.
The Tom Hughes lady, Mrs. George Earp,
Mrs. Laura Luffmau (who snatched 40
winks while Our sex expounded its views)
and Prof. Andy Stuart’s slim missus were
amongst the party. Outside the ballroom a
fierce combat of feathered warriors in a
Moreton Bay fig supplied a chorus to the
feminine corroboree.
Mrs. Prideaux-Brune, who used to be
Miss Garvan, lias presented the world with
a potential fighting man and polo-player.
The small son was born in Cairo. Still an-
other Egyptian-born Australian recently
opened her eyes to the dazzling Cairo sun-
shine. The new girl wall address Eric Bar-
hour (over there with the A.M.( J.) as
Daddy so soon as she masters that wild
animal, the English idiom. And Sydney’s
fine cricketer will, no doubt, reply in suit-
able language.
That restless athletic parson, Stacey
Waddy, is getting into khaki. He goes as a
chaplain to the Front as soon as he can get
inarching orders. In consequence, the old
King’s School, Parramatta, is looking for a
new headmaster. He asked the Council for
leave, but for some unexplained reason it
wasn’t forthcoming.
yTust back from the fringe of Verdun, S.
AY. Thornton had an interesting story to
tell the Red Cross workers the other after-
noon. In Paris, as in London, he visited
the procession of hospitals which follows up
the great war. Beyond Verdun he saw the
wonderful French in their healing work,
and admired the now-perfectly-organised
British Red Cross which runs its own trains
for wounded across France to the Channel
ports. For some months a toiler in
Pharaoh’s sandy vineyard for our own Red
Cross, the traveller emphasised the. occa-
sional weakness of a trio of commissioners.
“It’s better than the former arrangement
of two —particularly where they are both
strong men —hut not so good as one. For
instance, what help is an Australian com-
missioner, say, in Marseilles to the man in
Egypt?” The Cullen lady took the chair
at this chat. Mrs. A. Lee returned thanks
for the lucid Thornton’s address. The mild
acclamation which comes from the smack-
ing together of kid gloves followed.
