A recent English bride. Nora Somerville,
who married Captain Cecil Fox, of H.M.S.
Powerful, is related to the Lloyds, of
N. S. Wales. Charles Lloyd, senior, now
in England, a one-time pastdralist here, be-
longs to a Shropshire family. I don’t know
how they missed Taffy’s Land, but there’s
probably a Llewellwyn somewhere in the
back-centuries. Leslie Wade, the late charm-
ing irrigation engineer, married a Charles
Lloyd daughter. The Fox bride, described
as a girl who can turn her hand to art and
music and her feet to intricate figures'on
the ice, is a Shropshire lamb.
Northern Maoriland received a sad shock
when a cable the other day reported the
death of Enid Hamilton. The only child of
squatter Charlie Elgar, of Fernside. Feather-
ston, she was married a few years back to
Governor Islington’s one-time aide, now
Lieut.-Colonel Gavin Hamilton, of the
Grenadier Guards. Mrs. Hamilton, who
had a rose-tinted existence, had only lately
been joined in England by an old girl-pal,
Miss Monekton —some relation of the comic-
opera Lionel —who was to keep her company
during the strife.
In these strenuous times Eaditli Walker
spends four of her week days flatting at
Craig-nish. Between meals she serves tea
and red cross buns to recruits at Padding-
ton. Others of the gilded grade give help.
The other day a sunburnt soldier-to-be waved
his cup energeticallv in the face of the mil-
lionaire spinster-lady, demanding: “Give us
some more tea, mother!” And “mother”
obediently poured it out just as if she hadn’t
another job to go to.
A. L. Blythe, one of the pillars of the
Amelioration Committee, has givfen himself
a brief holiday—the first since Europe
caught fire. He is spending it in Maoriland.
The Tom Moore concert on Saturday
night wasn’t the success that last year’s
effort proved. Probably the hungry war-
funds have chastened the public desire to
rush Tom’s birthday party. Still the Town
Hall was fairly full, and “the Harp that
Or.ce” was twanged at frequent intervals.
Flavor was given to a thin musical potage
by the first-rate orchestral work of the
schoolgirls from the Convent of Mercy.
Parramatta. Carl Gotch conducted this
beautifully-trained string band. That ever-
green art enthusiast. Dr. Charles McCarthy,
adjusted his specs, to accompany Sid
Macdonald’s warble, “Bendemeer’s Stream.”
set to a McCarthy composition. Winifred
Carter, at the harp, was an agreeable inter-
lude in a wearisome reiteration of popular
airs. Lilian Frost, ut the organ, gave “God
Save,” as Miss Strickland with General
Finn and ’an official tail marched on to the
sub-regal carpet. The encore fiend was
rampant. The most dismal singers were
frantically recalled, and at 10 p.m. the con-
cert threatened to finish up at the end of
the war.
One of the numerous Antill familv—this
time it is Doris, of the Walcha offshoot—
is quite sure she likes khaki best. Her
young man is Lieut. Graham Gilder, son
of the solicitor who catches a morning train
at Pymble. Gilder junior is now on his
way to the innumerable Front.
Again the Moreton Bay fig and the axe.
This time and once more it is a Melbourne
visitor, Curator George Cooper, of the Alex-
andra Gardens, who yearns to grub out some
of the beautiful shade trees in Hyde Park.
Sydney’s long hot summer clamors for leafy
foliage. But the average gardener wants
flower-beds all the time. Yet whoever
heard of perspiring citizens cooling down
under the shade of a pansy? If modern
Eve, a few years hence, wants to make fig-
leaf’kilts. so as to put her dress allowance
into war loans, .she’ll have to travel to
Moreton Bay to find the material.
Walter Lucas, Burns-Philp’s _ coconut
(they don’t spell it with an “a” in Island
circles, my dear) specialist, demonstrates
that neither matrimony nor malaria is
necessarily fatal by celebrating his silver
’moon on June 1. The ’groom was nearly
nailed in German New Guinea on the
break of war, but slipped out at the 59th
second. Two engineer sons took to the
water at the same time. One was on a
collier carrying diamonds for the North
Sea Fleet until March, when a submarine
broke up the floating home. After 48 hours’
boat picnic and two days’ spell ashore sonny
was off to sea again with more coals for
Jellicoe. The baby, a six-foot Australian,
attained his 21st year the other day, and
is now training for service as a sub-looten-
ant in our own navy.
Surgeon Acland O’Hara (his brass
plate has brightened up a front gate on
Bayswater-road for ever so many years)
took in a life-partner a couple of weeks ago
—Alice, the daughter of brother-medico and
man-of-many-arts Charles McCarthy, who
is father also to Maud, the violinist.
Howard Hinton, a Sydney collector, de-
serves the cheers of all art-lovers for pre-
senting two water-colors by the late J. J.
Hilder to Sydney National Gallery, at a
time when Ililders are being jealously
treasured. The Gallery now hangs four
examples of the dead Australian’s genius.

Warrant-officer Peterson, R.A.G.A., said

A former Mosman man, George Wootten,
is getting close up to Jack Dyer’s
record. Jack was a major on Active
Service on his 22nd birthday. George had

There’s a hive of industry in Ash-street,
where the display of pumpkins alone is
worth seeing. It’s the fruit and vegetable
depot which, under the Red Cross banner,
passes on its goods to the returned wounded
and their dependants. On the floor above
there’s a condiment factory to tickle the
palates of the boys. The factory ’ands—
daughters of judges and such like—are
getting an education in the art of making
sauce and pickle and chutney from Mrs.
Jocelyn. That slender lady in a mob cap
is an expert, brought up in a bush home,
where Mother taught the girls how to make
everything except the beef, the mutton and
the weather. Plelp rolls in so fast that the
Ash-street factory will need a building to
itself if the war drifts into 1917.
Stripes are still in—most helpful of de-
signs to the lady who is five feet in her
Louis XIV. heels. Mixtures of olive green
and navy striped shimmer, like the frock
in which Mrs. Jim McMaster dazzled local
society a week or two ago, are considered
It. But the frock-builder who turns out
these blends is a highflyer in the business
or the result is liable to be a shriek.

Artist Ruby Winekler, the Sydney
girl who spent two years or so in
Boston, brings home vivacious anecdotes of
that serious city. “You’ve got-to be von-
rich, or get along as best you can with a
sub-conscious feeling that your hats are too
few and a failure at that. When I went
abroad at sale time to pounce on a millinery
bargain, the Boston friend who went with
me bought three hats for herself. She didn’t
want them—it was just a sort of duty to
herself. Yet she was considered badly' off
according to the local standard, as she only
paid the dollar equivalent of £lO a week
for her home. Money? Boston eats it'
Yet the squalor and poverty of the poor
(there seems to be no medium) makes an
Australian ill. We aren’t acquainted with
poverty as they know it in George Wash-
ington’s country. There, in beautiful Bos-
ton, with its wealthy Amazonian women
is a woeful underworld. In it the people
are so small and shrivelled with want, and
the children so famine-stricken and tiny,
that they seem to belong to a different race.
But while the knowledge of these things
doesn’t offend stately Boston society, color
is considered too “loud” for every-day
wear and therefore to be shuddered at
behind half-drawn blinds. Hence black and
navy are the general garb of the Socially
Correct. The gifted Ruby is settling down
here with father and mother for two years.
A N’York publishing firm, E. B. Dutton &
Co., has commissioned her to illustrate the
Odyssey, and it’s going to take that time
to complete the job.
Ex-Governor Carrington’s youngest
daughter Victoria is putting aside her
widow’s weeds for a new wedding frock.
Iler first husband, Lieut. Nigel Bourke, of
the Coldstream Guards, was killed in the
first dreadful days of the Hun rush in
France. Victoria’s second choice is also a
Guardsman—of the Royal Horse. He is
Major the Hon. (etc.) Weld-Forster.
Miss Brace has bought a poultry farm in
the Regents Park district. It is some-
where behind Granville, and you get there
in a light spring cart. This is the off-sea-
son for eggs, but I understand the Regents
Park hens are not going back on their new
missus.
At the Feminists’ Club the other evening
the ’Murkan Miss Elsie Simpson read Prof.
Irvine’s lecture, as he was unable to deliver
it in person. The Club, which is new and
solemnly banded together to boost Woman,
received the Word with applause. Irvine,
as you know, is the first man to sit in a
chair of Economics in Sydney ’Varsity, and
the U.S. visitor, who is a graduate of Colum-
bia. is his Associate.
A cyclone of foreign tongues sweeps over
hitherto peaceful suburbs. The association
for spreading the French language has a
new rival. A B. Piddington, who has been
interested in the struggle with irregular
Parisian verbs, is lion, boss of the latest
scheme for attacking Spanish vowels.
They melt, of course, like honey in the roof
of the mouth, and are usually accompanied
by a guitar. The indefatigable French
Consul Chayet took part in the preliminary
meeting.
The Women’s Horticultural Society is
looking for a depot. Something central and
not too expensive—-you can guess how easy
the quest is ! They’ll use it as a clearing-
house for the members’ home-grown radishes
and personally-stirred jam and sweets. A
tea-room is to be a side-line.
Vera Cree, a 19-year-old Sydney girl,
hopes soon to give up clerking in tlm City
and leave for England and two years’ free
tuition at the Royal Academy of Music.
The Associated Board gave only one scholar-
ship in Australia for 1915, and Vera Cree’s
mezzo-contralto won it. Sydney hasn t
heard anything of her; but Miss Ada Gee,
her teacher, is full of enthusiasm, and has
built plans for a concert in Sydney and an
early flight to London afterwards.
In a very new house that looks very old,
photo-artist May Moore (she is now Mrs.
Harry Wilkes) and her 6ft.-and-more hus-
band have set up their household gods. It s
in Spit-road, where the view reaches out,
in varying blues, between the Pleads.
Yours affectionately.
VANDORIAN.

MELBOURNE CHATTER
Melbourne, May 29, 1916.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Larry Rentoul was not trying to be either
funny or insulting, but he convulsed an
Empire Day gathering at the Town Hail,
when eulogising Brigadier-General Hughes,
by declaring; “Nobody was surprised when
General Hughes went to the war. for is he
not the husband of Mrs. Eva Hughes?’’
Tony Mason, son of a former Speaker
who died just a year ago, is back in Mel-
bourne after doing the round trip to the war.
Wounded at Gallipoli and patched up at
Alexandria, he left there with a limp for
England, where he took a degree for,. some
special branch of homicide at Bisley. He
is now instructor in machine-gunnery at
Seymour Camp, and his lonely widow-mother
lives quietly at South Yarra, thanking the
Fates that ‘she has her boy within cooee.
The general exodus of men is having its
effect in the fashionable flats. Cliveden is
beginning to advertise its bachelor roosts at
bargain prices.
Instead of penning lines, poet Hugh
McCrae now stands in ’em. He looks taller
than ever in khaki.
Dr. Helen Sexton, who has been working
at a French hospital since the early days of
the war and was long ago made a major, has
been standing up to the collar ever since.
Now word comes that the strenuous woman
is at length on holiday. But that is only
because she was damaged in a street-acci-
dent and can’t walk, much less perform
surgical operations.
Edward Rigby’s lad Harry, who went to
Hendon to try for a job in the R.F.C., has
got his wings and the stars that go there-
with. But the British authorities have now
closed this avenue of army-employment to
would-be fliers from Australia, so a healthier
interest may be taken in aviation at our
own Point Cook. It was time, too. Many
of the bright young men who left for Eng-
land ostensibly to take part in the great
adventure per aeroplane are actually driv-
ing motor-lorries instead of petrolling splen-
didly through the blue, empyrean.
Arthur St. Clair Tisdall. a naval lieu-
tenant who has just been given the v.C.
ten months after his death in action, was
a son of Dr. W. St. Clair Tisdall, a cleric
one-time of Maoriland. The youngster
was a scholar of Trinity College, Cam-
bridge, and Chancellor’s Gold Medallist for
1913 and he wrote some verse which is to
be put into a little book. The \ .C., by the
way, was won in connection with the land-
ing at Gallipoli, when he brought boat-load
after boat-load of wounded back to the ship
under a deadly fire.
Should soldiers’ grass-widows smother
themselves in sackcloth and ashes and hide
away in obscure flatteries, or should they
try to get a reasonable amount of enjoy-
ment out of life and clothes? If a vote isn’t
taken soon to decide the question, some of
the more critical squaws will die of mind-
ing other people’s business. The new. fur
coat of a senior officer’s dashing grass-widow
is now tantalising the gossips; and that is
only a sample case of hundreds. Could the
Arbitration Court be asked for a ruling on
the question?
Guy Madden’s wife —Charlie McEvoy’s
daughter—is just beginning to sit up and
ask for a few particulars of the motor smash
in which she was hurt.
The customary messages of sympathy to
the widow of John Murray reminded the
public of the almost-forgotten existence of
the lady, who dwells, almost a recluse, in. a
lonely house on a high hill between Mit-
cham and Bayswater. A neat garden sur-
rounds the home, but it is Mrs. Murray’s
whim to leave the rest of the 100-acres to
gardener Nature. An overgrown track leads
to the door and the rest is just wild bush.
’Enry Weedon was tipped a while ago as
a “possible” for the mayoral catskins next
year. But lately his stock has slumped,
and William Cabena is now first in the
betting. It was on Cabena’s recommenda-
tion that the council lately extended its
activities in connection with the supply of
electric power ; and generally, though he is
an importer, he has a less narrow political
outlook than the conventional Weedon.
The programme for Empire Day at the
Town Hall was arranged, on behalf of the
Women’s National League, by bright-hued
Mrs. Edward Collins, who used to be Violet
Singleton in the peaceful days when politics
were never mentioned except by the male
members of the family. Young Mrs. Robert
Singleton, Mrs. Collins’s mamma-by-
marriage, belongs to a different political
camp, having a record of considerable
activity with the Women’s Political Asso-
ciation —certainly a brainier affair than the
Irrational League, though it probably
doesn’t do any more in the way of pushing
ahead this country than the more fatheaded
body.
RiveHna Squatter Charles Mills passed
out at his Toorak home last week, after
many months of ill health. Three sons and
two daughters survive him. The eldest son
Ainslie, who married Colonel Tom Price s
only daughter, died a few years back. An-
other son married Melba’s pretty prot£g6e,
Sylvia Ffrench. The younger daughter
married one of Colonel Price’s sons, the
naval Vivian, who died at the Dardanelles.
Photographer Mina Moore and Tainish,
who was on the stage before he became
secretary of the Savage Club, . have entered
into an engagement with a view to matri-
mony.
Rich girl Beryl Atkinson is to become
the wife of Captain Gerard Blitz early in
June.
Jean Knox’s finger (third left) is spark-
ling with a signal lamp placed there by a
soldier-son of the S. Australian house of
Robertson.
Reggie, of the Frank Graham tribe, who
stopped woolsorting to pass compliments,
etc., to the Turks at Gallipoli, and was
finally shrapnelled off the active list, has
resumed civilian clothes and gone off to the
London office of the in which he earns
his crust. Before he went aboard his
steamer the young man presented a half-
hoop to the widow Langdon’s handsome
youngest daughter Marjorie. Poor Mrs.
Langdon is mourning the loss of her only
son, Leslie, who, after being listed as miss-
ing since the day after the confused and
terrible landing, is now written off as
killed in action.
A son of the Lush clan, of Kew, having
put his Western district sheep-run out to
nurse, has gone to one of the fronts. Before
he went he persuaded Vera McCall to say
“Yes”; but as Vera’s father does not
approve of war weddings the orange blos-
soming will happen when Johnny comes
marching home.
St. John Ambulance Association, which in
more peaceful days was led by medicine-man
Springthorpe, is asking for a lifebelt. It
has a debt of £l5OO, mostly caused by the
2234 calls that were answered last year free.
If you’ve got a last leaf in your cheque-
book, and there is no collector clamoring for
Something else on your mat —well, here’s
your chance.
Mrs. Alex. Cameron and Miss Laidman
are secretarying a dramatic outburst in aid
of the Malvern Nursing Division of St.
John. The Australian National Theatre
Co. (from Oakleigh, if you please) is to
appear at Malvern Town Hall (within wav-
ing distance of State Government House)
on Saturday, June 10, at eight o’clock.
There will be two original Australian plays,
“Mother Mine” and the “Moon Child,” the
authors of which are remaining in ambush
for the present. The affair is going to be
stiffly fashionable, with reserved seats at
three shillings.
The past pupils of the Loretta Convent
have dropped their knitting and started to
get in cash for their free kindergarten. Last
week they mixed a tea-party and sale of
gifts, and are now preparing for another
tremendous Verdun effort on June 28.
Adelaide tells All: —
Two Conferences and a visit from the Q.-G. have
given us a nervous headache. We have already
weathered (1) a Government House lunch, (2) a
Parliamentary dinner, (3) an Empire Day demon-
stration, (4) a Mayoral reception, (5) another lunch
at Waterfall Gully, (6) inspection of various camps
and military hospitals, (7) a visit to a school or
two, and (8) the presentation by the Wattle Day
League of some more motor ambulances. I doubt
whether George Reid or Hughes could go at such a
pace for long. A Victoria League meeting, at
which Lady Galway presided and her Excellency
and Professor Darnley Naylor were the speakers,
brought the whirling week to a close. (P.S.: I am
writing this in bed.)
Eugene Alderman, whose orchestra has been worth
many a pound to patriotic funds since war
began, has arranged with local musicians for the
organisation of a Patriotic Orchestra. It will give
three concerts during the winter, one half the pro-
ceeds to go to patriotic funds and the other to the
performers. It is about time something of the
kind was done.
A Rejected Volunteers’ League is to be added to
the list of local societies, at the instance of Corporal
E. C. Evans, who is also treasurer of the
Returned Soldiers’ Association. In the early days
of the war rejected volunteers waxed indignant at
any suggestion that they should be labelled as such;
but when the choice lies between being known as a
shirker or a reject it is a diiferent matter.
Mrs. Walter Hawker and her daughter are back
from England, and the incoming mail has amongst
its passengers W. J. Isbister, K.C., who has been a
Red Cross Commissioner in Egypt for the past six
months.
William Pile, who died at Glenelg at the end of
the week, was the son of the James Pile who came
to the State about 67 years ago and subsequently
took up land on the Darling in partnership with
McKinley, the explorer. At his death the three sons
carried on the properties in a partnership that lasted
till about eleven years ago.
Tba winter lectures have been started at the Art
Gallery. President E. Davies, of the Fine Arts
Committee, kicked off. .
No Bundey poetry prize is to be awarded this
year. Various efforts on the set subject—Matthew
Flinders —were received, but none of them were up
to standard. Next year’s subject is “The Great
War of 1914 and After.” I don’t know why people
want to encourage the manufacture of poetry about
the war. No printer would do it.
E. F. Playford, assistant Chief Draughtsman of
the Lands Titles Office in Adelaide, has been so-
longed by the staff, of which he is the 12th member
to enlist. He goes with the rank of captain, and
is of the same massive build as his father, “Honest
Tcm,” the lately-dead politician whose big boots
and cherries were almost equally well-known to
Adelaide.
Organist Dr. William Price, of Oxford,
has been succeeded at St. John s, the
Toorak- holy of holies, by Nickson, I .R.C.0.,
one of the University Conservatorium s
instructors. Besides being an organist
Nickson is a choirmaster of much patience
and practice, so Toorak should now break
out into spots of melody upon the Sabbath
morn.
Resplendent in white furs, Evelyn Scot-
ney White was welcomed back from the
U.S.A. at the Austral Salon. Her Yankee
husband is also a singer. Likewise he is
large, and, I should imagine, a joy for ever
to his tailor.
A musical matron of considerable weight
and wealth pants to play Mimi in
“Boh&me” ; so she has hired the new Reper-
tory Theatre on off nights for family-party
rehearsals, and the unfinished building
creaks to the dying agonies of the well-
nourished singer.
At the opening of the new Music Club,
Melba made a fighting speech to the effect
that the institution was new and the only
one of its kind in Australia. It had no
connection with other clubs hereabout, and
was determined to do or burst in pursuit of
the highest musical ideals. Then the party
settled down peacefully to coffee, cakes and
chatter, when it shpuld have quaffed wine
and danced the carmagnole around bonfires
made of the worst music.
At the Selinsky recital in the Auditorium
on Saturday the. young man whom Melba
had blessed* in advance stood on a delicate
carpet of vieux-roses quite unsuited to his
style of beauty. The Russian looks every
inch a —ski, but with no trace of the Jew-
ish nose of many —skis. He is a rugged-
faced, brown-skinned creature with a mop
of thick-curled hair like the fleece of a
black merino. He is unduly solemn and
leaves all the laughing to his violin. Elsie
Treweek, who filled in intervals with song,
wore a flame-colored dress which made me
wonder all the more at the carpet of vieux-
roses. The flame-colored crepe was ankle
length and fur-edged. Several inches of
flame-silk stocking, atop of flame-red satin
shoes, gleamed below the fur hem. Her exit
showed a marvellous obi-sash of Allied flags,
and she wore a ferocious bunch of red and
white tulle bon-bons in her hair. She was
determined to brighten up the fog-grey a
bit.
The Bj-itish Bull Dog Club of Victoria
holds its annual bark at the Guildhall pn
Friday and Saturday, June 2 and 3. Chief
Justice Madden will make a speech, and
Dorothy Brunton will act as judge, with the
expert assistance of some man who really
knows what dogs should be. A fifth of the
“gate” goes to the Fund for Sick and
Wounded Soldiers.
The woman dispenser is here to stay all
right. The Victorian chemist has to put
in a year at the College of Pharmacy, with
a four years’ apprenticeship and a qualify-
ing exam, at the end thereof, so dispensing
isn’t a pursuit which is likely to attract any
except bright misses. But the wages beat
those of most women’s occupations outside
the learned professions and Government em-
ployment, while there is always a chance
of striking a sov. a day as chemist to a
manufacturing concern. Muriel Archer is
one of the latest recruits in Melbourne. She
came from the tall timber of Gippsland, and
is fresh from the tutelage of dispenser
Ethel Leon, of the Queen Victoria Hospital
—which, by the way, is staffed throughout
by women.
The Victorian Artists’ smoke night at the
Albert-street Galleries was a laboriously gay
function. Libations of lager from a keg
and melody from an instrumental trio didn’t
add much sparkle. Many honest people who
do not paint put on what they imagined to
be the uniform of art, namely wide sash-
ties and velvet jackets. They hadn’t heard
that modern art is a plainclothes job.
Magaret Baskerville’s blanc-mange cupids
loomed large in the middle of the room and
almost cried aloud for raspberry jam gar-
nishings. Some “fancy-dress” Bacchantes
(it was a mixed smoke-o) wore diamond
combs in their hair as well as vine-leaf
wreaths, and jumped aboard the last tram
convinced that they had been the right sort
of Bohemians.
Conductor Fritz Hart gays that h c
doesn’t know how Trantbam Fryers man
aged to evolve a portrait of him for the
Victorian Artists’ show seeing that hi
never sat for the thing. Perhaps the artist
was The Face at the Window when Frit?
thought he was alone and unobserved.
The old Austral Salon is preparing to
improve its collective mind this winter On
June 2, the learned Enid Derham will‘read
from “Hamlet.” After that Professor Wal
lace will explain how Shakspeare did it
and why he doesn’t do something 0 f the
kind himself. Then Dr. J. P. Wilson will
orate on the “Genesis of the Shakspearian
Society.” Altogether the sort of evening that
makes young people feel as if they had
been sandpapered.
In drapery, according to a commercial
expert, “a better volume is being done than
might be expected at the high prices anv
slackness in sales of men’s apparel being
frequently offset by liberal expenditure on
the part of the other sex.” For one thing
there is an epidemic of fur-coat madness’
Much of it is rabbit-fur, sold as conev
ermine or spotted dingbat, at from six to
sixteen guineas the garment. As a good
rabbit coat can be dyed and made at a
cost of about three sovs., even the six-
guinea cloak shows a clear profit of 100
per cent. The time-payment shark helps
to dispose of the goods; and some of the
“best” shops now tout for that kind of
trade. The customer goes in to spend ten
guineas. “This is a very nice coat,” declares
the saleslady. “Twenty guineas.” The
customer’s face falls a couple of inches
whereupon the sales-countess lowers her
voice: “Of course, we can arrange terms
if you like this one. This one is twelve
guineas; but you can see the difference for
yourself.” The ten guineas goes as a
deposit—and the firm is all right whatever
happens. In the case of the kitchen maid
and the factory miss -who want something
to match their sweet little faces, and can’t
resist spending their last bit of available
cash on an interest in a fur coat, extended
payments are the very thing. But the
reckoning is liable to be a brutal business.
Old Tom Pockett, curator of the Malvern
Gardens, might have been a rich man if he
had known how to turn his particular hobby
into coin. As things are he simply owns
an international reputation. In some of the
most expensive, exclusive gardens of Britain
and the U.S.A. the name of Pockett stands
for quite a lot. Indeed, had there been such
a thing as copyright in chrysanthemums
Pockett would have been floated into a com-
pany long since. Still, there have been some
rewards. Though an unpolished diamond,
he had the time of his life on his last trip
’Ome, exchanging intimacies on chrys-
anthemums with all kinds of nobility
and gilded gentry, hobnobbing at dinner with
the peerage after viewing the conservatory
and grounds, and engaging in abstruse dis-
cussions of shallow-planting and liquid-
manuring over the nuts and nerve-tonic.
Pockett’s account of that trip, published in
book form, would undoubtedly qualify as a
best-seller in the Court Gossip class.
I have found a job for the Censor when
the war is over —to expurgate the tramcar
conversations of Respectable Married Women
I continually hear with inward shudders
the most intimate details of operations
whereby they have been practically made over
again like worn-out hats or rebuilt type-
writers. In between whiles they babble of
castor-oil and flannel next the skin till it
seems to me as if half the world hasn’t got
its clothes on.
“Adelaide” at the Premiers’ picnic:—
Holman did not reflect any credit on N. S. Wales
at the Conference. To a discussion on a suggested
strategic railway he contributed the remark that
‘‘the only good that would come out of it was that
it would enable the soldiers to travel quickly from
anywhere to Brisbane, the only capital in Australia
where the pubs do not close early” ; which remark,
the papers record, ‘‘knocked all the seriousness out
of the debate.” On a claim by Blundell that South
Aus. always keeps its pledges, he retorted with the
cheerful insult: “Yes, when it suits you!” Another
joke, possibly ? His State Frights attitude was
something S. Aus. hadn’t seen for years. He
admitted not having taken the trouble to understand
the Tasmanian electoral system, even though it
“gives most satisfactory results” ; he had to be
told that the N.S.W. system of preparing rolls is
not that which obtains in the other States; and he
nearly blocked a common-sense proposal to have
State and Federal rolls identical by objecting to
let the Commonwealth officers prepare them. Hol-
man, if you please, “would not trust them. There
has been enough Federal bungling as it is. IV hat
are you going to have? Some mysterious Common-
wealth functionary sitting’ in Melbourne, and about
as conveniently situated, so far as the States are
concerned, as gentlemen in Melbourne usually are.
So there the cat was out of the bag, and the other
men round the table sat and stared. Was this the
big-minded Holman they had heard so much about!
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

The Black Squad.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
/’YE sung of his good points and bad ’uns,
And the music rings true to life;
You can laud his virtues to skyward,
But hush on the rest to his wife,
His mother, and genteel sisters;
They know that he travels his span,
But there’s lots they don’t know for certain,
And women aren’t built like a man.
When a steamer’s heaving ashes to the broad
blue ocean’s bed,
There’s a youthful tribe aslaving sick of
heart and sore of head;
When a steampipe goes to glory or the
gauges burst and fly,
There’s a youthlet standing handy who gets
it in the eye.
He’s a junior engineer, a blue-robed overseer,
With coaldust on his features and grease
spots on his rear;
He’s the junior engineer, the brand new
engineer,
Deploring ocean motion with innards feeling
queer.
When a gland is oozing vapor where the
packing wasn’t tight,
Or a winch snaps its eccentric in the ’witch-
ing hours of night,
There’s a nasty sniff of burning where the
piston got no drink,
And a youth is rudely wakened to get it
into pink.
He’s a junior engineer, a youthful engineer,
Groping in the darkness with sleepy eyes
ablear;
It’s a brand-new engineer, blaspheming most
severe,
Around the winches crawling with a little
bag of gear.
When the stormy seas are weathered and
the Indies loom in sight
The greenhorn feels that strangeness and
lure of tropic night;
Temptations strong beset him ; good resolu-
tions go,
As suddenly and surely his wild oats start
to grow.
It’s the junior engineer and a painted, poic-
dered dear
Baying things she shouldn’t in liis eager,
list'ning ear;
It’s the junior engineer, the brand-new
engineer
Canoodling icith his first 'un, icith the devil
lurking near.
When the beer is freely flowing in a Black
Squad-haunted inn.
The jobs are pulled to pieces and the tales
begin to spin
Of hell-fire chiefs and seconds, of breaking
down in gales,
Huge firemen smashed to atoms, and the
punch that never fails.
It’s a junior engineer, ashore upon his ear.
(Oh, the language waxes louder and dead
marines appear !)
It’s a brand-new engineer explaining rake
and sheer,
And building Deisel engines on fumes of
frothy beer.
When a steamer’s moored in harbor and the
fireman gets his pay,
It’s ten to one he’ll spend it along the liquid
way;
Then it’s lush for Lil and Liza, and he’s on
the Devil’s hob,
While the Black Squad bends its shoulders
to keep him in his job.
It’s the junior engineer, the brand-new over-
seer,
Sweeping hard and sweating till the smutty
tubes are clear;
It’s the youthful engineer, with slice and
shovel gear,
Getting down to double duties ichile the fire-
man mops his beer.
There is little kudos given to the toilers of
the Squad ;
There are few that know the value of the
rousing stoke-hold god ;
The skipper claims the glory for the record-
breaking run,
And the Chief collects the leavings, so
“Stoke her up, my son !”
It’s the junior engineer who wipes out the
arrear,
Who finds the extra mileage and keeps the
stokehold clear;
And daily proves a maxim, in'spite of slight
and sneer,
The backbone of his calling is the youthful
engineer.

JACK H. HAMILTON.
The Mule-breaker.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SOUP,” said one at the next table;
“soup, and then some fixings that I
' ~ can work with one wing. I’m a
hand short —understand ?”
He leaned back in his chair and sprawled
his feet abroad. His left sleeve was empty
and tucked into a pocket, and from the open-
ing of the loosened khaki jacket his fingers
stuck, straight and stiff, bound to a deal
board. I touched him on the shoulder, and
he wheeled where he sat.
“Good Lord! It’s you? Just when I was
dying for someone to talk to!” His great
brown hand cramped my arm to the elbow.
“This is making me feel better already,”
he said. “I’ve been looking for old mates
all day, but not one could I find. And then
to bump against you in this ancient place!
This is my lucky day.
“I’ve been seeing life since I met you last
at Dubbo. It’s been a lovely time. Left-
right, left-right. At the halt on the left
form platoon. Even numbers, take one pace
to the front with the hind-foot. I know it
all backwards. But the three-stripe bloke
that pounded it into us! He was just the
sweet pea. What that man didn’t know
about cursing doesn’t count. He could make
a horse blush.
“But he taught us. Oh, yes, he taught us,
and out Maadi way we don’t fall over our
rifles now much. A dependable push, our
lot; and when they get going, something’s
got to give. There’ll be dents.
“Been to Gallipoli? Not much! All I've
seen of the firing-line you could put in a pint
billycan. But there’s plenty of time....
Oh, my arm you mean? It was like this.
After I’d been treading desert sand for
about ten weeks the O.C. asked me what I
was good for.
“‘Ah!’ he says. ‘Horse-breaker, eh?
Ah! Then you know something about
stock, what?’
“I wasn’t going to argue. I couldn’t see
the back of his mind, but anything was
better than drilling in sand.
“ ‘Horse-breaker?’ he says. ‘Then per-
haps you know something about mules?
Report yourself to the farrier-sergeant.’
That was what I got for blowing.
"Breaking in mules! I’d sooner teach
snakes to salute by numbers. There was
one in particular. She took a dislike to me
from the jump. You couldn’t see her when
she put her head down and really got going
—only a sort of haze.
LINE ENGAGED.
ABSENT-MINDED DOCTOR: “Hello! Central? 00)3 Windsor, please."
“Gerty her name was. A noble animal
with a leg at each corner and several spare
ones stuck on in between. When she blew
out her nostrils and laid her lips back, which
she mostly did all the time, her mouth looked
like the front of a piano. A peevish crea-
ture, but very earnest.
“One day, when she was looking pensive,
I got astride of her. She turned her head
round, nodded to me and started semaphor-
ing with her ears. Flip-flap, flip-flap-flap!
“I patted her neck just to let her see I
was friendly, but ydu might as well have
tickled the corner post of a bush pub with
a straw. She had no time for patting. Just
turned round and tried to eat one of my
boots. . , .
“So I started to deal to her with a whip,
and that was where I did my dash. Her
feelings were hurt, and she started off in
the direction of Luxor, mostly sideways and
on two legs—any two that came handy.
Then she tried turning cartwheels and
standing on her head, but that didn t do any
good.
“Her imitation of an aeroplane was both
intellectual and elevating, and it was on a
bit of hard ground that she shed me over
the tail-end. As I spun, she reached out
her left and landed me a beauty.
“I took the count to nine, and decided to
miss the next round, and when I got the
sand out of my mouth and eyes there was
Gerty doing a corroboree all on her lonesome
about a mile away.
“That was the end of my mule-breaking.
There was an inquiry about a missing mule,
and I had to hand in a written explanation
full of ‘wherefores,’ with two carbon copies.”
BILL BOWYANG.
The Victim.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
ORSTRUTII ! See what the bloke
-w- says here: —
Ai Our heroes are often the victims of the
well-meanin’ but mistaken hospitality of
people who think it necessary to show their appre-
ciation of the service they have volunteered to render.
Gorstruth !” Further words failed him.
I had noticed Bill on the Manly boat in
the morning. He wore his khaki, and he had
a girl with him. The enterprising chocolate-
boy with the clothes three sizes too big for
him had seen an opportunity for trade, and
by judicious flattery and giggling assistance
from the girl he had worked up to a half-
crown box of chocolates. After I had seated
myself for the return trip the same lad in
khaki had wandered on board, and,
remembering my smile upon his transaction
of the morning, had dropped himself beside
me.
“That tart gave me the dirty go-by,” he
said. “I bought her half a dollar’s worth
of chocolates, paid the fares and all that,
and I’d gone in to get her some fruit she
asked for when she does a bunk. I’m off
looking for her for an hour or so; and I
just misses the boat before this. There she
was, sitting down with another bloke, eating
my chocolates with ’im ; and, as the boat’s
moving off, blow me if she don’t wave to
me!”
“Pretty hot!” I said.
“It’s up to dirt !” he returned. “If I find
them over at the Quay I’ll towel him up, I
promise you! I reckon this paper yarn
about misplaced hospitality to soldiers is all
rot. It don’t exist!
“First leave I get after I come to camp
in Sydney I swank into town feeling like a
moving picture for people to look at. I
think there’s a bit of free beer for me. But
nobody holds me up and drags me into a pub
to pour admirin’ beer into me. When I feel
I want a drink I have to go in and shout
for meself. And I’ve hardly started my pot
before a cove edges up and says: ‘You’re the
kind of bloke I like to meet —a chap that
isn’t skulking away when his country needs
him.’ ‘Have a drink?’ I says. ‘Thanks,’ he
says; ‘I was just going to ask you !’
“He orders a beer, and by-and-bye he
THE CHEQUE.
“Well, what's the matter? It's right, isn't
it?"
“Yes, young man, .it is. But be careful—
it's only just right.”
seems to nod across the bar, and another
chap comes to our elbow. ‘Joe Smith—
friend of mine; soldier in camp—didn’t get
your name,’ he says. ‘Kelly,’ I says; and of
course I asks the new chap to have a drink.
When the beer got low they were busy telling
me what lots they thought of me for enlist-
ing. so what could I do but fill ’em up
again? Then they reminded one another of
the meeting they had to go to and they told
me I’d have to look ’em up some time, and
have a drink with them.
“Another time a chap who’s in a bar with
a friend invites me and another khaki bloke
into the shout. He seems a good sort. By
and by the barman says, ‘Note, then, who’s
paying?’ The bloke says, ‘That’s all right,
they’re mine—l'll pay you to-morrow.’ The
barman says it can’t be done, and there’s a
bit of a barney, so I weighs in. ‘lt’s damn
good of you,’ says the bloke; ‘you come down
with me to the office after, and I’ll return
the bob and we’ll have another drink.’ ‘Oh,
what rot!’ I says. ‘lt’s only a bob!’ ‘I
asked you to have a drink,’ he says, very
stiff. Then he says if I lent him a traybit
he could jump on the tram and get down to
the office and back in no time. So I lent him
sixpence; and his friend says he had to slip
over to the markets, but he’d be back in a
minute. That was the last I saw of them.
“There was one chap I met, though, who
was all right, only he didn’t have the money,
lie was a poet or something. He held me
up in the street —put one hand on my
shoulder and shook with his other. Then he
said: ‘You’re a soldier —glad to meet you!
Always glad to drink with a soldier!’ After
feeling in his pockets a long time he forked
out fourpence, and showed it to me. ‘Tup-
pence,’ he says—‘give me tuppence, and
we’ll have a drink.’ He was a good bloke;
but he was a poet.”
When we reached the Quay I was almost
leaving him : but I remembered in time to
do my little bit to remove his grievance. As
we consumed a glass of beer a seedy person
obtruded himself upon us.
“Soldier!” he said, extending his hand.
“If I hadn’t just spent my last bean I’d have
asked you to have a drink with me. I spend
all me money shouting for soldiers!”
“Gorstruth!” gasped my khaki friend.

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Pick”: When a shepherd-boy on the
cold Bibbenluke Plains (N.S.W.) I once found two
rams that had starved to death owing to their horns
becoming interlocked. Another pitiful death was
that of a steer on the sea cliffs near Bermagui. He
slipped over and went down a converging fissure in
the rocks until suspended some 10ft. from the beach
below. As he became emaciated he gradually low-
ered away until his hoofs reached the sand, where
the incoming tide finished him. At Kiandra, just
over the summit of Governor’s Hill, the skeleton of
a wild horse hung for months in the fork of a tree
30ft. up. He got there when floundering over a
frozen snowdrift. A magpie by some undiscoverable
means became held by the claw on the upper side of
a branch that belonged to an unclimbable tree near
the Corunna cheese factory. The poor fellow stayed
there calling distressingly and struggling for free-
dom, until after many days we ended his misery
with a pea-rifle.
“liimington”: “Stingo,” in his argument
in favor of high-velocity rifles against the older
types (B. 20/4/’l6), forgot to state that the bullets
must be softnosed or hollow pointed. With solid bullets
they do not come within cooee of the Martini-Henry,
whose heavy lead bullet puts a full stop to
the proceedings. Years ago, on Las Pampas, in
Argentina, I one night mistook an old creamy mare
lying in the long grass for a puma, and, dismount-
ing, took a prone shot at a distance of about 60yds.
The bullet struck the poor brute near the eye, lifted
the lid of her brainbox and left it as empty as Hol-
man’s Treasury. The mare belonged to a neighbor-
ing estancia, so I got back home in quick time.
Afterwards I heard the gauchos, for want of better,
blame the lightning—which couldn’t have made a
cleaner job of it. By the same token, any reliable
make of .38 .41 or .44 revolver is, at close range, a
more deadly weapon than the average automatic
pistol, yet it is not its equal for rapidity of fire,
range and penetration. As an ideal weapon for savage
places (like Western Europe) I think the palm goes
to the Mauser pistol, with a 20-shot magazine filled
with soft-nosed cartridges. But don’t go t and get
captured with soft-noses in your pouch.
* * * *
“Scaler”: My method of exterminating
dead stumps is easier and cheaper than “Leo Lear’s”
(B. 6/4/’l6). Pile a few sticks around the tree or
stump, cover the stack completely with sods, and
set fire on one side. The sods, which must be placed
with the grass side in, will force the heat down and
prevent the flames from rising more than a foot up
the trunk. Consequently the butt burns like
November 5 until the tree falls and breaks /off,
as a rule, about three inches above the ground. It
requires only another sod or so to cover where the
tree has broken off, and in a fortnight stumps with,
say, a diameter of 4ft. or sft. are settled, as the
sod fire burns many feet into the ground. This
method is in use all over the Wide Bay district
of Queensland, and for cheapness and efficiency can-
not be equalled.
“Billy T.” : Herne, on my right, is a rabid
amateur entomologist. His collection of domestic
and dog fleas is the most comprehensive in Aus-
tralia, and includes a stuffed old-man red ’roo, 7ft.
high, for it’s Herne’s intention to demonstrate that
the red kangaroo is the ultimate development of the
Putex irritans or familiar bedfellow. Yesterday
Campbell at the back was burning rubbish in his
yard. Herne came over my fence, landing in my
narcissus bed. He had his wife’s large yard hat in
his hand. He then went over Cable’s -fence. Never
did I see a man of 50 negotiate a 7ft. fence with
such celerity. He simply ran up the palings like
a cat, and fell off the top. Then he smashed through
Cable’s hotbed, overturned a perambulator and went
over the fence into Brown’s yard. Brown, who was
licsing the dahlias, turned the water on him in
alarm. But Herne had now got the hat pinned down,
and he hung on. “Stop, stop!” he said. “It’s only
a butterfly. I’ve got him! A wonderful specimen
of bisexual Kallima inachis —extremely rare. I’ve
got him!” Very gently he raised the hat, very
carefully he grabbed the rare specimen—a scrap of
burnt paper sent eddying in the wind from Camp-
bell’s fire.

“Block 10”: The mutton-birds that hail
from the Bass Strait Islands are short-tailed, and
abound in millions. Salted, smoked or pickled, they
form the chief food of the half-castes on Cape Barren
Island reserve, and are also on sale in most fish-shops
in Tassy and Melbourne. But there is another
species, which abounds in the Five Islands, near
Wollongong (N.S.W.). These chaps winter some-
where out over the Pacific, return to the islands in
September, clean out their burrows and otherwise
fill in time till about the first week in November,
put to sea again and return three weeks later, when
each hen-bird lays a single egg, white in color, 2Jin.
long and 14in. through. If any abo. thinks of tramp-
ing down from the Queensland side in search of
mutton-bird for breakfast let me advise him to drop
Five Islands astern and keep on till he strikes the
Bass Strait species, which are also to be found in
Southern Victoria. The difference between the two
is worth it. If the traveller brings as much of his
landlord’s hide with him as will cover the hand he
shoves into the mutton’s dug-out, so much the better ;
the average bird would peck a hole through a boot.
* * * *
“X.Y.”: “A.G.8.” (B. 11/5/T6) asks for
information concerning smut in cereals. From long
and extensive experience I advise the Jensen treat-
ment. It gives results far superior to any chemical
lay-out. The process is described in the N.S.W.
Agricultural Gazette, March, 1893, vol. 4, part 3,
page 188. As the publication may not be readily
available, and, as the matter is of national import-
ance, I pass on a condensed description of the treat-
ment : Two vessels of water, one heated to a tempera-
ture of from llOdeg. to 130 deg. F., the other to
135 deg. F. The first is' for raising the temperature
of the grain before it is introduced into the actual
sterilising bath. Unless this course is adopted it
will be difficult to maintain the correct temperature
in the second vessel. The grain should be placed in
a bag of light, open texture, such as a chaff bag, so
that the water can readily enter. According to the
size of the vessels the amount of grain treated at one
time may vary from half a bushel to a bushel. Dip
the grain into No. 1 vessel (llOdeg. to 130 deg.),
raising and lowering it two or three times to secure
equal warming. This process will only take a couple
of minutes. Then dip the bag of grain into the
warmer vessel, and stir the grain so that it may
become uniformly heated. It may also be lifted and
plunged in several times, for the same purpose. This
should be continued for from 10 to 15 minutes
according to the temperature of the water, and the
grain is then spread out to dry. An assistant should
regulate the temperature of the sterilising vessel, and
this may readily be done by having one, or more,
kerosene-tins of boiling water at hand. The tempera-
ture should be 135 deg. F., when the grain is put in.
If at the end of 10 minutes the temperature of the
water has not fallen below 133 deg. F. the grain should
be removed and plunged into cold water. If below
133 deg. F: at the end of 10 minutes it should be
left in 15 minutes, or even longer if the temperature
should fall below 130 deg. F. In treating oats, the
temperature of the sterilising-bath should be 140 deg.
F. when the grain is dipped. If the temperature
does not fall below 135 deg. F. at the end of 10
minutes the oats may be removed and plunged into
cold water. Should the temperature be below 135 deg.,
but above 130 deg., the oats may remain for five
minutes longer. Although only a bushel is treated
at a time a considerable quantity may be dealt with
in half a day. The plunge into cold water, for a
finish, is an essential part of the process. Do not let
the treated grain come into contact with the original
bags, or with anything liable to be infected. Bags
and planting machine may be sterilised with boiling
water. The grain may be sown by hand immediately
after treatment, but must be quite dry to run
through a machine properly. I used this process for
many years, and I was unanimously satisfied. Only
one heating vessel was used, a large, galvanised
washtub in which the water was heated by steam
from a boiler. A thermometer must be used; any
cheap make that will register up to 140 deg. and
will not be injured by hot water will serve.
“Saltwater” : Now that you’ve all wasted
time with cumbersome methods of finding the points
of the compass when you're bushed, I’ll explain how
it’s done. Sight the Southern Cross after sunset
(before, if possible). Then if a straight line start-
ing from the top star of the long leg and passing
down that leg be extended beyond the bottom star
of the long leg for about four lengths of the Cross,
it will locate pretty closely the south pole of the
heavens. Our national emblem always points towards
this pole.
“Sarto”: I stirred up a big ugly toad
this morning while he was trying to get outside one of
those huge moths that come out of the ground after
the first autumn rains, leaving the three-inch shell
that held the pupa behind. These shells are brcwr
and yellow, and anything from sixpence to a shilling
in diameter, so you can imagine the size of the
moth. Last year and this they were here (Southern
Riverina) in thousands. The brown monsters bang
against the roof and window-panes like clods, and
this is not exaggerating a bit. The end of the moth
that this toad hadn’t yet swallowed was fully two
inches long and three-quarters of an inch thick,
tapering to a point. I tried to turn the malignant
party over with my boot, and he blew himself out
until I thought he’d burst with rage.
(Continued on page 26.)
NIRVANA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When I am done with this blasted war
I will go where the bul-bul sings,
And sit at my ease
Under suitable trees
Discoursing of scandalous things.
I will buy me a mug that shall hold a quart,
And charge it with foaming ale,
Which I’ll frequently quaff
And as constantly scoff
At any heroic tale.
Others will tell how the bullets sang,
And how good blood ran red.
But I will relate,
In contemplative state,
How (’ tention! ) the generals fed.
I will tell of the mountains of tins at the base-
There was pate and chicken and tongue ;
And their beautiful beds,
How around the heads
Immaculate network hung.
Oh, yes, I will tell of the portly men
In dugouts with soft settees,
And bottles and corks
And asparagus forks
At the shrines of our bold O.C.’s.
Then loudly I’ll laugh at the tales of strife,
And I’ll leer at my tankard’s brim,
For my soul shall find peace
When their babblings cease
And the clamor of war grows dim.
SOMEWHERE.
ADAM TOSSPOT, Sapper.

“Hay” : I don’t know whether carnivorous
horses are common, but I once owned an old chestnut
in Centralia that was quite ready to share steak and
damper with me. He was what bushmen would call
a hard-doer, and would keep in good fettle where
most other horses would starve. But as an eater
of flesh the horse isn’t in it with the donkey. On a
station in the northern part of South Aus. where
mules were raised I have seen two old jacks come up
to the men’s hut regularly for their meals. A
shoulder of mutton or any other kind of cooked
meat would be thrown out to them, and holding the
joint down with one foot they would tear the meat
off just as a dog would. They seemed to thrive on a
meat diet and to prefer it to any other. When tank-
making on a West o’ Darling station a pet wether fol-
lowed the camp and boarded with us for a year and
a half. He ate everything that we had to spare from
the table, and shared the men’s tobacco as well. He
was a handsome jumbuck, and grew to a great size,
but died eventually of gout.

“Oopabella” : There had been a little argu-
ment between the newchum parson and his horse. The
rescuing cocky gathered up a parson here, a surplice-
bag there, hat, coat, cushions, sermon, Bible and
prayerbook in other places. “Spreadin’ the gospel a
bit, Mister!” he said, affably.

“Bill Bedsox” : As a kick off for a further
arboreal shindy, what about W.A. York gum for
length of grain ? A representative of this variety
shattered by lightning some years ago, and exhibited,
I believe, by the Agricultural Department in Perth,
had splinters 12ft. long. For wheel-hubs it is ideal
timber, but trees of any size are rarely sound, and
straight logs of more than seven or eight feet in
length are the exception. York gum is difficult to
kill, and will sucker from the roots for seven years
at least after being cleared to ground level. There
is a tradition that the hobs of Hades are composed of
green York gum stumps. Have known Pommies to
burn a ton or more of wood round one stump and
then only make crackling of the bark.
* * * *
“Agricola”: As the summer dries the
swampy flats at the head of Wapengo Lake (South
Coast, N.S.W.), the eels in residence suffer their
annual Gehenna. In older days the blacks played
the part of the devil and waxed fat, but nowadays
snakes and gohannas run the show. The snakes go
right in among the eels and consume them on the
premises; the ’gos pay regular visits from the
surrounding scrub and carry their catch home for
dinner. They wade into the mud, seize the local
inhabitant, and paddle out again. Pigs belonging
to neighboring cookies also revel there and look the
picture of happiness with 2ft. of slimy tucker hang-
ing out of each side of their mouths. If the porker
roots out a snake by mistake, it appears to trouble
him not, except that he seems to be careful to grab
it near the head.
“K.G.C.”: Some George-street bush-
whacker wants to know if the Clarence is the only
river in which freshwater cod and eel live together.
No ; it is not, Ethelbert. Here on the Upper Bris-
bane River, near Toogoolawah (Q.), we often catch
them on the same line—a set line, which is a whip
running from bank to bank, with hooks at intervals
of sft. to 6ft. The cod denounces the eel as a
cannibal, and with good reason. I have opened a
191 b. wriggler with a 31b. cod in his safe deposit.

“Cholmondley Fitzmaggot”: In the won-
derful museum of antiquities at Cairo is a collection
of carved wooden objects labelled “boomerangs,”
quite like some of ours. The application of this
essentially Australian word to these weapons of
ancient Egypt puzzled me, and I hunted up the
director to find out all about it. He told me that
these “throw-sticks” were used by the ancients as
weapons of the chase, and showed me some pictorial
(ancient) representations of their use, particularly
in securing such birds as ducks. He could not deter-
mine definitely (though he inclined to the negative)
whether or not these “throw-sticks” returned to the
pitcher in similar manner to our boomerang. There
is no written or other evidence that they did. What
interests me is how the Egyptian failed to discover
the gyrating possibilities of the weapon. The word
“boomerang” was applied by the museum authorities
as representing a generic term for all “throw-sticks.”
I was able to tell the director that the word had no
such meaning in its blaekfellow application; that it
was an aboriginal word applied to a special weapon,
and did not comprehend the nulla-nulla and spear,
the other “throw-sticks” of our natives. Honors
in connection with the boomerang—if not in its
discovery, then at least in its development—seem to
rest wholly with our abo.
* * * *
“Harry Gee”: I thought after a long resi-
dence in the north-west of W.A. that I had nothing
to learn of the habits of aboriginals; but this one
was new to me. I was convoying sheep to a station
on the Murchison and had for assistance a combo
and his native better half, Julia. Rows between the
two were frequent, and at last for the whole of one
day they were not on speaking terms. That night
w-hen Combo was seated at the camp fire I saw Julia
approach him holding at arm’s length a kangaroo
tail. Combo took no notice, and the lady retired.
Presently she reappeared, this time carrying a fair-
sized bungarra by the neck. By almost impercept-
ible movements the gin got closer and closer to the
man and no words were spoken. At last the bunga
hung straight down from the rim of Combo’s hat,
passing in a line with his nose, the tail reaching
the end of his long grey beard. Slowly Combo put
out his hand, and with a deft movement Julia laid
the bunga on his upturned palm. Then the silence
was broken. The bunga was cooked and shared
between them, and they were still holding wongi
when I went to sleep.
* * * #
“Gecko”: From my observations so-called
water snakes flirt in a fashion foreign to all other
Australian fanged fauna. Like the African puff adder
they possess the power of inflating their throats for
the purpose of producing a weird ventriloquial call.
I saw a couple of the black crawlers, on a sheep
track close to a creek, standing up and cooing like
doves to one another. Suddenly her ladyship lovingly
looped the loop around Othello until her paramour
formed the last letter of the alphabet.
“D.F.”: The protection of our native
fauna is about as big a farce as the alleged conserva-
tion of our native timber trees. Some years ago
the Tasmanian Government set aside a large part of
Schouten Main, on the East Coast, as a reserve where
the kangaroo, the wallaby, the ’possum and other
native beasts and birds could dwell and multiply in
peace. Several alleged settlers got in ahead of the
proclamation, and took up little blocks of worthless
land on the peninsula, which is pretty poor at the
best. There’s nobody to look after the reserve except
the infrequent local policeman, who has all the work
he can manage without that. So the net result of
the supposed “sanctuary” is that it is a first-class
hunting park for poachers. Some recent visitors
found snares set all over the place and mighty little
native game about. The Lands Department
was warned, and the officials said they’d see if
something could not be done about it. You know
what that means. A Sceneries Preservation Board
has just been appointed to look after national re-
serves, but it hasn’t any money and its chance of
getting enough to jingle is vague and afar off.
* * * #
“Hay”: “Barcoo” (B. 6/4/T6) describes
two different kinds of emu. I have known the emu
for many years all over Australia, except part of
N. S. Wales east of the Darling, and so far have
only met with one species. I have gathered eggs
from their nests in settings varying from one to a
dozen. To one camp enough of them were brought
to fill a bullock dray, and on the menu they
appeared boiled, fried, roasted in the ashes, and
mixed in cakes and puddings. In flavor there
seemed to be no difference between them and the
fruit of the common or garden fowl. The emu
makes its nest on the ground beside a bush so as
to get a little shelter from the noonday sun. The
blacks say the male bird sits on the fruit at night
and the female in the daytime. The dark, coarse
beefy meat of the emu, especially when bordered
with a nice rim of oily yellow fat, is highly
esteemed by Binghi, while the shredded sinews of
its legs yield serviceable lengths of material for
sewing and net-making. They are better than even
kangaroo-tail sinews for Binghi’s requirements.
* * * *
“Greenhorn”: Will the cook for the Aho.
camp inform me whether the flesh of the black
cockatoo (with the yellow tail), which feeds on white
grubs extracted from the stringy-bark, is worth
sharpening an appetite for? Also, I want to know
a simple method of tanning or dry-curing opossum or
wallaby skins, either singly or about half a dozen at
a time. That’ll be all to-day.

“Hambidge”: If it’s queer grub for
animals you’re talking about, my cat ought to be
heard from. Her craving was for beetroot, pickled.
So strong was her appetite for this that I’ve known
her to knock a glass-jar of beet off a shelf and gorge
on the mess until she was just about drunk with the
vinegar.

iSgatilt ’: Sharks are probably more plan
tiful on the reef skirting the Satowan Lagoon fCen

‘‘Ckee Tah”: Only spasmodic efforts are
made to enforce the Thistle Act in Northern Victoria
where the shire councillors who are supposed to ad-
minister the law are mostly large landowners and
arch-offenders in regard to dirty paddocks. Star- and
saffron-thistle areas are the rule rather than the
exception away from the irrigation and wheat-raising
areas. The lazy grazier affects to see a virtue in the
pests on the plea that they keep the dry grass from
being blown away ! When a shire council does sum-
mons Victorian ratepayers (often absentees residing
in the city) for their long-standing derelictions, the
police magistrate’s favorite fine is a fiver. Seein»
the cost of eradicating the star and saffron nuisances
is at least fourpence per acre, it can be easily calcu-
lated that it pays the big estate owners to hand up a
series of fines. Until the Thistle Act is administered
by the Government on the same drastic lines as the
Rabbit Extermination Act, the present farce will
continue.
* * * *
“Woychugga”: Eet me help you to some
mere mutton-bird. Matthew Flinders, accompanied
by Ross, in December, 1798, calculated a flock that
ho saw in the vicinity of Hummock Island, Bass
Strait, at some 132 millions. Maybe be was right,
for Davies, in the Tasmanian Journal, recorded that
he sailed through a flight of young muttons from
Flinders Island to the Tamar Heads, a distance of
89 miles. Anyhow, one and a half million of the
young birds are captured annually on Babel and
Chappel Islands.

Snappy Temper, Rheumatism.
All women suffer—some more than others—during some periods of their lives. Have you
ever stopped to think why this should be? Long-suffering women become so used to it as to
think that they must suffer for the rest of their natural lives. This is wrong. Most of this
suffering is absolutely unnecessary, and is in many cases due to kidney or bladder trouble—
nothing more. And you can prove it.
Kidney and bladder trouble is far commoner than most people would imagine. It is
i eported that at least six out of every ten people who “go sick” do so through kidney disease.
And, moreover, five out of every ten sufferers die from this same cause. If people only knew
how to tell whether their kidneys were diseased or not it would not be so bad, but thousands
upon thousands of men and women to-day are content to suffer and die in ignorance. Weak
kidneys lead to deadly Bright’s Disease in many cases.
Sure and Certain Symptoms of Kidney Trouble.
How are you to really recognise Kidney Trouble? Here is the way: —Have you a nasty
taste in the mouth the first thing in the morning, puffiness under the eyes, general feeling of
weakness and irritability, aches and pains in various parts of the body? Have you constipa-
tion, cloudy urine, or sharp stabbing or aching pains—a pain “as though my back will break ?
People who suffer from backache, rheumatism, gout, sciatica, gravel, stone, cystitis
(inflammation of the bladder), or pains in the muscles and joints, are all suffering these pains
through weak or diseased kidneys, for these are positively the symptoms of kidney trouble.
These signs of kidney or bladder trouble are caused by the poisonous acid in the blood
which the kidneys should remove, but they do not, owning to their being in a w r eak state. De

within twenty-four hours these wonderful pills have without doubt commenced their healing
work guaranteed. Do not put off, because w r eak kidneys very often means Bright s Disease
m the end. But when you buy the pills be sure you get De Witt’s, in large white boxes printed
in blue and gold wdtli a blue wax seal on the top of the bottle. Refuse any of similar name
without this special seal.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Murrabit”: War and high freights are,
thank God, bringing a few Australian lines
before the public, i lately had to renew my
stock of a well-known English modelling
medium. It was not procurable, but I found
an equally good plastic clay manufactured
at Echuca. A patent was obtained for this
as far back as September, 1912; yet this
was the first time the local article was
stocked by the large firm from which we get
our supplies. It’s cheaper, too; though this
idea that the local stuff should always be
cheaper is an unmitigated curse.
* * * *
“Pick”: Since Europe started to lose its
temper and tear its uniform, shipments of
Rhodesian and Magaliesberg (S.African)
tobaccos to N. S. Wales have apparently
ceased. The nearest approach to the
S’African smoke is an Australian manufac-
ture with the brand of “Yanco” upon the
tin. Among smokers whose taste inclines
them to the pure, unpressed weed, this home-
made article might now get its chance. Try
it, brothers, in your patriotic pipes.
“Quidam”: It was in the early days of
Charters Towers that a miner met a mate
and asked him if he could do with a share
in a show. He explained that he had a leg-
in in the Day Dawn and another in the
Wellington, and he believed in them both,
but could only finance one. The chum ex-
plained that he, too, was full-handed, but he
knew a young chap, just arrived from Syd-
ney, who was earning good wages as a car-
penter, and perhaps he might take it. When
the young chap was interviewed he said he
didn’t believe in risking money in mining—
he’d come to the Towers to saw wood. After
a lot of arguing, however, he gave in, and
accepted the Day Dawn share and the calls
hanging to it. The Wellington turned out a
duffer at depth ; the Day Dawn P.C. turned
out a mint of money. Further, the carpenter
did well with his lot, and eventually be-
came one of the very rich business men of
Sydney.

During 1915 four Hobart milksellers were
found guilty of adding H2O to their cow-
juice, and the city health-officer observes
that the severest punishment would not be
too heavy for dishonest traders who adulter-
ate the children’s food. He does not say a
word about the local beaks whose idea of
punishment worked out, including fine and
costs, at £3 os. 44d. per time.
Tasmanian clay is good enough to make
drain-pipes and flower-pots with, but in the
past it has ben rank heresy to talk of making
it into chinaware. Now even Agent-
General McCall has seen a glimmering of the
light. He reports with great regret that it
would, he is afraid, not pay to drag the clay
to Britain and then freight the cups and
saucers back again. But with an inspira-
tion as brilliant as it is rare, he suggests
doing the work —-right—on—the spot!
* * * *
“Moana”: The New Zealand Shipping
Co.’s new route between the lOU Isles and
Fogland recalls that about 50 years ago a
line of steamers owned by the N.Z. and
Panama Co. started a service between Wel-
lington (M.L.) and Panama in conjunction
with a line running from Colon to Plymouth
on the Atlantic. Passengers and mails were
hurried by rail across the strip of land where
the canal now is. Two brig-rigged steamers,
the Kaikoura and the Ruahine, pioneered
the service in the Pacific, calling on the way
at the island of Rapa-iti, out in the Eastern
Pacific, for coal. Within a few years via
Panama will probably be the sole route for
cargo beween Volcanolaud and the Northern
Hemisphere.
* * * *
An 8000-gallon tank built of kauri pine,
put up in the Hobart railway yards in 1874
and in use throughout the intervening 42
years, was pulled down the other day. The
whole of the timber, except three or four
staves, was found to be as good as new.
Anyway, it is to be used to make another
tank somewhere else on the railway. Tas-
manian Huon pine lasts equally well, for
some tanks of this timber built at the same
time (when the old Main Line railway
was put through) are still sound.
“Hamer”: A number of business sharks
have a habit of addins interest on overdue
accounts ; and although the charge is a pure
bluff it is no doubt pulled off very fre-
quently—like the “collection fees” . that
debt-collecting agencies tack on to their de-
mands. The whole thing is entirely illegal;
yet how well it may succeed, was. illustrated
in a recent series of cases in Victoria. A
firm sued a batch of debtors, securing judg-
ment in each case for the amount claimed.
Then, luckily, a contentious defendant up-
rose to say that what he objected to about
the proceedings was the claim for interest
on his account. That set the Court ferret-
ing, and eventually it recalled all the other
orders, and, while deducting the interest
charge from the totals, told the firm’s repre-
sentative that he was lucky to escape prose-
cution for perjury for swearing that the
amounts claimed were “for goods sold and
delivered.” But if it hadn’t been for the
one contentious hard-case the whole lot
would have gone through.
* * * *
“Ivinso”: A large rabbit-freezing factory
is going up at Oberon (N.S.W.), and already
Bunny is putting on airs in that district.
-Even now 5-id. a pair is being paid by
carters who have to run them 16 miles ; and
the prospect of much more when the freezer
gets to work is making prosperous land-
owners take a benevolent interest in the old
curse. The factory w’ill cost about £4OOO,
and it will take a pair or two to pay exes,
and give dividends on that lot.
* * * *
“M.E.H.”: A contributor (B. 24/2/TO)
says that, “with all its faults, the automatic
[i.e., Wheatstone] is the only possible means
of coping with telegraph traffic.” I suppose
it is for this reason that the M.L. service,
which discarded the Wheatstone years ago,
can produce a profit of £50,000 per annum.
What kills the Wheatstone is the army of
skilled men required to dance attendance on
it at each end. For years M.L., which had
already shelved the apparatus during ordi-
nary traffic, wheeled in the 'Wheatstone at
what is termed the “annual endurance test”
—Christmas Eve. After the onslaught
scores of enervated lightning-jerkers wearily
dragged themselves homewards, and tumbled
into their beds with a curse on all perforated
and Morse-inked tapes, while mentally kicking
the automatic and all its gear 10 miles past
Tophet. It has been down and out for a few
years now, but it took more than one
straight to the point to out it. The Wheat-
stone was the pioneer of automatic signalling-
and-recording telegraphy, but some later sys-
tems knock it into a cocked hat. Recently
I flitted through some of the Australian
offices, expecting to see something. I did—
Chaos! Specimens of the tape contrap-
tions seen in country offices have been in the
M.L. departmental museums for years. A
paucity of quadruplex sets, which enable one
line to do the work of four, was very appar-
ent. This method of economising lines, per-
fected by some person named Edison, is see -
in even third-rate offices on this side of the
Tasman. Failing the quad., a few new lines
(which are now undoubtedly necessary before
the Wheatstone can be oblivioned) would
probably help things along.
* * * *
A Drouin (Vic.) man wins the prize for
a butcher’s motto. Over the slain ox and
the slaughtered lamb he has written : “Live
and let live.”
RUBBER ONIONS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The dodge was discovered by one of the boarding
party dropping an onion on the deck. It bounced,
and on examination was found to be pure rubber.
—Blockade item.
Ho! I have taken coal from Wales to Egypt’s burnin’
sands,
An’ carried rice from ’ot Rangoon to fill a Chow-
slave’s ’ands ;
I've beat the sea with tons of grain from River
Plate to ’Ull,
An’ staggered back with rusted rails in spite o’
shark an’ gull;
Around th’ world an’ back again with breakfast
foods an’ soap,
Riskin’ me life for ’ides an’ tea, an’ toilin’ without
’ope
To get a bale o’ rag to port—’twas all the same
to me;
But them there rubber onions took the pie we make
at sea.
They’d filled the ’oiler masts wi’ some, th’ rumblin’
shaft ’id niore,
An’ rubber onions ’ung above, an’ ’id .beneath th’
floor;
They looked like onions always do, for they ’ad made
’em well,
An’ there was real live onions to give tli’ proper
smell.
We turned around an’ went to sea to do our rummy
trade,
When somethin’ grey came smokin’ up—we’d struck
the d —d blockade !
An’ w’en th’ boat’s crew boarded us we got it in th’
neck,
For one o’ them damn sailors dropped a onion on
the deck.
It bounced an’ bounced, that onion did, like any
kid’s toy ball,
For it was rubber through an’ through an’ couldn’t
be let fall;
An’ pretty soon the cruiser said with flags, “Please
foller me,”
An’ so we ’awked our onions ’ome across th’ cold
North Sea.
If raftin’ rice from old Rangoon an’ takin’ coal from
Wales
Is not enuf to make a song or fill a book o’ tales,
I’d like to ask if anyone can thumb an ’arp with
me
On sneakin’ rubber onions o’er the same ole boundin’
sea ?
N.S.W.
THE CARPENTER’S MATE.
A DREAM OF BILLJIM'S VENGEANCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The way was long, the world was cold,
The stockman sat his weary moke.
For years he had not tasted gold,
For days he had not known a smoke.
The State in both its feet was cold
When Cohen aimed his counterstroke.
The merry war had blown to peace;
No more the loans were large and free.
The noisy cannon had to cease
In ravaged lands beyond the sea;
Yes, graves were green in utter peace,
And Cohen shrieked for £ s. d.
He battled for his pound of flesh—•
In fact, for many pounds he strove;
And every day he tried afresh
To drag the whiskers off some cove.
His trade was both in souls and flesh;
His face was harder than a stove.
Pawn-tickets were the nation’s wealth,
Sad things finance could not redeem ;
Nor oaths, nor prayers, nor force, nor stealth
Could make one pale State surplus gleam;
For all about the Commonwealth
To think of tuppence was to dream.
The way was long, the world was cold.
The stockman sat his weary moke;
The happy sunbeams minted gold,
The merry breeze enjoyed the joke
That Billjim knew he had been sold,
And thought about his counterstroke.
Where were the men that borrowed so,
And spent the cash with lavish hand?
He thought of days of long ago
When things went well in all the land;
And then his hot blood bubbled so
It almost burst his singlet band.
And still the folk in whispers tell,
When night is soft and very brown,
Of stirring matters that befell
When Billjim in his wrath came down—
Yes, with a shaking lip they tell
How sudden vengeance came to town.
I know not all the things he did
To those who pawned his priceless years:
One statesman in a drainpipe hid,
One filled three buckets vyith his tears;
But none escaped of all who did
The country in for Mo’s arrears.
And this I know, that in the night
Men wake with sudden gasp and yell,
The cold sweat sitting on their fright,
Though summer slumbers warm and well:
Ar.d these remember that' wild night
When Billjim on the traitors fell.
The Kaiser swinging on his rope,
The hempen knot beside his ear,
Felt blithe as Joy and gay as Hope
Compared with these when fate was near;
Had things been simplified to rope
They might have thought it mild as beer.
And now, though all the land were broke,
And Cohen offered wealth of gold,
No statesman e’er would dare to yoke
His name with loans, like those of old;
He thinks of Billjim's counterstroke,
And feels his ten pink toes grow cold.
x.s. w.
PAT O’MAORI.

THERMOGENE
WAS
INVENTED
BY A
BELGIAN

IN BRUSSELS.
The Truth about Thermogene.
Thermogene was discovered by Charles Vanden Broeck, of the Boulevard de
Waterloo, Brussels, a Belgian Chemist of high repute. In the year 1901, Charles
Vanden Broeck sold the British rights of Thermogene to an English Family, who are
the present owners. Since that date Thermogene has been British-owned and
manufactured entirely by British Labour at Hayward’s Heath, Sussex, England.
In S 909, when the steadily increasing demand for Thermogene called for a larger
output and an extension of the factory, the owners formed themselves into a private
limited company, known as the Thermogene Co., Ltd. The Shareholders, seven in
number, were all British, and, although one of the original Shareholders is now deceased,
the entire ownership remains in the hands of the same private British Company.
Thermogene, besides being a British property manufactured in Britain by British
labour, has not only secured the goodwill of the British Public on its own merits, but
is used by the British Red Cross Society and the Royal Navy.
General Manager and Secretary.

requires no preparation whatsoever—no boiling water, no mess, no fierce blister-raising heat.
A piece can be taken straight out of the box and applied at once. It can be secured by
means of a simple tape bandage, or may be lightly tacked to the underclothing over the
affected part, and can be worn without the least inconvenience or discomfort night and day.
