POLITICAL
POINTS
The S. M. Herald nearly burst its moral
boiler when it indignantly reported “a most
disgraceful” recruiting meeting last week.
The recruiting officers, it appears, had
hardly commenced operations when a street-
singer and his accomplice, with a degraded
harmonium, arrived nearby and spoiled
their pitch by drawing away the crowd
“until a tactful policeman moved the
singers on.” But this paper thinks it was
most unfair of the policeman. If the
authorities are content to assemble armies
on side-show fines, there should, anyhow,
be fair competition.
None of Hughes's new-fangled Protection
for 'Siali Thomas, the Federal Laborite
from Broken Hill! Treasurer Higgs said
the Australian Government had no desire
to continue to receive so much as
£15.000,000 in Customs revenue. “Then,”
said ’Siah, “do you want ships to come
out in ballast for our wheat, instead of
bringing goods?” 'Siah, who has an idea
that he is a very devil of a wild Socialist,
is really a sort of twin brother of B. Smith
and the most respectable old English Tory
iu the Reps.; so The Bulletin has no
hope of ever convincing him that, with a
tariff, we might presently get a population
to eat our own wheat. Precisely why we
should send gram 15,000 miles to Europe
to feed people who are doing things for us
that we might do for ourselves, nobody but
Bruce Smith’s twin-brother can explain.
The uumber of new industries that neu-
tral nations have started since the Hun blew
Belgium to bits is astonishing. Someone was
lately bemoaning the scarcity <>f enamel ware
in Australia, and wondering why it wnsn t
made here. Of course it is made in Australia
but there is n.» need now to keep the indus-
try going, because considerable quantities
are coming forward through England. It is
branded "Ivor. Sweden.' with a small
Swedish flag burnt in. The consular cer-
tificates. etc., are all in order, and the stuff
-may be Scandinavian : still, what if it is?
What does it profit the Australian manu-
facturer to be saved from being squeezed
to death by the German if his life is to be
crushed out of him by the Swede?
In the House of Commons somebody
asked - Asquith for an assurance that mil-
lions of people will not be driven back to
starvation wages when the war is over. In
reply Asquith the Unready merely asked:
“How can I give that assurance?”. instead
of saying (with an oath if he liked t : “There
shall be no more starvation wages if the
Government eau help it.” Anyhow, he gave
the munition workers no encouragement to
redouble their efforts. The only assistance
they can be sure of getting from the Asquith
Government is fair wages whilst the war
lasts. After that? Well, the Free-
trader thinks of his dear old friends, the
large employers of labor, and asks: “How
do / know?”
N.S.W. Attorney-General Hall is at any
rate a cheerful ass. At the State Premiers'
Conference in Adelaide last week the pic-
nickers were expressing apprehension about
the unemployment there would be if the
troops happened to come back in the absence
of copious supplies of loan, and they re-
fused to be comforted until Hall threw the
light of a brilliant intellect across the
gloom. “Oh. they won't all be unemployed,”
he explained, “because the increased demand
for bread will require the exertions of 5<k 5
additional bakers.” Yes. and if they will
eat cake, with icing and figures of doves on
the top of it, there will be room for 1000
confectioners. Then if everybody has a 10-
course dinner, it will call for 5000 cooks
and 10,000 waiters, with 500 to make tooth-
picks. Why, it all seems quite easy now
that Hall's radiance has lit the dark.
DELIGHTFUL ECOYOMY.
The Acting-Premier is overjoyed at the fact that
his department has carried out certain works at New-
castle at under the estimated cost. —Sydney paper.
GRANNY: “My goodness! What are you
doing?”
THE OVERJOYED ACTING-PREMIER: "Hoo-
ray! Just spent an unnecessary mil-
lion and saved one and tuppence. Hoo-
ray!"
Now we know what The Argus means
when, mumbling over polities, it talks about
the wisdom of proceeding cautiously:—
Major-General Gorringe's army was within 17
miles of Kut-el-Amara when that place fell. Sime
that time the British have no doubt proceeded
cautiously, and apparently are now practically
where they were a month ago.
The Argus knows which is the greatest
machine ever invented. It is the treadmill,
of course.
All this ground and lofty oratory by our
Mr. Hughes about the Allies blotting out
the enemy with tariffs, boycotts, etc., can
bo summarised in one sentence: “The hand
that blocks the trade'll rule the world.”
WAR PROFITS.
THE CROWN PRINCE: “Yes, father, der cost
is terrible. But I must have enough to
make me a Field-Marshal’s uniform."
The War Pensions Act, as amended, pro-
vides that a married soldier totally incap-
acitated and with one child shall be paid
£2 10s. a week. If he has a second child
he gets an extra 10s. a week ; but he has to
have three more children before he gets
a second 10s. Apparently the Govern-
ment reckons that the parents of one or
two young Australians are entitled to con-
sideration, but that anything larger in the
way of a family is rather an impertinence.
The Vic. Chamber of Commerce has
resolved that the Federal Parliament be
urged to prohibit the importation of enemy
goods after the war, and to arrange a sys-
tem whereby neutral goods shall be certified
as being produced or manufactured in those
countries. And who is going to do the certi-
fying? The Customs were lately warned
about a consignment of ladies' fancy leather
bags. They were certified all right as
Yankee; but experts were ready to swear
they were German. Importers themselves
had doubts; but they were cheap, and that
settled the argument. The simple and dead-
easy solution is an unsealeable tariff; but.
then, how would the Chamber of Commerce
live?
In moving at the Hobart “Labor” Con-
gress a resolution expressing uncompromis-
ing hostility to the “conscription of life and
labor,” and declaring war against any
attempt to “foist conscription” on the people
of Australia, one Howard said -
When they thought of the teeming millions of
India and the teeming millions of Russia and of
the Allied countries, it would not make the slightest
difference, as far as the successful termination of the
war was concerned, if conscription of males was
brought about.
It is a pretty dreadful argument that we
ought not to bother about protecting our
liberties if anyone else will do it for us;
still it seemed to go with the “Labor” Con-
gress, for the motion was passed unani-
mously.
There is a man in Maoriland who cocked
his hat on three hairs when he heard about
John Bull going in for daylight saving. This
is T. K. Sidey, who sits in the House of
Reps, as member for Caversham. Nearly
every session for about 15 years Sidey. u
mild-spoken, faded-looking Liberal, solemnly
introduced his Daylight Saving Bill, and
after earnestly explaining its admirable fea-
tures saw it booted out. Daylight saving with
Sidey was a holy crusade, and had it not
been for the ponderous witticisms of the
bucolic Massey and the cow-cockies from
Taranaki he would by his sheer persistence
have carried the innovation long ago. But
faith in what J. Bull does is strong enough
to move mountains in M.L., and there will
be a loud howl directly from Dunedin for
national economy in candles and a knight-
hood for Tam Sidey.

Concerning the activities
of the Imperial Govern-
ment Supply Department
uf Maoriland. the dear old
lady declared the other day
that the affair “had demon-
strated the fact that a
Government department
can be run upon strictly
business lines,” the Admin-
istration having handled
over £10.000,000 worth of
meat and cheese since the
date mentioned, “to sav
nothing of the distribution
of butter to camps and
transports, and the scheel-
ite and kauri-gum exports.”
The timid old lady will yet be discovered
furtively offering the savage animal a saucer
of milk and the hearthrug in the drawing-
room.
The Speck s Public Works Department is
prepared to put up a good fight against
all-comers in a championship contest to
settle who is able to spend the maximum
amount of money on a given job with the
minimum result. Recently it built a
miles tramway to carry limestone from Mel-
rose to Devonport for the Broken Ilill Prop.
Co., and on this simple little job it managed
to work off nearly £3O.(XK) of borrowed coin.
One little incident which helped to this re-
sult was that an official or two were bowled
out in stuffing the paysheet and pocketing
the wages of non-existent toilers. About
the same time the Broken Hill Frop. itself
was building a very similar tramway for the
handling of its iron ore at Iron Knob (S.A.).
For about £70,000 it got 35 miles of tram-
way, a pier 180 ft. long ( with accessories for
the shipping of the ore) and a number of
cottages for its employees.
TIME.
London, Monday.—The daylight-saving came into
operation to-day, when all clocks in the United King-
dom were advanced one hour. — Cable.

THE PARABLE OF BLOGGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He had a decent job and liked
The racecour.se and the bar,
And did not mean to give these tip
To hasten to the war.
The powers agreed 't would not be right
To force young Willie Bloggs to fight !
He lived a pleasant sort of life;
His girl was passing fair;
His only worry was he had
Of late been losing hair.
His friends departed, one by one,
To make things nasty for the Hun.
Soon when recruiting posters met
His gaze, upon liis brow
Sat trouble; when lie read “Your King
And Country need you now !”
He sometimes felt his color rise,
And would not meet his Phoebe's eves.
It seemed to Bloggs, as time went on,
The whole world had conspired
To force him khaki cloth to don ;
Large jaws that never tired,
Allied with paunches round and sleek,
Urged him a uniform to seek.
The bar where, when his work was done,
lb garnered Bacchic fire
Was closed ; but still to fight the Hun
Was not young Bloggs' desire;
And e’en when racing was curtailed,
To seek enlistment Willie failed.
Next flappers whom he knew by sight
Approached him in the street
And gave him feathers large and white,
Instead of glances sweet.
He cleaned his pipe with them : but, still,
These presentations worried Will.
Young men in uniform arrayed
At corners he would meet.
Who asked him to enlist and made
Remarks anent his feet :
He swallowed insults by the score.
But loved his “freedom” more and more.
And then his firm began to think
Of sacking single men.
Although it caused his heart to sink,
Bloggs saw in marriage then
His only chance; and sorrow’s crown
He wore when Phoebe turned him down!
He still held out, until one day
The grand surrender came.
To the Town Hall he made his way
And handed in his name.
He shortly went to camp and then
Fair Phoebe's smiles were his again.
His bosses shook him by the hand,
Stout oldsters bought him beer,
Because, I trust you understand,
He was a volunteer — -
One of the kind that's worth much more
Than laggard “pressed” men by the score!
VICTORIA.
HARRISON O.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Pommy horses are earning more hay than
they can devour these times. Two of them got
away with the big prizes at the Adelaide meeting-
on Saturday, Polybius winning the City Handicap
(£750) and Cyklon helping himself to the £5OO
hung up for the Parkside Stakes. Polybius belongs
to Walter Hickenbotham, who seldom has anything
of his own much above the ordinary these days.
The last good one he had was Fidelio. Polybius
made no mistake and romped home three lengths
in front of the unlucky lownit, covering the seven
furlongs in lmin. 27Jsec. In Sydney alone this
season imported animals have won 21 races, includ-
ing the Challenge Stakes, with which Quinn’s Post
bolted at 50 to 1. Others, William the Silent and
Green Cap, were second in the Metropolitan Stakes
and Sydney Cup respectively.
'** * *
Anybody bringing a horse to Sydney from
Maoriland is the cause of awful suffering among
punters. They remember the memorable day on
which Maorilanders secured every event on the
programme at a big Randwick meeting. Barley
King is a case in point. Except that he showed
pace at Warwick Farm about two months ago there
was nothing to justify the take-vour-boots-off rush
which set in for him as soon as betting began on
rhe first division of the Park Stakes at Canterbury
( Sydney) on Saturday. Wild-eyed citizens swooped
into the Land of Israel waving such large wads that
Moses rushed for shelter and asked 6 to 4 against.
He finished fifth.
* * * *
The push which aids and abets the pony
owner by hanging up cash for him to chase is
making hay while the boom lasts. For last meeting
at Victoria Park (Sydney) 304 entries were re-
ceived. This is proudly claimed as “a record.” To
allow all the ponies a chance to start, the “sport”
was commenced at noon. The time is more than
ripe for something to he done about this sort of
thing. It is getting indecent.
* * * *
Randwick trainer Kelso and the lads
associated with his stable are having an innings.
Twice -within the past month Kelso has picked up a
double on outside courses with his own nags, and
his lads Pike and Meddiek have been kicking home
more than their share of winners. At Canterbury
Park (Sydney) on Saturday, Meddiek nearly per-
formed the hat trick. He was aboard Lace Front,
backed from twelves down to 5 to 1, in the Nursery,
and followed it up by steering Albuna to the
front in Canterbury Handicap. Had Valior not
shown so much toe in the Welter Mile Meddiek
would have been first to reach the judge on top of
the equal favorite Malheur. About the best perform-
ance of this boy was when he won the Melbourne
Cup on L. K. s! Mackinnon’s Kingshurgh. Only the
wonderfully fine condition of the mannikin enabled
the horse to down Sir Alwynton by a short neck in
record time. Meddiek is a ball of muscle and is
always as fit as a fiddle. Pike on Saturday won
the Flying Handicap on Herbie, who had not won
in these parts since landing a similar event a
couple of months hack. Herbie’s proprietor, V.
Berney, also got away with the second division of
the Park Stakes with Ruenalf Maid.
* * * *
Because the stewards didn’t like the way
she performed at Gosford recently Amuse has been
sent into retirement for a year with her owner
(McLean) and the jockey (McCabe) to keep her
company. Amuse was a fair performer. Some time
ago she ran a- close second to Willow Green in the
Rosehill Handicap, but a week later went one better
and appropriated the Moorefield Handicap. Early
in her career the frivolous damsel was trained at
Randwick, but crossed to Bananaland, where she won
several races. McCabe must have received the shock
of his life. He was in Brisbane with engagements
to fulfil on Saturday when the joyful news was sent
to him. Owner and rider are appealing to the
A.J.C. to have a look into the matter.
• * • *
Brisbane two-year-old Amberdown stands
out of the ruck. Ridden by Myles Connell the
youngster won the Stradbroke Handicap on Satur-
day, beating the consistent La Steppe by a head.
A' week earlier La Steppe finished just in front of
Amberdown in Brisbane Thousand, which was a
furlong further than Saturday’s dash. La Steppe
gave the winner a heap of weight—over three stone
—-so that the honors were with her. The only Syd-
ney nag to do any good at the meeting was Secret
Service. Ulva’s Isle could get no nearer than third
in the King’s Plate, w.f.a. This looks like some-
thing worth thinking over. Bananaland prads are
seldom good for an investment in Sydney, but on
their own ground nearly always manage to down
the cracks from outside. Apparently they are firm
State Righters.
t> * * * *
Paddy Piggott, a once famous jockey —
amongst other winners he rode Chester in the Mel-
bourne Cup and V.R.C. Derby of ’77 —is in Brisbane
Hospital. He was employed at Noud’s training
stables at Ascot. A horse trod upon his foot and
blood poisoning followed. Up to the time of the
accident the old-timer used to “ride work” in the
mornings, and wouldn’t knock off until his hoof
became too bad to stand oh.
* * * *
The Australian-liorse boom in racing in
India looks like a thing of the past. At the Cal-
cutta meeting on February 5 only five of the 39
runners hailed from these parts. At the meeting a
week later only three out of the 34 were of Aus-
tralian birth and pedigree. India is no longer a
profitable market for Australian prads, the slump
being due to several sultry-priced purchases having
been easily downed by cheap animals from Fogland.
So the fashion just now is to drag horses from the
North, the idea being that they are both better and
cheaper than those from the South, and the brutal
truth is that they are.
Gilgandra (X.S.W.) Jockey Club is
going to run a dry race meeting next month, partly
because Bung declined to give more than £7 10s.
for the right to make it wet, and partly “because
it would be a blessing to the bookmakers not to be
delayed in their work by persons under the influence
of drink.” Meanwhile the local Stiggins is won-
dering whether these reasons are a revelation of
piety or hypocrisy. But Ikey won’t mind. He’ll get
the lot now.
Over in the Speck something different is happen-
ing. There the Devonport Racing Club is inviting
Bung to tender for the irrigation rights, it .being
explained that this is necessary because the official
auctioneer is ill in bed and the other auctioneers
in the district, being on the: waggqn, “have con-
scientious objections to doing business” with anj
thing in the soda.

Two good horsemen passed from ietorian
courses lately—Charley Wakefield, amateur, ki e
in a pony-race at Yarra Glen; and J. 1 .
pro., fatally damaged by falling with Expeditio e
at Epsom. Wakefield made his first stir by riding
Quarry Park a winner at 100 to 3, and set the stipes
looking to see if he had a battery concealed in his
ear or somewhere. Edwards was one of the bo e s
cross-timber riders in Vic., and in bygone years gare
some dashing displays on Leah Kleshna. He was a
rare judge of pace and won many of his races in
the run over the last hurdle. His two biggest
wins were on Zephuron, in the 1 .R C. Granu
National Steeple a couple of seasons ago, and on
Battleship at Caulfield. Edwards had a nasty ride
recently at Mentone when Expeditioner, a good
jumper when he liked and a rotten one yyhen he
didn’t, ran off and then bolted. Edwards pulled
the brute on to the training track, then back on to
the steeple course, and there managed to stop up.
It’s a desperate game riding over fences, and to
to the top men have to take a lot of risks that
punters never notice. #
Died recently, a very ancient English
jockey who rode a historical second for the Derby.
It was in 1857, and Jim Covey’s mount was a rank
outsider—Black Tommy, owned by a miserable old
hunks named Drinkald. Black Tommy had been
treated as a joke. One of the bets laid against him
yvas £2OOO to a suit of clothes, Drinkald stipulating
that the togs should be inexpensive. He posed as
the infatuated owner of a tenth-rate prad yvho was
running it in the Derbv to please his vanity. Rut
when the field came flashing down the straight with
Black Tommy in front, Drinkald’s wicked old head
lost its cunning. The dark horse seemed to get
first past the post, and Drinkald threw up his hat
and chuckled aloud: “Won a hundred thousand
pounds, by God, and not another soul has got a
penny on it!” His delight in bis own good fortune
was less pronounced than his joy in the thought
that he had no friends to share it. Then he found
that he had viewed the finish from a flattering
angle. The judge decided that another outsider had
pipped Black Tommv on the post. The immortal
turf story of Drinkald’s fond delusion must have
given origin to the axiom that you never can tell
till the numbers are up.
* * * *
Randwick, which is popularly regarded as
the home of horse owners, trainers, punters, bookies
and jockeys, leads the Sydney Metropolitan area in
rifle shooting, supplying seven teams to the three
grades of the championship. Four of these are from
the Randwick Literary Institute. It seems to be
an unusually militant kind of Institute.
* * * *
A Yankee writer recently mentioned three
marvellous cases of racing horses showing excep-
tional form on the turf after meeting with bad acci-
dents. The famous Waterbird broke his hip-bone
near the socket when running in a race at Morris
Park (U.S.A.), and although it was thought he
would have to be destroyed, he was patched up and
the following season became champion of the Yankee
turf. When running in the Saratoga Handicap of
1903 a nag named South Trimble was thrown against
the rails and had his skull so badly smashed that
his owner was going to destroy him. But a cley-er
vet. objected and made an astonishing save. The
following year South Trimble appeared in public
with a silver plate where his frontal bone used to
be, and it didn’t prevent him from winning a lot
of good races. Another great prad got so much
knocked about that it was decided to remove about
an inch of the metacarpal nerve—a very ticklish
operation. It saved the moke and he proved a
better speed animal the next season than he had
ever been before.
* * * *
The Maori’s hospitality to the pakelia,
once the latter has ingratiated himself into the
native’s confidence, is as limitless as the seas, and
is often carried so far as to be positively injurious
to the Maori himself. For instance: A native race
meeting was organised at Otaki. Maoris acted in
all capacities, from judge to jockey. But a pakeha
trainer lived at Otaki, and he was beloved and
honored with the respect due to a king of the turf,
especially as he rode his own horses at the local
meeting. • Qn these occasions he was the victim of
much lionising, and when he breasted the tape he
yvas invariably- given the inside running. One race
day the native starter, in a spontaneous outburst of
enthusiasm for the white race, determined to break
all precedents and shoyv his appreciation in a tangible
way. A race yvas about to start, and the usual
confusion yvas being experienced at the post. But
the starter only had eyes for the white man, and,
regardless of the Maori-ridden prads, he waited until
the pakeha and his nag had secured the best position.
Then, fearful lest the other horses might suddenly
come into the line, he cried: “Are you ready,
Charlie?” and Charlie nodding in the affirmative, the
starter y-elled “Go!”

“Barcoo”: Fur the .frnre article give me
amateur grass-fed racing. A year or so ago, at one
of the leading centres on the Central Queensland
railyvay, the horses were paddocked for the forth-
coming congregation of “sports.” Two horses be-
longing to friends were exactly alike in color, con-
formation and height. The 'brands were alike as to
the first letter and numeral, -and after a little
scientific plucking the two were almost undistinguish-
able. One nag was to carry almost top weight; the
other was near the minimum. When the races came
off the lightweight accepted. He romped off with
the “double,” and left by train for the “siding’
where the other had been turned out. The oyvner
removed a white star from the prad’s forehead
before releasing him.

Mick King was in fine nick for sprinting
on Saturday night at Melbourne, and made the
elderly-looking Fritz Holland do a strenuous run.
Fritz' wanted fight and hinted to this effect with
solid rights to the head and body; but Mick yvas
usnallv half-way to somewhere else yvhen they
arrived and took delivery yvhen the sting had evap-
orated. Holland yvas leading at half-time, but at
this stage Michael commenced dropping his left
into Fritz’s face, lifting himself away each time
with great precipitancy. The harder Fritz tore into
the frav the more abrupt was the bump. Result.
Verdict" for Mick. Holland had the worst of the
luck on the toss up. It was one of those disturb-
ances in which judgment hinged on whether the
referee thought most of Continued aggressiveness or
defence in retreat. Anyhow, it was up to Mick to
have a win. # *

ber, quickly-shift-
ing Englishman
W'as not to be
rushed with the
kind of stuff
McCoy usually wins
with." His left is
an awkward thing
to get past, and a right that
is swift to move looks un-
pleasant to meet. The fre-
quent arrival of that left in
the commissariat department
and its habit of suddenly
appearing on the end of
his nose warned McCoy to
beware. For the first half
of the scrap the Australian,
though beaten, held his end
up fairly well. The rest
of the journey was a pil-
grimage of misery. In the
thirteenth and fourteenth
rounds he was on the verge
of being passed out, and
only weathered the rest of
the* gale by rapidly retreat-
ing and hanging on. By
the end of the twentieth he
had t n ken a rare hiding and
■was mighty glad to adiourn.
Edwards has not the same
kick as McCoy, but as a
boxer he is an artist.

The Rev. P. Mullineux, who captained
the English football team to Australia in the late
’9o’s, turned up in Wellington (M.L.) the other
day, having worked his way over from Frisco on
the Moana in order to enlist. He has been sky-
piloting among the seamen of ’Frisco for some time
past. When he couldn’t stand it any longer, he
approached his Bishop for leave to quit. Ihe
Bishop said no —he looked to “Mull” for the finances
of the mission. “Mull” then asked if he would be
allowed to get awav if the. sugar was guaranteed
for a vear, and the Bish said, “My oath, ’ or some-
thing like that. So the game little half-back chucked
in his salary for the year, roped in other funds,
and, penniless, approached Capt. Stringer for a job
on the Moana. Stringer gave him a berth for ard
and, right through, the skv-pilot took his watch
with the scrub-broom or down below' with the shovel.
Said he thought he knew the sailor-man through
and through, but found it was only his shore-going
veneer he knew. Now he knows him right to
the backbone, and reckons his was a profitable trip.
He is wearing the khaki of a private in the M.L.
ambulance now.

Jim Cameron, the Glebe (Sydney) foot-
baller who has “signed on” for the third time,
sniffed powder at Rabaul and Gallipoli. It w T as_ a
Turkish bomb which sent him back to N.S.W. ;
now, patched up, he is off for another dash towards
the goal posts.
* * * *
Troubles with uproarious players in
N.S.W. Rugby League are not going to end with
milk-and-water chastisement. On successive week-
ends unruly offenders have been ordered off by the
referee; but the League blessed one erring brother
with a week’s stand-down, and the worst that two
others received was a caution. On Saturday another
player was sent off. How about giving him a purse
of sovereigns ? Unless the League does the sensible
thing it will find the heroes who use their fists
instead of kicking the ball increasingly difficult to
handle.
* * * *
N.S.W. Rugby League’s policy of cutting
out 10 per cent, of the “gate” for patriotic purposes
is turning in a fair handful. Four rounds have
already gathered about £150; but if the affluent
League had trebled the percentage to help those who
are doing their bit with the gun instead of booting
a football, it would have achieved something better
worth throwing bouquets about.
* * * *
As a trooper in a Light Horse regiment
Albert Cotter is bowling nearly as well as he ever
did in his civilian days. He began the season in
Cairo by getting five wickets for 15 against the
Gezira Sporting Club. That is not to sav that the
club is weak. Cotter's regiment, which contains
at least three other first-class cricketers, only won
by 26 runs. <

“Myhn” : Exit the last of the male mem-
bers of the most famous cricket family the world
has known. Dr. H. M. Grace, the father, ceased to
prescribe for his patients as far back as 1871. His
enthusiasm for the game knew no bounds. George
Frederick, the baby, followed in 1880, aged 30 just
a fortnight after making “a pair” in the first test
match played in England. It was in this match
that W.G. made his famous 152, and Billv u,
doc-h went better with 153 not out. Henry the
firstborn, was the next to cease troubling the'scnJi
—Nov., 1895, aged 62. Then there was a 2
break till 1911, when Edward Mills, 69, i n a wav
as remarkable a player as W.G., had a trip
Charon’s Ferry ; and William Gilbert, the Great fin
ished his innings in October, 1915. Alfred ’ the
second son, kept going till a few days ago, when hr
innings closed at 76. He was thus'the patriarch nt
the family. W.G., E.M. and G.F. played in
tests; Henry and Alfred were good batsmen' in less
important company in earlier days. Four out of
the five were doctors.

Jack Marsh, outed at Orange last week
was the greatest bowler N.S.W. cricket ever s hel’
tered. But he was a darky troubled with manners
which white brother found it often impossible to
put up with. In Svdnc.v premier cricket he captured
the greatest number of wickets three years in succes-
sion (1901-1901). In 23 premierships the oniv
other trundlers to get the “greatest wickets” honor
more than once have been S. Austin (’95-’96 and
’97-’9B), and A. J. Bowden (1906-7 and 1908-9)
His ability to bowl straight and fast was uncannv
In the professional ped. boom days of the ’9o's
brothers Larry and Jack Marsh were “even-timers”’
who boiled over many a pot. Lithe, and a bundle
of muscle, Jack Marsh was an athlete of great possi-
bilities, if his enemy could have been cgst into
darkness among thousands of other devils.
Marsh had been on a bad wicket this last few years,
wandering round the country mostly, looking for
work without much enthusiasm. Six weeks ago he
struck Orange. One night last week he turned up in
a billiard room. It is alleged that he started to
throw things about. Anyhow, the next that trans-
pired was a badly knocked about blackfellow at the
hospital just in time to die. Subsequently a couple
of -arrests were made. Jack was bom at Yugilbar
(N.S.W.) and his innings closed at 42.

cycle championship at Rooty Hill (N.S.W.) gave

Wiekham, the machine at one part of the trial
whirled along at 80 miles an hour and afterwards,
from a standing start, put a half-mile behind in
35sec.
* * * *
A fine tribute to Anthony Wilding was
given by a French Cabinet Minister when the war
was only threatening: “Many of our young men,
fired by his example, have added at least five years
to their lives since they began to use the punchball,
run on the track and eschew late hours at caffe. We
in France have always envied the British method of
physical training, of inculcating discipline by games.
In a sudden emergency, such as the mobilisation of
our young manhood, this factor would be of supreme
value.” Wallis Myers, the English veteran, analyses
and compares Brookes and Wilding in his biography
of the lat'ter. “Brookes had more inherent genius. He
was the artist, Anthony the athlete... .Brookes may
be described as a born player; Wilding was essen-
tially a player brought to maturity by assiduous
practice.” On a hard court Wilding was practically
unbeatable. On grass his extraordinary physical
fitness —he had no vices and no “parlor tricks” —was
always liable to bring him through in any company.
Wilding won the world's championship at Winibledon
four times, but always with Brookes away. “Norman
is perfectly' sure,” he said himself, “both before and
during a match, that nothing but some dreadful catas-
trophe could ever cheat him of victory.” So, when a
great English authority says that he “can only call
to mind one who was so evidently confident and self-
reliant as Wilding,” it must be the other Australian
champion that he meant. Their last time together
in U.S.A., the week after war was declared—they
won back the Davis Cup for Australasia, after beating
a jolly pair of Germans —who were gathered in by a
British warship on their way home afterwards. Then
the one other great interest of Wilding's life, motor-
ing, took him to the British transport-service in
France, where he did fine work till he was killed.
The cheap animal goes on its conquering
way! At the big Noo Yark dog show lately an
Airedale terrier named Vickery Amen Gem, compet-
ing against many famous tykes that had cost hun-
dreds of quids, was declared to be the best in her
class. Y’et the lady’s pedigree was a blank, one
had been bought bv the exhibitor in a London dog-
pound for 16s. She won the first time she was
exhibited, and now bags what is practically the cha -
pionship of the planet. And why shouldnt
cheap quadruped do big things, anyhow? The cheap
man elbows his way through mighty often.

Maoriland’s Old Age Pensions Dept, got
a shock the other day. It noticed that a
pensioner, 67 years old. had not drawn his
money for over a year. When it started
to inquire if he had come into a fortune or
was merely dead, it discovered that the
ancient had enlisted as a youth of 44, had
got past the doctor, scrambled through the
training-camp at Trentham and gone away
to fight at Gallipoli with Maoriland’s sth
Reinforcements. He lasted through the
disturbance on the famous peninsula until
the evacuation, and is probably now in
France bashing Germans. A warrior of his
type makes the hard-and-fast age limit look
silly.

Melbourne's new theatre, The Playhouse
(it used to be the Snowden Picture Theatre
and is on the south side of Prince’s Bridge),
will be opened on June 10 by the Repertory
people with G. B. Pshaw's "Man and Super-
man,” in aid of the French Red Cross. A
fortnight later (June 24) the first repertory
season will begin with H. A. Vachell’s
“Quinney’s,” which reads well and was a
big success in London. Later in the season
"Yellow and White,” by E. H. Oliphant,
who helps to run the Mining Standard
and specialises in Hun-hatred, will be put
on.
“Monkbarns”: Reference was made by a
Pointer (B. 27/4/Y6) to Edward Emery act-
ing in "Treasure Island” at New York. He is
the son of Sam Emery, and brother of
Winifred (Mrs. Cyril Maude), both of
whom visited Australia. Sam Emery’s fav-
orite yarn was about his own birth. His
father, John Emery, was a distinguished
London comedian. One night “Pizarro" was
acted at Drury Lane, with John Kemble as
Rolla. Mrs. Siddons as Elvira and Mrs. Jor-
dan as Cora. The end of the third act
seemed to be bungled somehow. When the
curtain fell, Kemble came forward in a
rage and said: "Ladies and gentlemen, I
regret that the finale of that act was spoilt
through the non-arrival of Mr. Emery as the
Sentinel.” Emery then rushed panting
before the curtain, and exclaimed: "Ladies
and gentlemen, you will excuse me when I
say that 15 minutes ago I became the happy
father of a squ.” Excused, of course, and
even Kemble burst out laughing.
“Tito” : The only pub was miles away over
the moonlit hills; but that didn't matter,
because every man who came to the darnce
had a bottle or two planted in a hollow log
or behind a prickly-pear bush. Now that the
city pubs are being shut like oysters at the
commencement of the evening, why don’t the
theatrical managers bring the idea to town
and have a hollow-log room for the con-
venience of their parched customers?
“Glen H.”: At least three English plays
have exceeded 1000 performances each—"A
Chinese Honeymoon” (1075). "Our Boys”
(1362) and “Charley’s Aunt” (1466). Are
there any others?
Some idea of what the flicker picture
business must mean to L ncle Wilson can be
gained from the fact that the industry occu-
pies fifth place amongst Yankee manufac-
tures. £100.000,000 worth of capital is
invested in it and it gives permanent emplov-
ment to over 100.000 people, several regi-
ments of which make faces all day. The
average daily attendance of the nation at
the flicker palaces is reckoned to be not less
than 15 millions.
“Found drowned” ends the record of
Graham Moore. Australian musician and
pianist. Born at Ballarat 50-odd years ago,
he learnt a lot in his native land and then
went to England, where he became a pro-
fessor of the piano at the Royal Academy
of Music. He edited a lot of works for his
instrument, and visited Australia about 15
years ago as an examiner for the R.A.M,
Returning to Melbourne again a few months
back, he got a chair at Fritz Hart’s conser-
vatorium. But somehow pupils did not seem
to want him. and he talked about getting
off to England. But he gave the coroner a
job instead.
“8.J.”: "C. Ockney” mentions (B.
IS 5/16) that actor Charles Wyndham,
who has just married, is "over 70.” He is
indeed. Two years hence he will be over
'Rosna (London) : Willie Murdoch, the
sane-haired Victorian pianist, has been mak-
ing his best noises in "the Provinces." and
even gave a concert in London on April 5.
He sp irts a khaki armlet nowadays as evi-
dence that he's ready to go and pot Germans
when George Rex gets short of warriors. In
fact, there are mighty few of the slacker
brigade among 'Roolanders in London, and
there would be fewer still if the Comm on -
wealth would come down from its elevated
steed and permit Australians to join their
own army. Maorilanders and Canadians in
London may link up with their own con-
tingents. but Australians must fling them-
selves into a British regiment and can only
join the Australian army by paying their
own fares back to the Commonwealth to do
it. And we flatter ourselves we don't grow
red tape in the Commonwealth !
"Private Boxer ': Haddon Chambers, late
of Sydney, mostly of America these days, is,
like a number of his fellow-playwrights, very
quiet just now as far as new productions
are concerned. But. not to be idle, he has
started brushing up the old stuff. Recently
New York staged, mostly with British
mimes, “The Great Pursuit.” with Cham-
bers’s name attached. It turns out to be an
old-time success. “The Idler,” which Haddon
has re-written and brought up to date.
THE CIGAR ACTOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One time the villain in the play
Was glad a simple smoke to get;
He chased the maiden all the way
From rural bliss and meadow hay
To gilded halls in London set
On one pale, little cigarette.
But now he apes the Yankee dart—
A black cigar he needs must bring
To work upon dramatic art.
And not content to play the part
Of smoker, see, with roll and swing,
He fairly eats the awful thing!
In every play the peeler’s plight
Is demonstrated by the fuss
The pro. makes with cigars. I quite
Believe he eats a score a night.
He chews, and champs, and gulps—the cuss !
With motions simply ravenous.
I hoped this pro. of stage and screen
With smoker's heart would be attacked, or
Destroyed with subtle nicotine!
No chance! Just recently I’ve seen
This shop-ad. of a sweetmeat factor:
“Cigars of Chocolate for the Actor!”
VICTORIA.

every speck and smear and
scratch. There is only one
cleaner that cleans perfectly
enough to be used on mirrors
—that is Bon Ami. This is a

apply a thin, white lather of
Bon Ami on the glass, let it
dry and wipe it off.
It will not scratch. It is not
oily. It leaves no smears be-
cause it comes off dry.
Bon Ami is the only cleaner
in its class. It is a special
cleaner for mirrors, windows,
polished metals and enamel.
It is not for hard scouring, but
just for cleaning and polish-
ing smooth surfaces which are
too delicate to be cleaned by
scouring soaps.

How to Treat Your Hair and Complexion.
A Few Simple Beauty Hints.
By Mdlle. GABY DESLYS, the well-known Parisian Actress.
YOU ask me for a few hints on the
treatment of the hair and complexion.
Well, the less “treatment” you give
the skin the better. I do not
believe much in massage, but a
little cream to the face is neces-
sary to counteract the effects of
wind or sun. What cream
would I recommend? Well, I
advise you to use a little mer-
colised wax every night and
again in the morning, after
washing the face. Rub it
gently into the skin, then wipe
off any superfluous wax and
dust a little barri-agar over
the face. You ■ will find that
this will be the only “treat-
ment” necessary and will keep
your face fresh and youthful-
looking for all your life. The mercolised
wax removes all the dead outer skin, so
that you have always a fair, fresh com-
plexion like a girl’s.
For the hair, the first and most important
Photo: Wrather A Buys.
thing is a good shampoo. Never use any-
thing inferior to wash the hair with. Get
some good stallax from your chemist and use,
a teaspoonful in a cup of hot’
water. Then rinse the hair
well and it will look bright and
glossy.
A tonic is necessary when the
hair is inclined to fall out more
than it should, and is always
good to use during the spring
and- autumn. Then the hair
needs a little —what do you call
it? —stimulant, and for this 1
would advise you to get a packet
of boranium and mix it with
some bay rum: dab this into the
roots and it will not only stop
the fall, but make your hair
grow long and thick. Give your
hair a good brushing every night, and that
will be all that you need do.
Blackheads Fly Away.
Instantaneous Remedy for Blackheads, Greasy Skin and Enlarged Pores.
A practically instantaneous remedy for
blackheads, greasy skins and enlarged pores,
recently discovered, is now coming into
general use in the boudoir. It is very
simple, harmless and pleasant. Drop a
stymol tablet, obtained at the chemist’s, in
a tumblerful of hot water. After the effer-
vescence has subsided bathe the face in the
liquid, using a small sponge or soft cloth.
. Qrey Hair—Home Remedy.
An old-fashioned Recipe restores Youthful Appearance.
In a few minutes dry the face and the offen-
sive blackheads will come right off on the
towel. Also the large oily pores immedi-
ately close up and efface themselves natu-
rally. The greasiness disappears and the
skin is left smooth, soft and cool/ This
simple treatment is then repeated a few
times at intervals of four or five days, to
ensure the permanence of the result.
There are plenty of reasons why grey
hair is not desirable, and plenty of reasons
why hair dyes should not be used. But, on
the other hand, there is no reason why you
should have grey hair if you do not want
it. To turn the hair back to a natural color
is really a very simple matter. One has
only to get from the chemist two ounces of
concentrate of tammalite and mix it
three ounces of bay rum. Apply to t h
hair with a small sponge for a few mg s
and the greyness will gradually disappea ■
This liquid is not sticky or greasy and does
not injure the hair in any way. It has o
used for generations with most satisfacto
results by those who have known
formula.

“Copy Boy”: “Nemo” (B. 18/5/’l6) is
in error in speaking of Dave Watterston as
the oldest member of Melbourne Yorick Club
and in referring to Bartrop as one of the
original members. The club was formed in
1868, Watterston joined the following year
and Major Bartrop not till 1873. Three of
the original members who survive are Byron
Moore, the bearded Bajah of Flemington;
R. S. Smythe and Tom Carrington, art
editor of the Australasian and dramatic
critic of the Abgus. Among the founders of
the club were Marcus Clarke (who was its
first hon. sec.), Dr. J. E. Neild (then
dramatic critic of the Australasian), G.
W. Rusden (the first Victorian Clerk of
Parliaments), George Walstab and Howard
Willoughby.
“J.F.H.’ (London): Dropped out, the
London Standard, long-time a prosperous
morning paper, and recognised official organ
of the Conservative party. It was known
to be in difficulties for some months past;
and on St. Patrick’s Day nobody would
finance it any further, so editor W. A. Wood-
ward called his staff together and drowned
it in the inkpot. As the Standard was
started as an evening paper 80 years ago to
oppose Catholic Emancipation, it was a case
of the whirligig of time having its revenge
on the 17th of March. The usually stodgy
old thing reached its zenith under the 30
years’ editorship of W. H. Mudford, when
its profits were £lOO.OOO a year. The late
Marquis of Salisbury wrote its political
leaders for six years. After he became Prime
Minister he was going into the House of
Lords one afternoon when he caught sight
of an old chum of the Standard editorial
room. “Hello, Billy,” he exclaimed, “who
pays for the beer to-night?” “Billy” reckoned
it was Salisbury’s turn, so they went round
to the House of Lords’ dining-room, and had
—I don’t think either of them remembered
what.

IN BANANA LAND
“Barcoo”: Big shouts? Blackall, after
25 years of agitation and political tug-of-
war, got its railway. A week of high car-
nival was topped off with the toasts of
"Success to Blackall” and “The Wool
Industry.” To do the first in style Arthur
Whittingham. godfather of the hamlet,
ordered up 200 large bottles of champagne ;
and Harry Hart, of Springfield, on the
Barcoo, followed his friend’s example to
the glory of the jumbucks.
J. S. Badger, the autocrat of Brisbane
Tramways, is looking for trouble again.
Hill, M.L.A. of S. Aus., and several other
officials of the Australian Tramway
Employees' Association, dropped in to dis-
cuss a few matters the other day. The
’Murkan potentate, however,, declined to dis-
cuss anything, and further refused permis-
sion for Hill to inspect the Countess-street
depot. Later on he explained :
The matter of the wages of our own men is at
present under consideration, and we do not want any
outside assistance on the question.
Which is a splendid eruption from the man
who had the assistance of armed police when
things were not going quite his way in
1912.
There lias been a rumpus among the
clerks of Brisbane over the anti-conscrip-
tion resolution adopted by the recent con-
ference of quill-drivers in Sydney. Collings,
who sponsored the fatuous motion, got
hack to Brisbane just in time to find his
action publicly disowned by ten members of
his union. Thereat Colli ngs put an extra
point on his whiskers and berated the ten
decent Australians for “disloyalty” to their
union—loyalty to their couutry being, of
course, a matter of no consequence. The
alarmed half-score wobbled at the knees and
apologised for expressing their views out-
side the union. Now it is announced that
the loyal band have been suspended from
membership. It’s all very diverting; but
what sort of a condition of affairs is union-
ism creating when a man is throttled for
putting up a patriotic shout when it is most
needed ?
“L.T.”: Toowong Cem'otery, Brisbane, is
on a high ridge, and the ground is so hard
that a grave takes some time to sink. In
slack days the' cemetery staff therefore digs
graves on spec., and generally has a few
“empties” on hand in case of a rush. Which
reminds me of the early days of Cue. on
the Murchison goldfields (W.A.). There
were only two divisions for the dead —the
Catholic portion, on a siding of hard iron-
stone gravel, and the Protestants’ allotment,
in sandy loam. If there was any doubt as
to the religion of the deceased the under-
taker always made him a Baptist.
“Chink”: There rolled into a Brisbane
fish-stall the other day a mackerel, from
Wynnum waters, which broke the market
record by registering 421 b. Keeping it com-
pany was a jew of 601 b. They were almost
black with age and riotous living. Queens-
land waters are turning out an extraordi-
nary number of fish just now. On a recent
night the mart took in 1000 cases and trays,
approximately 55,0001 b. The deplorable
part of the business is that the hapless
householder still has to pay 9d. per lb. for
the food. The fish unsold at that price rots
and is returned to the sea with the city
refuse. We’re a great and mighty people
for economies in war time.
Mrs. Boardinghouse is not so particular
about her paying guests up North. This
from the Rockhampton Bulletin :
Vacancies 3 Gentlemen, shady side, close Trams
Even the patriotic hat hasn’t exhausted
the generous spirit of the Westerners. At
Duiacca recently, during the progress of an
entertainment, a house was totally destroyed
by fire. Promptly the hat was passed round
the 40 persons present and £5O was handed
over to help replace the sticks.
It seems that the Queensland Railways,
by turning down the Australian linseed oil
in favor of the Indian article, made, not a
penny per gallon, but a whole threepence.
This was a magnificent achievement. If
only we were all as careful of the pence
we should get our clothes from China (and
Germany as soon as the war is over), and
our railway engines and rolling stock and
ships and machinery from Japan, and our
sugar from Java, and our butter from
Russia and our maize from India. Then
we shouldn’t want any railways to speak
of, and could do without sugar farms and
mills and butter factories and woollen
mills and all the rest of it. In fact, if the
fine, patriotic lead of the Queensland Rail-
ways were followed, we could save three-
pence and do without Australia.
THE IMPETUOSITY OF THE RAVE.
P AT: “ Don't he ’walkin' so fast, Mary."
M ARY: "An' why not?"
P AT: "Sure, we’ll he home before we’re half-way there."
Barrister Hart has abandoned his .wig and
a cartload of briefs to take part in the Big
Argument. Learned counsel did not talk
much of what younger men should do, but
packed the wife and bairns safely away
and promptly went into Raining.
“North Q.”: The talk of big Queensland
“shouts” recalls to old residents of the
Maranoa an election fight of 30 years ago.
One candidate hired a special train, loaded
it with barrels of beer and sundry tangle-
foot, and ran it from one end of the elec-
torate to tlie other over the line which was
then in course of construction. The outing-
cost £5OO.
The Government effects a saving of about
£OOO per annum by abolishing the job of
Medical Inspector for North Queensland.
The office was established at Townsville in
1912 in response to urgent representations
of the then Commissioner, Dr. J. C. S.
Elkington. It started well, but its authority
to act was gradually withdrawn, until when
the axe arrived the medical officer in charge
had practically nothing to do with Public
Health" administration. The apparent fail-
ure of the Townsville office was not due to
lack either of possibilities in the North or
of effort on the part of the men who have
held the job, but to the inept administration
of the Health Department.
INTERLUDE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Love is but a scented kiss
On a wine-bowl’s rim
When two lips go “Ps-s”—like this —
And the light is dim.
Bertram, kiss me once again!
Damn! Here’s Auntie Kate.
Hope is ever cut in twain
By the swish of Fate.
QUEENSLAND,
ZORA CROSS.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, May 27, 1916.
My Dear Thargomindaii.—
Brisbane adjourned to Eagle Farm to-day
to see the Stradbroke Handicap run. His
Ex., who is human enough to believe the
war can be won even though he patronises
the gee-gees, brought his missus along in a
cream coat and skirt, and a black hat with
a crown of corn-colored brocade. Mrs.
Cosens backed winners in a coat and skirt
of fiavy serge and a blue hat trimmed with
flat nink velvet camellias. Mrs. Stuart
Hyslop was a delightful autumn study in
green and brown. Mostly the gathering was
tailor-made, toqued and furred and clinging
to the taffeta garments of waning autumn.
The chief interest in a Spin tea held in
the grounds of the Junction Park State
School this afternoon (proceeds to the Red
Cross) was a match between the Stephens
Shire riflemen and the ladies of the Bris-
bane Miniature Rifle Club. And what do
you think ! We won; hut to save the feel-
ings of the vanquished heroes I won’t men-
tion the extent of the slaughter. Doesn’t it
conclusively prove that we should be allowed
to go to the Front?
On Wednesday night, at the Glideway
Skating Rink, a carnival is to he held in
aid of the War Nurses’ Fund, which was
started by some returned Billjim admirers.
On Empire Day the veterans of the
Crimea, the Mutiny, the Maori war and
other ancient scraps were mobilised and
ordered to assault a dinner provided by
the Defence Department. The Gov. made
a speech; and the spokesmen for the
glories of the past were Capt. Vernon, of
the 88th Connaught Rangers, and an old
salt who had seen service before the A.I.F.
band which was tuning up on the verandah
was born.
Among the soldiers who returned from
Gallipoli too seriously wounded for further
active service was an Irishman who had
seen trouble in S’Africa and on the Indian
frontier. He "ot a billet in an office, and
the curious asked him to show them his
museum of war medals. The old chap un-
comfortably explained that Uncle Cohen
borrowed them many years ago and had not
returned them. A week later he was called
before a full gathering of the staff and
handed his trinkets, which had bee
recovered by the law and the profits— the
latter contributed by the staff.
A deputation of ladies, headed bv tin-
Mayoress (Mrs. Hetherington ), dropped in
on acting-Chief Sec. Hunter during the
week and asked him what he W as
going to do about the proposed Residential
Club for Returned Soldiers. The deputation
was invited to return when it had something
more definite to put forward. In the mean-
time he guaranteed the sympathy of the'
Government. The club should be open for
business in a couple of months.
Wounded Anzaes in England, while appre-
ciating all the hospitality that is shown
them, weary of. the rigid class distinctions
A party of Bananalanders, who had been
marked by a titled dame as her own exclu-
sive property, hit on a bright scheme for
escaping the aristocratic frills. One dav
they were all lined up ready for motoring
and as each Tommy Kangaroo passed into
tin; car he grasped the hand of the waiting
.Teames, and greeted him with a “Goodav.
Bill.” They also persisted iu. including
Jcames in the conversation, until his kneo
buckles rattled with self-consciousness. All
parades in that motor-car were from that
awful day cancelled.
Recently returned from England Alice
Davenport, a Brisbane girl with a beautiful
voice that has been cultivated by some of
the best of the world’s masters. Those
who remember her voice as it was before
the special cultivation began are eagerly
waiting for her first concert.
The Brisbane Operatic Players fare-
welled Mrs. Arthur Hunter last Monday
night and put silver-backed brushes on her
table. She returns about the end of August
to coax the 8.0.P.’s “La Mascotte” into
shape.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
Concerning a Northern row:
The blundering military authorities and the
Churc-h of England are having a scrap now. Some
weeks ago the Church of England opened a hut in
one of the camps, and at the opening the chief pilot
delivered himself on the subject of the Department
refusing to allow the Church to send unpaid chap-
lains on troopships. Tommy Dodds happened to be
in Brisbane, and rushed into print. The chaplain
prepared an answer, but found himself against a
dead wall. He could not get publication in the
papers. Subsequently Archbishop Donaldson, the
Senior Chaplain, applied for permission to publish a
statement. This also was refused. Then the
smouldering fire burst out in a number of places,
Women, mothers and other relatives.of soldiers gof
to work, wrote all over the country, got the clergy
to work, and even the inoffensive elderly
bishops were stirred to activity. The net
result is that the Military Department will
now have something sizeable to handle. The chap-
lain who started the hubbub is well known all over
Australia —Garland by name, the most successful
recruiter in Queensland.
Brisbane City Council recently practised
its bit of war economy by raising the
salaries of a number of officials get-
ting more than £4OO a year. No one
knew the reason until Aid. Diddams
announced “that these officials eat
better food than the laborers.” Eating capa-
city thus seems to be the test of a civic ser-
vant's value —not earning capacity. That
being so, “A fair round belly with good
capon lined'* would be an appropriate in-
scription for the portals of the new Town
Hall that is coming by and bye.

People really are buying Fords
FRANS-AUSTRALIAN MOTOR TRIP.
With no more demonstration than the noise made by his own “cut out,” Francis Rirtles,
the exploring motorist, left the G.P.0., Melbourne, at 7.30 p.m., on 20th March. 1910, for
the Northern Territory. Birtles, who is accompanied by his brother Clive and his dog
“Chance It,” expects to be away for about 12 months. He is driving the FORD CAR
which has already taken him 37,000 miles in Trans-Australian trips. His intention is to
proceed north to the Gulf of Carpentaria, and round the coast to Darwin. He will spend
some time in prospecting about the King Leopold Ranges in North-western Australia, and
he carries an assaying plant to assist him in his work. The back of the car was loaded up
with petrol, and a complete equipment for the tour, including a motion-picture camera.
This is Birtles’s sixth Trans-Continental trip. —Melbourne press.

The Inky Way.
“Deo et Litteris”: Space for the In
Memoriam of one who, at the age of 20
years, was prevented by a Turkish bullet,
10 days after his landing at Anzac, from
filling jais destined place among Australia’s
press poets! He was Corporal J. D. Burns,
sometime editor of the Scotch Collegian,
Melbourne, and soldier of the 21st Infantry
Battalion, lie Was on board the transport
Southland when she was torpedoed, and
wrote up the incident in graphic style. Vice-
captain of his school, he had rowed in the
college eight, and had gained notable honors
in English and literature. A son of 11. M.
Burns, a Lilydale minister, he maybe drew
his talenr for versifying from a Presbyterian
grandfather, author of some of the kirk’s
finest hymns. Why he should have gone to
meet his death in D company’s trenches is
best explained in the verses he left behind :
Sydney Sunday Times has changed
hands, after being the property of the
Evans family for over 30 years. A co., of
which Hugh D. Mclntosh is chairman, has
bought it and the associated papers, and
fresh men are to be set to work in place of
several of the old hands. A new
editor (Bert Toy, of the S. M.
Herald) and news-editor (Claude Cor-
bett, of Sydney Sun) and several good
men of the local dailies will see what can
be done to improve things. But the question
is whether the Times will be improved by
improvement. A large proportion of its
readers take the paper because it offers the
style of goods they can appreciate. Mcln-
tosh says the policy will not be altered, but
there’s a suspicion that politics will be a
larger feature than formerly. With that
in view r the new bosses had hopes that
Peters would cross over from the D.T. and
take charge ; but they couldn't coax him.
“Copy Boy”: The Herald and Weekly
Times office, Melbourne, has the best
army of newspaper veterans in Aus-
tralia. The collection includes 16
Herald employees with over 30 years’
service each. A man in the publish-
ing department attached himself to the office
40 years ago, and in the lino, room is one
who started type-snatching for the propri-
etary 39 years ago. There are four others
—all now on the linos. —with tallies of 36,
34, 31 and 30 years. On the Weekly
Times side are men with records of 39, 38
and 30 years ; and three —a comp., a reader
and a front-office man —who have hung on
for 35 years. Another who had been on the
job for 39 years retired a while back. Among
the new-chums are lads who have been in
the office for 28, 27. 25 and 20 years; and a
fellow has only just looked in if he hasn’t
been there 15 years.

HAWKINS. —17th May, 1916, suddenly, William
Hawkins, of 173 George Street, Redfern, and late
of Dural; aged 83 yards.

By the author of The Table Groaned :
That war on journalese, which used to delight
Hilaire Belloc before a more serious conflict occupied
his mind, still seems to be waged with success, if
the Age (16/5/’l6) is a reliable witness for the
prosecution. The death of that Crown Prince of
abbreviations, “etc.,” was announced by the Argus
some years ago, when it stated that “sheep,
cattle, etc., have been drowned.” Unfortunately the
rumor was exaggerated, and “etc.” is still among
the verbal battalions with which the slovenly reporter
storms the impregnable Verdun of literature. Now,
however, it would appear that one of the stodgiest
Romans of them all has been at the least interned,
as witness this heading from the Age of the date
above quoted : —•
THE LAW’S LONG ARM.
CAPTURED AFTER MONTHS OF LIBERTY.
This news has spread dismay in the ranks of the
tripe-dressers. “Old Sol” is trembling in the sky,
“the devouring element” has lost its appetite,- ‘ the
long arm of coincidence” is paralysed, “green old
age” is tottering, “quite a gloom is cast without
hope of illumination, and the Law’s elongated limb
leaves a large family of country correspondents to
mourn its loss. “The funeral cortege” and the
“numerous and costly floral tributes are obtainable
at the usual address.
Jack Flanagan, the young Australian
<renius in black-and-white, arrived in \an-
ccuver early in March and found one ot his
own miracles staring at him from the book-
stalls. His “Coming of Spring.” published
as a front-page cartoon in The Bulletin
on January 27, had been reproduced by an
admiring local print, and the bookstalls
hung it forth to catch the flying lankee
e y e - , * * .
“Black Rock”: Melbourne Argus will be
70 years old and a jolly good fossil on
June 1. William Kerr, its founder and first
editor, used to dip his pen in vitriol many
times daily, and pray to all his gods to
deliver the enemy into his hands. As a
result the law took a tight grip on the vig-
orous journalist, and the sheriff sold the
paper to satisfy a libel-action judgment.
Respectability descended upon the wreck
when Edward Wilson, then a grazier at
Dandenong, decided to turn his plough-
shares into pens, and bought the plant for
£3OO. His descendants have never been
sorry that he made this bargain. Lachlan
Mackinnon joined Wilson early in the
’fifties, and the firm has been Wilson and
Mackinnon ever since. The Argus is only
the third-oldest paper in Victoria, Mel-
bourne Herald and the Geelong Advertiser
having been hatched out six years earlier.
The bugles of England were blowing o’er the sea,
As they had called a thousand years, calling now to
me;
They woke me from dreaming in the dawning of the
day,
The bugles of England—and how could I-stay?
The banners of England, unfurled across the sea,
Floating out upon the wind, were beckoning to me;
Storm-rent and battle-torn, smoke-stained and grey,
The banners of England—and how could I stay?
O England, I heard the cry of those that died for
thee,
Sounding like an organ-voice across the winter sea ;
They lived and died for England, and gladly went
their way,
England, O England—how could / stay?

A Sleep Measure
Set Big Ben for
your rising hour and
forget him —he won’t
forget you.
Tick by tick he counts
off your sleep hours.
Steady, accurate, reli-
able, he gives you full
measure —not one minute
more.
When wake-time comes you’re
aroused by a lively alarm on a
mellow-toned gong that brushes
the sleep out of your eyes and
puts you in fighting trim to
tackle the work ahead of you.
Big Ben makes your last hour
of sleep as restful as your first —
as free from worry about
waking on time.
Big Ben is seven inches tall,
well built, handsome, punctual.
He has a big, easily read dial—
a cheerful, deep toned voice—
large, comfortable winding keys
a light running movement
with watch-like escapement—an
alarm that calls steadily for five
minutes or intermittently for
ten.
Big Ben undergoes a hundred inspec-
tions in the making and six rigid timing
tests before leaving the factory of the
Western Clock Co., La Salle, 111.,
U.S.A., Makers of Westclox. At all

“8.W.” : Allow me to rise above the
storm of curses and give a cheer for prickly-
pear. Use it as a whitewash. In South
America we slice the leaves of the blessing
and steep it in water for a day. Then we
add some lime to the creamy solution, and
have a wash which gives an endurable
glossy-white covering able to withstand any
climate except the one that Freetraders are
going to.
“Chink”: At a Brisbane suburban blow-
out the other night an amateur elocutionist
weighed in with “How We Beat the Favor-
ite.” He got his field away pretty right;
he even sprinted finely for the first few fur-
longs. Then he struck a hurdle that his
memory baulked at. Dead silence. En-
couraging applause. Silence some more.
Then young Australia shot in to the rescue.
“Well, any’ow,” chirruped the impatient
“sport”—“’oo won the race?”
McAndrew’s million miles at sea was a
short trip compared with the run of Cap-
tain H. E. Greenstreet, of the New Zealand
S.S. Co.’s Remuera. When he got home the
other day he was on his 90th trip to Maori-
land by the Cape of Good Hope and back
by the Horn or Magellan Straits. This
means that he has reeled off nearly 2,500,000
miles without counting in his other travels.
If he had made five trips to the moon he
wouldn’t have gone farther.
A Vic. forestry school is to be established
wherein the pupil will be taught how to
grow a tree, keep it in good health and value
it at various stages of its growth. After
one year’s tuition, and on passing an exami-
nation, the pupil will become a “forest
cadet” with a weekly wage. Later, when
he is wiser in timber lore and bush econo-
mics, he may become a chief forester with
a wage of £5. After that—-well, thus far
there is no thereafter in the Livingston
scheme. And a promise of only £250 per
annum for Victoria’s chief foresters looks
merely as though Livingston was trying to
get his scientists on the cheap.
“Thraso” wants Australia to grow its
own medicine :—■
The Medicinal Plants Board of Victoria is testing
nearly 40 vegetables in the field and in the laboratory
with a view to advising the public which to grow.
The question of the production of medicinal plants
and herbs has become an urgent one owing to the
war. We still rely on troubled Europe for our drugs
and medicines when nearly all of them can be raised
here. In fact, many medicinal local plants are
noxious weeds, like dandelion and stramonium.
Others would be dangerous to grow except under
proper supervision, such as opium poppy and henbane.
Some, like liquorice and peppermint, are easy, and
one ought to be careful to put in the right castor-
oil plant, as there are SO varieties. Camphor can
be produced from a native laurel. Climate, soil, lati-
tude and altitude of almost every degree exist in
Australia, and plant life thrives nearly everywhere.
There are over 80 varieties of eucalyptus oil, each
with distinct characteristics, and all saleable either
at home or abroad. This is another of the innumer-
able things we can do better here ourselves if we
can only drag ourselves out of bed.
As a school teacher, Jack Healy, late
secretary of Vic. Cricket Association, whom
a weak heart has just killed in his chair,
had his own methods. Yet the youngsters
who sat under him when he was master at
Yarra Park School remember his honest
nature no less than his heavy hand. His
pet aversion was the girl who allowed her
hair to fall about her face and screen her
whisperings. His plan was to march her
out in front of the class and tie up her
crowning glory with a piece of string,
apparently kept for the purpose. His prin-
cipal failing was a delight in atrocious
puns, but with this exception his sense of
fair play was notable, as befitted a cricketer
of his standing; and as “Markwell” of the
Australasian, his writings on football
were' a law unto those scholars whose
parents bought the paper.
Rumor is busy preparing a shift for Pre-
mier Scaddan from his Ivanhoe-Brownhill
pocket borough to a metropolitan constitu-
ency. Incidentally, it is assumed that the
election will tread on the heels of the ensu-
ing meeting of Parliament. John de-
clines to talk about it; but unless he has
wind of a stronger Jack in the field for the
Labor selection, or presses a Redistribution
Act, he is unlikely to forsake the district
which gave him his political baptism. The
Labor party lost every metropolitan seat but
one at the last general election, and its record
in the interim gives it small ground for hope
of recovery. With his deficit of a million
and a half Scaddan is not the Laughing Jack
of the early days of his Premiership; but
he is not so depressed that he yearns to
commit himself to the shadows.
Horace Stevens, who until a few months
ago was one of Melbourne’s best-known den-
tists, has laid down the forceps for good.
Chess men and musicians will miss him.
Singer Horace Stevens, now a lieutenant
in the A.1.F., was his son, and a son-in-law
is “Billy” Steele, the beauteous organist of
Scots’ Church. One of the late toothsmith’s
hobbies was pocket agriculture, especially
in connection with experimental wheat-grow-
ing. In the days when Jack Davidson, now
editor-in-chief of Melbourne Herald, was
editing the Weekly Times, which was
strong on the land and the harvest thereof,
Stevens- put before him some wonderful re-
sults in grain production. “Davy” was
greatly struck. “What area did you have
under cultivation?” he asked. “Ah,” said
Stevens, apologetically, “only a kerosene tin,
you know.” That was Davidson’s first in-
sight into the real meaning of “intense
cultivation.”
HIS LETTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He toiled, his small head bending o’er his pen,
Nor thought that grown-up folks did silent note
His secret heavy enterprise, and when
He something to his satisfaction wrote,
They watched him to the garden speed away,
And dig and dig for all his little worth
To hide his precious letter from the day,
lo post it safely in the soft brown earth.
The child then gone, they dug it up to scan
Its words in labored letters, thin and thick—
Dear Mr. Devil, come and take Aunt Ann ;
And please be quick!”
N.R.W.

The Big Ten-Ten,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HEAT of the summer had worked its
will;
The grass was tinder-dry ;
Mile after mile, on plain and hill,
The bush was withered and limp and still
Where winds once whispered by.
Glare as of furnaces seemed to fill
The eyes of a blinded sky.
Close to the track where the railway went.
The trees leaned in to feel
The sighing wind, like a breath nigh spent,
That passed, with clamor and discord blent
Of clattering car and wheel,
When quivering, burning metals bent
To the beat of a giant’s heel.
Low in the hills the night-mail paused
Where the block-bells clanged their code.
Leagues of a flame-lit plain she’d crossed,
Paths through the burning foothills forced
Where gathering flames had glowed.
Leaning out, like a knight well horsed,
Her driver spoke of the road.
“How are the fires at Snowbound Creek?
Ask him to flag me in.
The smoke’s been thick ; in the yellow reek
A semaphore’s but a murky streak.
It was hot work, once, between
Two walls of flame —aye, a narrow squeak,
But the Big Ten-Ten can wfln !”
Skirting the sidings dry and brown
By winding, rising ways,
The mail swung put of the foothills town
Where flames were raging and roaring down,
And treetops were ablaze;
She fought her way towards the mountain’s
crown,
Unseen in the smoky haze.
Down in the dried-up river beds
The dead scrub never stirred.
Loudly she blew at the winking “reds,”
While sleepers woke' in their rocking beds.
And the knight of the engine spurred
Till the punch of the steam on her piston-
heads
Were the only sounds he heard.
Snowbound Creek was a lurid place—
The red fires hemmed it round.
They flagged him through at a headlong
pace,
And saw the set of his flame-lit face
And the loom of her, vapor-crowned—
A monster, filled with the agile grace
And leap of a hunting hound.
Upwards, into a hell of flame,
The reeling engine strove ;
Here was the test of her mountain fame.
Speed on the grades, that the drivers claim
As test, all else above;
And the shudder of power in her mighty
frame
Was balm to the man who drove.
Fires of the forest were nearer now.
She cleared their clutch in time;
Straining yet to the mountain’s brow
She dared the threat of the blazing bough
With the lilt of a shouted rhyme.
Oh ! true were the tales men told of how
The Rig Ten-Ten could climb!
The song of her rocking funnel seemed
To shout to the heavens wide.
Rushing, rising, the Ten-Ten steamed
Nor slackened speed when a signal gleamed
Or a flange on the metals cried ;
Upwards, ever, and still there streamed
Red lights on the mountain-side.
Grey was the dawn when she topped the
crest
And the hissing brakes took hold ;
Dim stars shone in the shadowed west.
The east was warm as a maiden’s breast
’Neath royal cloth of gold :
But down where the mists of morning rest,
The smoke still rose and rolled.
Her counlings clattered, she grumbled, then
Moved off, to a muttered rhyme ;
They’d earned their rest, her engine-men,
Till stars rode over the hills again ;
But they smiled at the block-bell’s chime
Passing the word that the Big Ten-Ten
Had come through the fires, on time.
MAORILAND.
WILL LAWSON.
Bonzolino.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I STARTED up in bed.
“What the h-h-heaven is that?”
I waa curing myaelf of the swearing
habit, but I had been ao suddenly wrenched
out of slumber that the accustomed word
had very nearly slipped. Surely I hadn’t
dreamt such a flaring flourish on a big
brass instrument. No! —there it came
again; a rapid scale-passage, rushing, cres-
cendo, to a far-resounding summit; then
descending slowly over longer and longer
lapses to the very bottom of the phonetic
abyss.
This time the swear-word did come—
good and hard. It was my first night in
my new quarters; the time was half-past
twelve; yet here was some lunatic in the
next room diverting himself untimeously
with diabolical diapasons. With assault-
and-battery in my mind. I sprang out of
bed, but while I was getting into my trou-
sers I decided that expostulation should
come first. I went to the next door and
tapped thrice —unheard ; so I banged.
“Com’ een!” said a small, tired kind of
voice.
Stretched upon the bed was a coatless
little man. with beady black eyes and a
grizzling dark moustache. I looked hard at
him for a few moments; then my eye
travelled inquiringly round the room. The
occupier started up and stared angrily at
me. Then his face lighted, and he laughed
—the airy, spontaneous laugh of Southern
Europe.
“You look-a for da eenstruments?” he
said. “You no can tell-a vere I put zem,
eh ?”
“I certainly don’t see any; but you must
allow me to say that”
He bounded off the bed so suddenly that I
thought he was going to rush me, but he
merely grasped me impulsively by both
hands.
“ Perdonatemi , signor!- —a tousan’ par-
dons ! You sleep-a. een da nex’ r-r-room,
eh? Dat ees—you no sleep-a, because dat
r-rascal, Benedetto Bonzolino —dat ees me
—’e make-a da ongodlee dam row vis ’is
eenstruments.”
‘‘Ungodly damned row!” I repeated.
“That’s pretty idiomatic for an Italian, as
I presume you are, but”
“Say no more-a !” he cut in. “I make-a
you da apologee for deesturba you, but da
padrona —landleddee, you call —she no tella
me dat da nex’ appgrtamento vas occupy.
Taka da seat”—he darted to a cupboard in
the corner—-“an ve ’ave von glass of vine
togezzer, to show-a dere is no eel-veel.”
The queer little animal interested me. I
sat down and tasted his wine. “Sono stato
in Italia,'’'’ I remarked, casually. It was as
if I had touched a spring. Bonzolino liter-
ally jumped at me,' and, before I know what
was happening, I had been swiftly kissed
on both cheeks—infinitely to my British
discomfiture. Then off he went in his own
tongue, at a rate that left me hopelessly
in the rear, though I tried to catch-on with
an occasional “Si” or “ Certamente .” I
could make out, nevertheless, that his
Italian was high-class, and I began to scent
a mystery—perhaps a romance—about this
electric little performer upon invisible
musical instruments. lie grinned amiably
at me when he saw me looking round again
for them.
“Too late-a now,” he said ; “but I show-a
you dem domani— to-morrow, you call eet.
An’ den you no see dem not-at-all.”
This was cabalistic, but next day I under-
stood. I had brought Bonzolino into my
room, and was requiting his Marsala with
good old Scotch, when a bassoon began to
play, and I discovered, to my huge aston-
ishment, that my small friend was vocally
producing the sound. Next, he gave me a
bit of the bass-saxophone, and then of the
trombone; and so on, from instrument to
instrument —all imitated with such marvel-
lous fidelity that I simply sat and stared at
the performer.
“Why,” I said at last, “where, in the
name of all that’s miraculous, do you bring
that magnificent tone from? You don’t
speak in it.”
“No, I no spik een eem, an’ I no seeng
een eem. I shust play-a een eem.”
“Well, you are nothing short of a pheno-
menon. Why don’t you go on the variety-
stage? You’d simply coin money!”
If I had struck the little man in the face
be could not more suddenly have flashed
into anger. He grabbed himself violently
by the hair with both hands, and hopped
about the room like a demented sparrow
before he could find words.
“You dare-a ! You ’ave da eensolence to
—vat you call eet —suggaist—dat I go on
da stage-a? Vat you taka me for, eh?”
“Well, Mr. Bonzolino,” I began, “I don’t,
see anything very dreadful”
“Bah!” he broke in. “I not Bonzolino!
I no ’ave da nem of da dam beeliard-ball!”
“Why—you told me”
“Yais, I tol’ you Bonzolino. Good nuff
dat for nome-di-guerra; but, ven you talk-a
me about go on da varieetee-stage-a, I tella
you dat een my own countree I am nobile.
II Conte di Montegiallo, al vostro servizio .”
I was more amused than impressed, but
I sought to speak the little spitfire fair in
his own tongue. “ Con tutto rispetto,” I
began.
“ Basta!” he cut in. “I no spik no more-a
da Italiano viz you. You ’eve eensulta me,
an’ I Spik now onlee your own —vat you
call? —rottain Aingleesh —da lingua of da
porco —da peeg, you call!”
Tired of this teapot-tempest, I got up.
“Now, Mr. Bonzolino, or Montegiallo, or
whatever you are —out you go ! I haven’t
any use for high-explosives of your sort.”
lie made me a magnificent bow.
“I go,” he said, solemnly ; “but you ’ear-a
from me ver’ soon!”
“Ver’ soon” was next day, when I met
Collachini in George-street. He was an
Austro-Italian. whose modifications of Eng-
lish are too slight to be worth reproducing.
He smiled significantly as we shook hands.
“You know Bonzolino?” he said.
“Dropped across him the night before
last. Bit of a crank, isn’t he?”
“More of a rascal, I should say. Well-
educated man, though, and calls himself
sometimes Count of Montegiallo. At all
events, he says you insulted him grossly,
and he demands the satisfaction of a gentle-
man, as he-calls it. Of course the thing is
ridiculous, but I promised him I’d give you
the message. By-the-way, he didn’t tell me
what the insult was.”
Collachini had an enormous laugh, and,
when I had explained, his mighty guffaw
made the passers-by turn to look at us.
“Well,” he said, “I’ve given you the mes-
sage, anyhow. What shall I tell him you
said to it?”
“Tell him I said ‘Rubbish !’ ”
“Just that?”
“Just that.”
Collachini grinned and departed, and I
shoved the whole silly matter out of my mind.
But that evening, as I was hanging up my
hat in the hall, I became aware of half a
dozen fellow-residents grouped about the
foot of the staircase. Amongst them was
Bonzolino, and in the eyes of the others
was expectancy.
“Codardo!” the little man shot at me, as
I approached the stairs.
“Poltrone !” he added, as I made to ascend.
Ironically I thanked him. “Vi ringrazio
molto!” I said, and would have passed on,
but Bonzolino the Brave blocked my way,
and the dignity of him would have been
magnificent if it hadn’t been so ludicrous.
“I senda you a reques’ for la sodisfasione
—da satisfac’ of da gentlemans; an’ you
reply onlee ‘Rubbaish !’ Ees dat so?”
“Precisely. And that is what I say to you
now. Rubbish ! —get out of my way !”
“Den I ’ave da onore to pulla you da
nose !”
He thrust out his arm quickly, and—
w r hat would have happened if he had really
