The skin is one of the most sensitive parts
of the human body; and because it is also the
most exposed to injury and disease, it ought
to get more attention than most people thinir
is necessary. -
A sudden chill, dirt in a cold-crack or small
abrasion, and skin-weaknesses are all likelv
to lead to trouble. Neglected chafing
chaps, chilblains, cold-sores, or
cuts may also cause more serious
disease in the shape of festering
wounds, ulcers or itching sores, and
even chronic eczema is often trace-
able to neglect in the early stages of
a simple affection.
Zam-Buk is the ideal application
for the skin at this season^
No ordinary ointments or salves,

mineral irritants, can do for the

never give lasting satisfaction— the

rubbing in Zam-Buk.

pulled my nose I can’t say; but he merely
touched the end of the feature with a swift
forefinger—then stood aside and made me
an elaborate bow. “Kindlee conseeder your
nose pulla !” he said.
I laughed and passed on.
* * * *
About a fortnight later I strolled into one
of the variety theatres, and was looking care-
lessly down the programme when I struck: —
“The Celebrated Herr Gelbberg, in his
Astonishing Vocal Imitations of Musical In-
struments. An Absolute Marvel. Must be
Heard to be Credited,” and so on.
“Gelbberg?” I said to myself. “Oelb berg;
yellow mountain! Why, it’s a monte-giallo
—Montegiallo. Bonzolino, for a thousand !”
It was; and, when his turn was over, I
went round to the stage-door and waited.
Presently he came out —looked at me—made
to pass on—then turned and faced me.
“Veil?” he said.
“Well, Mr. Bonzolino-Gelbberg-Montegi-
allo; you appear to be doing pretty decently
out of the advice you wanted to assassinate
me for. Fifty pounds a week, eh? That’s
about the figure, I suppose?”
“Dat avviso you givva me ! Ila ! I onner-
stan’. You vanta me pave you for dat
avviso? Ah, dat ees da Aingleeshman
alvays. Da nazione of sliopkeepair, as da
gret Napoleone say! Veil, Meester Shop-
keepair—’owmosh you vant?”
With intentional insult he displayed upon
his palm a couple of half-crowns.
“Seems to me,” I went on unheeding, “that
the commercial instinct is pretty well devel-
oped even in the blue —or is it the yellow—
blood of the Haughty House of Montegiallo.”
At this not very brilliant play upon his
alleged name the little man simply blew-up ;
there is no other word for it. He glared—he
skipped—he spluttered. “I pulla your dam’
noce vonce more-a!” he hissed, as he rushed
at me with outstretched arm; but, expecting
no more than another figurative or hypo-
thetical assault, I didn’t lift a finger until I
found that my nose was being violently
tweaked in good earnest. Then I sent up a
half-arm “chip” to the jaw, and Mr. Bonzo-
lino sat down with great celerity.
* * * *
“For the defence” —said next evening’s
paper—“ Charles Cockerell, stage-doorkeeper
at the "Whirligig Theatre, deposed that he
saw the whole affair. The tw' men had been
talking quietly for a couple of minutes, when
Gelbberg became suddenly excited and
grabbed the nose of the defendant, who then
propped him and knocked him down.
“The Magistrate: ‘Propped him and
knocked him down’? Why, I always thought
propping anything kept it up! (Laughter.)
I dismiss this charge. The solicitor for the
complainant has laid great stress on the fact
that his client is the smaller man and
doesn’t know how to use his hands. Well,
that is all the more reason why he shouldn’t
go pulling the noses of other people who may
be better equipped for hostilities. If he had
merely struck the defendant, I might have
taken a different view ; but, to pull a nose —a
Britisher’s nose, at all events —is as deadly
an insult as to spit upon the beard of a
Mohammedan. Next case!”
* * * *
Here, for the first time, are the facts about
what has been so often charged against me
as the “Bumping of Bonzolino.” I trust I
shall hear no more of that extremely tired
misconstruction.
ALEX. E. MONTGOMERY.
Finn’s Head.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
nyy ORAII was the fine girl, tall and
straight, with hair to her blacker
than Quinlan’s pig—the same that
slid greased through the flues of Hell for a
wager—and the bluest blue eye out of Ire-
land, saving none. Norah Iveely took ser-
vice to Finn’s trifling hotel at Bogtrot, Bun-
garee East, from her mother’s arms, with
the school ways to her still, and the face
that dimpled with innocence. You’d think,
maybe, she was nothing but a cherub at all
that had come by the figure of Venus in the
way of an accident. Norah had nothing to
do anyone would mind the least, but wipe
up the few dishes, and scrub the seven
floors, and dust, and clean, and cook, and
milk, and make the beds, after chopping a
trifle of hard wood, or digging over the small
garden couldn’t turn a ten-horse team and a
waggon in. ’Twas Mrs. Finn attended the
bar, and himself did all the A T ork.
Phil Finn was the cocky bit of a man,
that impudent you’d think him six foot from
his boots, and thirteen stone after the long
fast, and him no taller than a small man,
and little heftier than a sound turkey bird
well plucked and stuffed for the Christmas
eating. Phil had a good idea of himself as
the bad boy of the family, and his airs with
women would tire the patient Job. Natur-
ally so a silly man got frisking with Norah
in the kitchen while Mrs. Finn was diverted,
tending the bar, giving drinks to hot,
droughty travellers and the like of that.
And Phil would throw an arm round Norah,
and tell her a tale of her blue eyes and his
heart breaking in two within him. Nothing
would Norah say in answer, but take up a
neat china plate and snap on his head. And
Mrs. Mary Finn, coming in, would look at
the broken crockery strewn on the floor, and
say ’twas a mortal pity, since the plate was
a great piece of fine metal, belonged to her
mother, and prized more than is ordinary.
And Norah would blush, and say nothing.
Finn, with a burning red patch to bis bald
bead, would be fussing most righteous in the
yard all this time.
But Finn was a monotonous man of sad
persistence, and his sore head would no
sooner be gone that back would he be at
Norah, making foolishness, and without any
ado but in defence of her self-respect, and
for the honor of the house, “Bang!” Norah
would crack the soup-tureen across the keel
of his skull. ’Twas only natural, hearing
the crash, Mrs. Mary Finn would come
again, and look sad towards Norah, clicking
her tongue the time, and saying that tureen
was her old grandmother’s 81 years since,
none finer out of China. Norah bore it all,
with a tear perhaps, and a sigh maybe, but
was not one to make trouble ’tween man
and wife. So the destruction went on. Was
never a day when Phil Finn did not have
a plate or two, or a big dish as like as not,
cracked over the head of him, and some times
lie bled a bit, and other times be took a
black bruise till his head looked like one
six Tipperary boys would be practising on,
and there was not a plate left to the hotel
you could set down to a decent customer.
Then when it came to correcting Phil
with the opal shade from the bar lamp she
was cleaning, Norah got more of the length
of Mrs. Mary’s tongue, Mrs. Finn being a
patient and quiet-spoken lady by nature and
custom, but one that knew her rights. “Have
yeh no sense to yeli at all, Norah Keely, and
you the daughter of a curious sane man?”
said she. “Nothin’ will be left me with yer
foolishness and your smashin’s and breakin’s,
’n’ is a body t’ feed her gentle guests, well
brought up ’n’ educated ivery one, wid
nothin’ but pig-tins ’n’ irun dishes no better?
’Tis come t’ this, though I like you well, ’n’
have a mind that you’re a honest, clean
girl, you’ll not stay here if me good new blue
dinner-set comes tumblin’ about me ears the
same way.” Norah gave no answer, but her
face was a flaming sign, and when she
blinked the falling tears would fill a cup.
Only next morning, at eleven about, Mrs.
Finn heard a great crash in the kitchen.
One hand to her heart for the fate of her
expensive new dishes of clear white China
you could see through, she ran for the door,
and there lay Phil Finn on the deal boards,
with a swelling lump to his head like the
big handle of a wide hall door. Over against
the hurt man stood Norah Keely, weeping
with her whole heart and soul, and in
Norah’s right hand, clutched for a weapon,
was a large, sound iron frying-pan. ’Twas
Norah Mrs. Finn went to, ’twas Norah she
soothed and condoled. “There, there, darlin’
sweet,” says she, “don’t you take on, will
you. Nothin’s broke that matters a penny.
Glad I am you’ve come to your siven sinses
at last. You stay where you are, me girl,
wid a two-shillin’ rise, ’n’ what crock’ry
you break you pay for, but all the irun
pans that’s cracked up you can charge
to the house.” Then she went off without a
glance to Phil sitting up, smoothing out his
grand, tender swelling.
Norah is with the -Finns still. ’Tis near
two year since Phil had any manner of hurt
to his head at all.
EDDYSON.
On the Old Highway.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
O BAKER, baker, give me a loaf,
For I met to-day
A red-cheeked wench and a lazy oaf—
Folk of a kind on the Old Highway.
“There’s gold in the town and work to do,”
1 sang as I sped;
But the sun was bright and the sky was blue,
And the air as sweet as your new-made
bread.
I sang within me: “I’ll do my share,
As to do is right,
In the round of toil”; but the gold, tossed
hair
And the eyes of the wench undid me quite.
From the crown of my head to the tips of
my toes
I tingled to see
That wench as red as a roadside rose,
And that oat as gay as an oaf might be.
Ah, the oaf at her side, were his thoughts
all vain,
And his hours all waste,
With never an ache in his heart or a pain,
And the mouth of his red-lipped wench
to taste?
With hands a-swing and a heedless glance
The twain went by ;
For the world was theirs, and the world’s
romance,
And as thistle-down on the wind was I.
’Tis pleasant to think of the glowing smiles
Each gave to each —
The long, long road with its meal-dust miles,
And the red, red roses within their reach.
My heart cried “Stay!” and a couch I
sought
Where the sun shone bright;
And I threw me down for a moment’s
thought
On vonder hill in the morning’s light.
I said, “His face is a face I know —
I knew in youth !
Her eyes,” I said, “where the wonders
glow—■
Her rose-sweet mouth.... it is she in
truth!”
The shadows shortened to meet the noon
From rock and tree;
And I lay full-stretched, like a man in a
swoon,
And a bird was singing of Arcady.
I mused to music—and what do you think
I thought of there?
Not bread nor honey, nor wine to drink,
But Youth the Reckless and Pleasure the
Fair.
It was they I met on the road, good man,
As to toil I strode ;
And ever and ever, since Time began
Till time grows old, they shall walk that
road.
I watched my Youth and my Youth’s Desire
Till the trapee-hour sped,
And my joy died down like a dying fire,
And I roused me up, and I yawned and
said:
“This air is sweeter than wine to breathe ;
Yon skies are blue ;
But there’s new-made bread in the town
beneath,
And money to earn and work to do.”
So baker, baker, give me a loaf
New-baked, I pray ;
Too long I’ve tarried, a wayside oaf.
With a red-cheeked wench on the Old
Highway!
N.S.W.
RODERIC QINN.
